{2}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-2-1"></span><span id="page-2-0"></span>**1 Introduction**

Threshold encryption [\[29,](#page-65-0) [30\]](#page-65-1) enables secure encryption among a set of *n* parties by generating a succinct ciphertext decryptable by a subset of at least *t* ≤ *n* parties, while ciphertexts should remain private against malicious coalitions of up to *t* − 1 parties. Motivated by applications such as encrypted mempools [\[2,](#page-63-0) [33\]](#page-65-2), recent works have explored variants including silent threshold encryption [\[36,](#page-65-3) [50,](#page-66-0) [42\]](#page-65-4), which removes interactive DKG, batched threshold encryption [\[27,](#page-64-0) [20,](#page-64-1) [18,](#page-64-2) [1,](#page-63-1) [9\]](#page-63-2), which reduces decryption communication, and context-dependent threshold encryption [\[7\]](#page-63-3), which tailors decryption to specific contexts. However, all these constructions remain vulnerable to collusion attacks, in which committee members cooperate to help the adversary decrypt ciphertexts.

Motivated by this limitation, recently Boneh, Partap, and Rotem [\[12\]](#page-63-4) introduced *threshold traitor tracing*, extending classical threshold encryption by equipping it with a *traceability* requirement. Informally, collusion is modeled as follows: A coalition of at least *t* parties may embed their secret keys into a *decoder* that decrypts ciphertexts. Given only black-box access to such a decoder, a tracing algorithm is required to identify at least one member of the coalition. A series of subsequent works advance threshold traitor tracing under various tradeoffs, including constructions without a trusted dealer or interaction [\[21,](#page-64-3) [24\]](#page-64-4), CCA-secure schemes [\[3\]](#page-63-5), public traceability [\[23\]](#page-64-5), and schemes with polylogarithmic parameters [\[28\]](#page-65-5).

The existing definition of threshold traitor tracing [\[12\]](#page-63-4) models collusion via a *standalone* decoder, namely one that decrypts correctly with noticeable probability *ε*. Such a decoder can then be used by anyone to decrypt arbitrary ciphertexts. This captures the setting in which a coalition sells a decoder to an external buyer, who then submits it to a tracer. That is, the buyer may act as a whistleblower (see, e.g., [\[44\]](#page-66-1)). Collusion deterrence arises from the risk that any buyer may blow the whistle by forwarding the decoder to the tracer, who can then trace at least one colluder and punish them, thus disincentivizing collusion.

However, we identify a fundamental limitation of this model when the buyer is an *insider*, i.e., a party that can obtain additional non-trivial information useful for decryption, such as valid secret key shares or partial decryptions. In the simplest case, an insider can be thought of as a committee member. Now, a set of colluders of arbitrary size can construct a decoder *D* that decrypts correctly only when provided with, say, the insider's secret key share sk*<sup>i</sup>* . While such a decoder can be used by the insider to decrypt, tracing it necessarily requires inputting this insider information into the decoder during tracing (otherwise, the decoder could simply refuse to decrypt). Thus, the colluders may be able to design the decoder so that tracing it *always outputs the insider*. In fact, we show later (Section [2.1\)](#page-3-1) that this attack is inherent to a widely used design pattern underlying many existing traceable threshold encryption schemes [\[12,](#page-63-4) [3,](#page-63-5) [24,](#page-64-4) [23\]](#page-64-5), rendering them vulnerable to such attacks. Consequently, colluders face no tracing risk, as insiders have no incentive to submit such a decoder for tracing, when tracing will only find insiders.

Insider attacks fundamentally separate traceability in the threshold setting from traceability in the non-threshold (broadcast) setting. In non-threshold traitor tracing [\[26\]](#page-64-6), insider attacks are irrelevant: any "insider" already possesses full decryption capabilities and therefore has no incentive to purchase a decoder. Consequently, the buyer of a decoder is always external. In contrast, under threshold encryption, committee members are not automatically authorized to decrypt arbitrary ciphertexts; as a result, they may maliciously obtain decryption capability by purchasing a decoder from colluders, underpinning the need to consider insider attacks in threshold traitor tracing.

This observation motivates us to broaden the study of threshold traitor tracing in the presence of insiders. In particular, we consider two types of insiders: (1) *full insiders*, who input their full secret key share into the decoder, and (2) *partial insiders*, who provide only their partial decryptions for ciphertexts.

While we consider (and later prove) partial insiders to be a *relaxation* of full insiders, we argue that the notion of partial insiders captures interesting realistic settings by itself: First, a buyer may not want to insert their full secret key into a decoder, as it is essentially untrusted, potentially malicious software. Further, this models a setting where *some* partial decryptions for ciphertexts that are supposed to remain private are exposed during normal protocol execution [\[8,](#page-63-6) [34\]](#page-65-6), e.g., as in encrypted mempools.

#### **Contributions and Results.** We now describe our contributions.

• We show that existing definitions of threshold traitor tracing are insufficient, as they admit constructions that are vulnerable to insider attacks, revealing a conceptual gap in current models.

{3}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-3-4"></span><span id="page-3-3"></span>

| Instantiation  | # Traitors Found | Full-Insider<br>Resilience | Partial-Insider<br>Resilience | Robust.      | Ctxt size     | Notes                                                      |
|----------------|------------------|----------------------------|-------------------------------|--------------|---------------|------------------------------------------------------------|
| Naive          | n/2              | 1-to-1                     | 1-to-1                        | n/2          | O(n)          | Ciphertext Size<br>Prohibitive                             |
| Corollary 2.10 | $\tau - x$       | 1-to-1                     | $(x+1) \cdot n/2\tau$         | n/3          | $O(\tau^2)$   | For any <b>constant</b> $\tau$ and all $x$ s.t. $x < \tau$ |
| Corollary 2.11 | $\Theta(n^a)$    | 1-to-1                     | n/3                           | n/3          | $O(n^a)$      | For any $a \in (0,1)$                                      |
| Theorem 2.7    | 5n/22            | 1-to-1                     | 1-to-1                        | $\sqrt{n}/2$ | $O(\sqrt{n})$ | _                                                          |

Figure 1: Representative instantiations of our compilers for t = n/2. The 1-to-1 indicates that full/partial insider resilience is guaranteed up to a 1-to-1 tradeoff from traitors found as per Corollary 5.1. Robustness refers to the maximum number of maliciously chosen crash failures the scheme can tolerate and still decrypt correctly.

To address this gap, we present a generalized definition of traceability against insider attacks (Section 4).

- We establish an approach for achieving traceability against insiders through multi-traitor tracing, i.e., guaranteeing that the tracing algorithm identifies not just one, but at least  $\tau \leq t$  traitors (which we refer to as  $\tau$ -traceability). In particular, we show that  $\tau$ -traceability implies resilience against  $\tau 1$  full insiders (Section 5), which in turn implies resilience against  $\tau 1$  partial insiders (Corollary 5.1). Henceforth, we refer to this as a 1-to-1 tradeoff.
- We present three compilers for multi-traitor tracing (and thus insider resilience). To the best of our knowledge, we are the first to introduce constructions that achieve multi-traitor tracing with succinct ciphertexts, which we believe to be of independent interest.
  - The first compiler amplifies the number of traitors identified by a given scheme by a multiplicative constant factor K (Theorems 2.2 and 2.5).
  - The second compiler reduces the ciphertext size of any n/2-traceable scheme by decreasing the number of traitors found to cn/2 for a constant 0 < c < 1 (Theorem 2.7). The resulting scheme, however, is only robust against  $O(\sqrt{n})$  maliciously chosen crash failures.
  - The third compiler amplifies the number of traitors identified by any given scheme to be  $O(n^a)$  for any 0 < a < 1. The resulting scheme incurs a ciphertext size blow-up of  $O(n^a)$  (Theorem 2.8).
- We extend a construction of [12] to achieve resilience against up to t-1 (i.e, maximal) partial insiders under a relaxed decoder model, while maintaining O(1)-size ciphertexts (Theorem 2.9). This result is notable since instantiating our compilers with a scheme that already provides partial-insider resilience yields a better tradeoff between the number of traitors identified and partial-insider resilience, surpassing the 1-to-1 tradeoff guaranteed by Corollary 5.1.
- Finally, we instantiate our compilers to obtain constructions that are multi-traitor and resilient against insiders. The results are summarized in Fig. 1.

## <span id="page-3-0"></span>2 Technical Overview

We next give a high-level exposition of our techniques. For simplicity, we will describe all constructions in this paper as key-encapsulation mechanisms (KEM). Extending KEM to encryption follows using standard techniques.

#### <span id="page-3-1"></span>2.1 The Starting Point of This Paper

Our starting observation is that, under the definition of [12], traceable threshold KEM (TT-KEM) can already be obtained generically from any non-threshold traitor tracing scheme (in which every

<span id="page-3-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>1</sup>There exists a naive construction with ciphertext size linear in n that is t-traceable.

{4}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-4-1"></span>individual party with a secret key can decrypt on its own), which shows that the current definition admits constructions whose traceability relies entirely on a non-threshold component. We will describe later why this is problematic for insider resilience.

Thresholdizing Traitor Tracing Generically. Let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}$  be any (non-threshold) traitor tracing scheme, and  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TE}}$  any (non-traceable) threshold KEM. We construct a traceable threshold KEM  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TTE}}$  by running key generation in both  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}$  and  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TE}}$  (the latter with threshold t), and assigning each party secret keys for both schemes  $\mathsf{sk}_i = (\mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TT}}, \mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}})$ . To encrypt, sample independently

$$(k^{\mathsf{TT}}, c^{\mathsf{TT}}) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TT}}) \text{ and } (k^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{TE}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}}),$$

set the KEM key  $k \leftarrow k^{\mathsf{TT}} \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}$ , and assemble the ciphertext as  $c \leftarrow (c^{\mathsf{TT}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}})$ . Any t parties can recover  $k^{\mathsf{TE}}$  from  $c^{\mathsf{TE}}$ , while any single party can decrypt  $c^{\mathsf{TT}}$  to obtain  $k^{\mathsf{TT}}$ , hence reconstructing k.

Traceability follows via a wrapping argument. Given a decoder D that distinguishes valid encapsulations of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TTE}}$  with advantage  $\varepsilon$ , the tracer (holding the tracing key tk of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}$ ) builds a decoder D' for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}$  as follows: On input  $(c^{\mathsf{TT}}, k^{\mathsf{TT}})$ , D' samples random  $(k^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{TE}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}})$ , sets  $k \leftarrow k^{\mathsf{TT}} \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}$ , and  $c \leftarrow (c^{\mathsf{TT}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}})$ , queries D on (c, k), and outputs the same bit as D. Note that if D is a good decoder, i.e., it can distinguish with advantage  $\varepsilon$  whether k is encapsulated in c, then so is D' for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}$ . The tracer can then trace D' using the tracing algorithm of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}$ . We formalize the following informal theorem in Section 11 (Corollary 11.2).

Theorem 2.1 (Generic Threshold Traitor Tracing (Informal)). If there exists a non-threshold traitor-tracing scheme  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}$  and a (non-traceable) threshold KEM  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TE}}$ , then there exists a traceable threshold KEM  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TTE}}$  whose public key, secret key, and ciphertext sizes are the sums of the corresponding sizes of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TT}}$  and  $\Pi_{\mathsf{TE}}$ .

Due to the broad literature on non-threshold traitor tracing, the above generic compiler recovers several recent results in traceable threshold encryption [12, 3, 24, 28], all of which effectively thresholdize a non-threshold traitor tracing scheme. However, the generic compiler exposes a critical weakness with respect to insider attacks. The traceable component  $c^{TT}$  can be decrypted by a single party, independently of the threshold component. We refer to this pattern as an unlocking mechanism: a single party can unlock the traceable part of the ciphertext, bypassing enforcement of the threshold requirement.

Colluders can exploit this mechanism by constructing a decoder that functions only when provided with the secret key or partial decryption of a designated insider. This input suffices to decrypt  $c^{\mathsf{TT}}$ , while the colluders' keys decrypt  $c^{\mathsf{TE}}$ . Tracing such a decoder inevitably accuses the insider, never the actual colluders, eliminating any incentive for the insider to cooperate with the tracer.

More generally, any construction exhibiting the unlocking mechanism discussed above cannot be secure against full or even partial insiders. Indeed, several existing schemes suffer from this limitation, including [24], which is essentially an instance of the above compiler, and the first construction in [12]. In contrast, constructions such as [21, 28] as well as one of the constructions in [12] avoid unlocking. We conjecture that these constructions can be made secure against partial insiders. We confirm this for the latter construction, proving partial-insider resilience under a relaxed decoder model. Nevertheless, achieving full-insider resilience directly (without going via multi-traitor tracing) seems out of reach for even these constructions, which we discuss in Appendix F.

Motivated by this attack and its relevance to essentially all existing threshold traitor tracing schemes, we next formalize traceability against insiders and introduce a novel approach to achieve both full and partial insider resilience via *multi-traitor tracing*.

#### <span id="page-4-0"></span>2.2 Defining Traceability Against Insiders

A traceable threshold KEM is essentially a standard threshold KEM with two modifications. (1) key generation outputs an additional secret tracing key tk, and (2) a  $Trace^{D}$  algorithm that, given the tracing key tk and black-box access to a decoder D with noticeable decryption advantage, outputs a set of traitors.

To capture insider attacks, we introduce a strengthened notion of traceability against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders. In our definition, the adversary may corrupt an arbitrary number of parties as traitors, thereby learning their secret keys, and then outputs a decoder D, together with a set of full insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{sk}$  and partial insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ , with  $|\mathcal{I}_{sk}| \leq \sigma$  and  $|\mathcal{I}_{pd}| \leq \rho$ . The decoder D is considered good if, given (k, c), the full insiders' secret

{5}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-5-3"></span>keys  $\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}}$ , and the partial insiders' partial decryptions  $\{\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i,c)\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}$ , it distinguishes whether k is encapsulated in c with non-negligible advantage  $\varepsilon$ ; without this insider information, D may refuse to decrypt.

A scheme is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders<sup>2</sup> if, for every adversary producing  $(D, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}})$ , it holds that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}] \ge \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \text{ and } \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}] \le \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where the events  $\mathsf{GoodTr}$ ,  $\mathsf{GoodDec}$ , and  $\mathsf{BadTr}$  are defined as follows with respect to running the tracing algorithm on D.

- GoodTr occurs, whenever at least  $\tau$  correct traitors are found during tracing.
- GoodDec occurs whenever the adversary produces  $(D, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}})$  such that D is good for insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}$  and  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}$ . More formally, if given  $\left(k, c, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}}, \{\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i, c)\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\right)$ , D can distinguish with noticeable advantage whether k is random, or encapsulated in the ciphertext c.
- BadTr occurs whenever the tracing algorithm *frames* an honest party when tracing *D*. Importantly, insiders count as honest parties, unless they were specifically corrupted by the adversary as traitors before.

We say that a TT-KEM is maximally resilient against full (resp., partial) insiders, if it is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $t - \tau$  full (resp., partial) insiders for any  $0 < t \le n$ . Our definition naturally generalizes the original definition due to [12]: their definition is equivalent to 1-traceability against (0,0)-insiders.

Insiders' Secret Keys are Required for Tracing. We grant the tracer access to insiders' secret keys during tracing, as without this access, the tracer may be unable to even run the decoder. Indeed, in Section 4.1 we show an impossibility result that no TT-KEM can be both semantically secure and 1-traceable against even a single full insider without access to that insider's secret key.

**Decoder Model.** Throughout this work, we consider distinguishing decoders, which, given a ciphertext c and a candidate KEM key k, output a bit indicating whether k is encapsulated in c. This is a stronger model than decrypting decoders, which output k when given c. One of our results (Theorem 2.9), however, holds only for decrypting decoders.

Our formal definition can be found in Section 4. We next outline how to realize constructions under our new definition.

#### <span id="page-5-0"></span>2.3 Insider Resilience from Multi-Traitor Tracing

Our main approach derives insider resilience from multi-traitor tracing. In Section 5, we show that any  $\tau$ -traceable scheme is resilient against up to  $\tau$ -1 full insiders at the cost of reducing the number of traitors found by the same amount (1-to-1 tradeoff). We show in a similar manner how full insider resilience implies partial insider resilience (Corollary 5.1). Having reduced the problem of insider resilience to that of finding multiple traitors, we next introduce compilers that transform a traceable threshold KEM into one that identifies more traitors. Our compilers achieve different tradeoffs with respect to number of traitors found, robustness, and ciphertext size. We denote by P(n), S(n), and C(n) the public key, secret share, and ciphertext size of a construction as a function of the number of parties.

**Ramp Setting.** We note that our compilers yield ramp schemes [5], requiring a gap between the security threshold t and the reconstruction threshold r > t. For simplicity, we carry out our analysis for reconstruction threshold r = n/2. In Section 11, we show how to eliminate this gap, i.e., achieving t = r, while preserving traceability. This is achieved by encrypting in a 2-out-of-2 fashion to the traceable ramp scheme and any (non-traceable) threshold KEM without ramp. This compiler is of independent interest; for example, applying it to the recent construction of [28] yields a post-quantum traceable threshold encryption scheme without ramp.

<span id="page-5-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>2</sup>We restrict  $\tau + \sigma + \rho \le t$ . If t parties can decrypt, and a decoder receives insider information from  $\sigma + \rho$  parties, we can expect to find at most  $\tau \le t - \sigma - \rho$  traitors.

<span id="page-5-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>3</sup>Intuitively, the tracer can feed the insiders' secret keys into the decoder, and if  $\tau$  traitors are found, only  $\tau - 1$  of them can be insiders.

{6}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-6-0"></span>2.4 Compiler from $\tau$ -Traceablity to $(K \cdot \tau)$ -Traceablity

We present compilers that transform any  $\tau$ -traceable scheme (including  $\tau = 1$ ) into a  $(K \cdot \tau)$ -traceable scheme for an arbitrary constant K, at the cost of a K-fold increase in ciphertext size.

For simplicity, let the reconstruction threshold t = n/2. We partition parties into K disjoint bins  $B_1, \ldots, B_K$  of size  $n_{\mathsf{bin}} = n/K$ . Henceforth, such a partition is called a (K, n)-balanced partition. For each bin  $B_i$ , we instantiate an independent instance of a  $\tau$ -traceable threshold KEM  $\Pi$  with threshold  $t_{\mathsf{bin}} = n_{\mathsf{bin}}/2$ . To encrypt, one iterates over all bins  $i \in [K]$  and generates  $(k_i, c_i)$  using the instance of bin i, computes the KEM key as  $k \leftarrow \bigoplus_{i \in [K]} k_i$ , and assembles the ciphertext as  $c \leftarrow \{c_i\}_{i \in [K]}$ . Decryption succeeds iff at least  $t_{\mathsf{bin}}$  parties from every bin participate.

The intuition is that, as decryption requires decrypting all components  $c_i$ , any successful decoder D must succeed in every bin, and can thus be transformed into a decoder  $D_i$  for every bin  $i \in [K]$ . Tracing each  $D_i$  identifies  $\tau$  traitors per bin, and disjointness of the bins implies  $K \cdot \tau$  distinct traitors.

Unfortunately, this construction has two limitations: (1) there exist many authorized sets S with  $|S| \ge t = n/2$  that do not meet the threshold  $t_{\sf bin}$  in every bin, and thus would be unable to decrypt.<sup>4</sup> And (2), crashing  $n_{\sf bin} - t_{\sf bin} \approx n/2K$  parties in any bin prevents decryption, thus breaking robustness.

To address (1), we relax correctness to probabilistic correctness, requiring that a uniformly random authorized set decrypts with overwhelming probability. Since a random set of size n/2 is unlikely to be perfectly balanced, we lower the per-bin threshold to  $t_{\text{bin}} = \frac{n}{2K} - O(\sqrt{n/K})$ . By a Chernoff bound, such a set intersects every bin in at least  $t_{\text{bin}}$  parties with overwhelming probability. This yields a ramp threshold scheme: For reconstruction threshold r = n/2, we obtain a security threshold  $t = n/2 - O(\sqrt{Kn})$ . To deal with this, as discussed in Section 2.3, we apply our gap elimination compiler introduced in Section 11. Issue (2) can be mitigated using  $multi-instance\ expansion$ , discussed later in this section. The above compiler, henceforth called  $\Pi_{1-part}$ , is formalized in the following theorem.

<span id="page-6-1"></span>Theorem 2.2 (1-Partition Compiler). Let  $\Pi$  be a  $\tau$ -traceable TT-KEM. Then, there exists a  $(K \cdot \tau)$ -traceable ramp TT-KEM of sizes  $(P'(n), S'(n), C'(n)) = \left(KP_{\Pi}(\frac{n}{K}), S_{\Pi}(\frac{n}{K}), KC_{\Pi}(\frac{n}{K})\right)$ , gap  $g = O(\sqrt{Kn})$ , and  $(\frac{n}{2K} + g/K)$ -robustness.

To trace D in each bin, we next show how to transform D into decoders  $D_i$  for  $\Pi$  ciphertexts for every bin  $i \in [K]$ . The tracer constructs a decoder  $D_i$  for all bins  $i \in [K]$  as follows: On input  $(k_i, c_i)$ , the decoder  $D_i$  generates encryptions in the remaining bins as  $(k_j, c_j) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_j)$  for all  $j \neq i \in [K]$ . Then, it computes  $k \leftarrow \bigoplus_{j \in [K]} k_j$ , queries  $D(k, c := \{c_j\}_{j \in [K]})$ , and forwards D's output. It is easy to see that every decoder  $D_i$  has the same advantage against  $\Pi$  as the decoder D has against  $\Pi_{1\text{-part}}$ : If  $k_i$  is encapsulated in  $c_i$ , then so is k in ciphertext c. If  $k_i$  is uniform random and independent of  $c_i$ , then so is k by the perfect security of the one-time pad. Since  $D_i$  forwards D's output, both decoders have identical distinguishing advantage. Formally proving the above is technically involved, and we summarize the main challenges below. For a formal construction and proof, we refer to Section 6.

**Proof Idea of Theorem 2.2.** On a high level, we aim to show that if D is good, then tracing succeeds in all bins, and thus  $K \cdot \tau$  traitors are found. A standard technique for proving this is a hybrid argument. We define hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_0$  to be identical to the traceability game, and in  $\mathcal{H}_j$ , we modify the adversary's winning condition such that the adversary cannot win by breaking traceability in any bin  $u \leq j$ . As a first attempt, consider the following definition of  $\mathcal{H}_j$ : If, in any bin  $u \leq j$ , tracing identifies fewer than  $\tau$  traitors, we overwrite  $\mathsf{GoodTr} \leftarrow 1$ , and if tracing in any bin  $u \leq j$  falsely accuses a non-traitor, we overwrite  $\mathsf{BadTr} \leftarrow 0$ .

Observe that traceability holds by definition in the final hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_K$ : Either the additional condition is hit and GoodTr is set to 1, or  $\tau$  traitors were found in every bin, again implying GoodTr = 1. Likewise, BadTr is set to 0 whenever a non-traitor is falsely accused in any bin.

The challenge is to show that our modifications decreased Pr[BadTr], and increased Pr[GoodTr] by at most a negligible amount, which we aim to prove by a reduction to traceability of  $\Pi$ . Here, however, we run into a problem: Consider an adversary that outputs a good decoder only with probability, say 1/2, and thus might cause GoodTr = 1 with that same probability in  $\mathcal{H}_0$ . In  $\mathcal{H}_K$ , however, GoodTr is always 1. Clearly, this means that for such an adversary, we have increased Pr[GoodTr] by 1/2 across the hybrids, which is non-negligible.

<span id="page-6-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>4</sup>As an extreme example, consider the set  $S = B_1 \cup B_2 \cup \ldots \cup B_{K/2}$ . Clearly it holds that  $|S| \ge n/2$ , so S should be authorized, but S cannot decrypt in any of the bins  $B_{K/2+1}$  to  $B_K$ .

{7}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-7-0"></span>To address this issue, we do the following instead. At the beginning of tracing, we empirically test whether the decoder *D* is good, setting test ← 1 if it passes and test ← 0 otherwise. Then, whenever the modified condition in H*<sup>j</sup>* is hit, we set GoodTr ← test instead of GoodTr ← 1. Assume for now that the test perfectly distinguishes good decoders from bad ones. Then, we avoid the above issue, since in the final hybrid, Pr[GoodTr] would be elevated to Pr[GoodDec] = 1*/*2, and not 1. Also, observe that traceability still automatically holds in the last hybrid H*K*, since Pr[GoodTr] ≥ Pr[test] = Pr[GoodDec].

Finally, we design the empirical test such that *ε*-good decoders cannot test bad, and decoders that test good are at least *ε/*4-good, except with negligible probability, making them traceable in the base scheme Π. For a formal proof, we refer to Section [6.](#page-23-0)

**Amplifying Partial Insider Resilience.** So far, we have relied solely on the 1-to-1 tradeoff between traitors found and partial insiders to guarantee partial-insider resilience. To improve on this, we show next that we can achieve a better tradeoff by instantiating our compiler with a base scheme that is already resilient against *ρ* partial insiders (see Section [2.7](#page-10-0) for a construction of such a scheme). In particular, instead of the generic 1-to-1 tradeoff between traitors found and partial-insider resilience, this construction achieves a 1-to-(*ρ* + 1) tradeoff. We prove the following proposition, which extends Theorem [2.2,](#page-6-1) in Section [6.](#page-23-0)

**Proposition 2.3** (1-Partition Compiler with Partial Insiders (Informal)). *Let K* ∈ N *be the number of bins. If* Π *is τ -traceable against ρ partial insiders for τ* + *ρ* ≤ *t*bin*, then* Π1-part *is* ((*K* − *x*) · *τ* )*-traceable against* (*ρ* + *x* · (*ρ* + 1))*-partial insiders for all* 0 ≤ *x < K.*

To see this, observe that an adversary must concentrate at least *ρ* + 1 partial insiders in a bin, to cause tracing in that bin to fail. Thus, we still find *τ* traitors in all bins *i* ∈ [*K*], where |Ipd ∩ *B<sup>i</sup>* | ≤ *ρ*.

*Example 2.4* (1-to-(*ρ* + 1) Tradeoff). Let Π be a 1-traceable scheme that is resilient against *ρ* = *t*bin − 1 partial insiders. Let *K* = 3. Then, the scheme Π1-part is

- 3-traceable against (*t*bin−1)-partial insiders, and
- 2-traceable against (2*t*bin−1)-partial insiders, and
- 1-traceable against (3*t*bin−1)-partial insiders.

ciphertexts for all remaining partitions

**Robustness via Multi-Instance Expansion.** As discussed, the above scheme only achieves weak robustness: crashing *n*bin−*t*bin ≈ *n/*2*K* parties in any bin causes decryption to fail. To improve robustness, we instantiate *ℓ* independent copies of the 1-partition scheme Π1-part, sampling *ℓ* (*K, n*)-balanced partitions independently at key generation. To encrypt, sample a random key *k* ← { \$ 0*,* 1} *λ* , and for each partition *j* ∈ [*ℓ*], generate (*k<sup>j</sup> , c<sup>j</sup>* ) ←\$ Π1-part*.*Enc(pk*<sup>j</sup>* ). Set *u<sup>j</sup>* ← *k* ⊕ *k<sup>j</sup>* and *c* ← {(*c<sup>j</sup> , u<sup>j</sup>* )}*j*∈[*ℓ*] , which forms a 1-out-of-*ℓ* sharing of *k* across partitions.

The key intuition is that decryption succeeds if the honest parties successfully decrypt in at least one partition. Consequently, an adversary must block decryption in all *ℓ* partitions to break robustness, which, intuitively, should require crashing more than *n/*(2*K*) − *t*bin parties. In fact, we will show that we can achieve robustness against *n/*2 − *cn* maliciously chosen crash failures for any constant *c* ∈ (0*,* 1*/*2), at the cost of increasing ciphertext size by a factor *O*( *K*(1−*c*) *c* <sup>2</sup> ).

**Tracing via a PLBE-style Technique.** On a high level, the tracing in our Π*ℓ*-part compiler relies on a technique originating from Private Linear Broadcast Encryption (PLBE) [\[16\]](#page-64-7), where we view the partitions as users. A similar idea was used in [\[51\]](#page-66-2) to extend traceable broadcast encryption schemes to support more users. We note that we use this idea in multiple places. In particular, we extend it to the threshold setting in Section [9.](#page-33-0)

To build intuition, consider a decoder *D* against the multi-instance scheme Π*ℓ*-part. To decrypt a ciphertext *c* = {*c<sup>j</sup> , uj*}*j*∈[*ℓ*] successfully, it must have decrypted successfully in at least one partition *j* ∈ [*ℓ*]. Hence, if *D* has advantage *ε*, there must exist at least one partition, where *D* has advantage *ε/ℓ*. More formally, given a decoder *D* that has advantage *ε* against Π*ℓ*-part, we construct a decoder *D<sup>j</sup>* against the scheme Π1-part in partition *j* for all *j* ∈ [*ℓ*] as follows. On input (*c<sup>j</sup> , k<sup>j</sup>* ), *D<sup>j</sup>* generates

$$(k_q, c_q) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\text{1-part}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_q) \quad \forall q \neq j \in [\ell].$$

{8}------------------------------------------------

Then, it samples two random KEM keys  $k^0, k^1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , and shares  $k^0$  among all  $q \geq j$  partitions, while revoking all partitions q with q < j. More precisely, for q < j, sample  $u_q \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , and for  $q \geq j$ , set  $u_q \leftarrow k_q \oplus k^0$ . Then, the decoder  $D_j$  samples a random bit b and outputs  $b \oplus D(\{c_q, u_q\}_{q \in [\ell]}, k^b)$ .

We argue that, if D is  $\varepsilon$ -good, then there must exist at least one partition  $j \in [\ell]$ , for which  $D_j$  is  $\varepsilon/2\ell$ -good. Denote with  $X_j(b)$  the distribution  $(k^b, \{(c_q, u_q\}_{q \in [\ell]}), \text{ generated by sampling random } k^0, k^1,$  and encrypting  $k^0$  while revoking all  $u_q$  for  $q \leq j$  by replacing them with random values. Denote with  $p_j$  the decoder D's probability to distinguish  $X_j(0)$  from  $X_j(1)$ , i.e.,

$$p_i := \Pr[D(X_i(b)) = b].$$

Observe that, since D is  $\varepsilon$ -good, it must hold that  $p_0 \geq 1/2 + \varepsilon$ . Further, since in  $X_{\ell}(b)$  all partitions are revoked, it holds that  $X_{\ell}(0) \equiv X_{\ell}(1)$ , and thus  $p_{\ell} = 1/2$ . Hence, by pigeonhole principle, there must exist some  $j \in [\ell]$  such that the distinguishing probability drops significantly, i.e.,  $p_j \leq p_{j-1} - \varepsilon/\ell$ .

Now, let  $j^*$  be such j. When  $k_{j^*}$  is valid, i.e.,  $k_{j^*}$  is encapsulated in  $c_{j^*}$ ,  $D_{j^*}$  effectively runs D on  $X_{j^*-1}(b)$ , and outputs 0, whenever D guesses b correctly, and 1 otherwise. In other words,  $D_{j^*}$  correctly guesses that  $k_{j^*}$  is valid with probability  $p_{j^*-1}$ . If, however,  $k_{j^*}$  is invalid, then  $D_{j^*}$  runs D on  $X_{j^*}(b)$ , and outputs 1, whenever D guesses b incorrectly. Thus,  $D_{j^*}$  correctly guesses that  $k_{j^*}$  is invalid with probability  $1 - p_{j^*}$ . Overall, we conclude that  $D_{j^*}$  correctly predicts the validity of  $k_{j^*}$  with probability

$$\frac{1}{2}(p_{j^*-1}+1-p_{j^*}) = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{p_{j^*-1}-p_{j^*}}{2} \ge \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon}{2\ell}.$$

This means that  $D_{j^*}$  is  $(\varepsilon/2\ell)$ -good.

Tracing the good decoder  $D_{j^*}$  then identifies at least  $\tau$  traitors. A full formalization appears in Section 7, where we show that traceability is preserved for any  $\tau$ -traceable TT-KEM as base scheme, not only  $\Pi_{1-part}$ . The following Theorem shows that multi-instance expansion as above maintains traceability.

<span id="page-8-1"></span>**Theorem 2.5 (Multi-Instance Expansion (Informal)).** Let  $\Pi$  be a TT-KEM that is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $\rho$ -partial insiders. Then, expanding  $\Pi$  to  $\ell$  instances retains  $\tau$ -traceability against  $\rho$ -partial insiders. The public key, secret key, and ciphertext sizes grow by a factor  $\ell$ .

Consider instantiating Theorem 2.2 with a 1-traceable scheme  $\Pi$  across K bins. To achieve robustness against n/2-cn maliciously chosen crash failures for 0 < c < 1/2, it suffices to have  $\ell = \Theta \left( (1-c)K/c^2 \right)$  partitions. We prove this by showing that, with this choice of  $\ell$ , except with negligible probability, no set S with  $|S| \le n/2-cn$  intersects a bin in at least  $\frac{n}{2K}-t_{\text{bin}}$  parties in all partitions. Then, applying Theorem 2.5 with  $\ell = \frac{2K(1-c)}{c^2}$  yields the following Corollary.

<span id="page-8-2"></span>Corollary 2.6 For any  $K \in \mathbb{N}$  and  $c \in (0,1/2)$  there exists a K-traceable TT-KEM with  $(\frac{n}{2}-cn)$ -robustness and parameter sizes  $(P'(n),S'(n),C'(n))=\frac{2K^2(1-c)}{c^2}\cdot \left(P(n/K),\frac{1}{K}S(n/K),C(n/K)\right)$ .

Full multi-instance expansion and robustness analyses are given in Section 7.

## <span id="page-8-0"></span>**2.5** Tracing $\Theta(t)$ Traitors

Our next goal is to identify a linear number of traitors while maintaining succinct ciphertexts. Starting from a t-traceable scheme with O(n)-size ciphertexts, our approach is to design a compiler that reduces the ciphertext size of the scheme while maintaining a linear number of traitors found. To do so, we modify the 1-partition scheme as follows. We first fix  $K = \sqrt{n}$  bins, and thus  $n_{\text{bin}} = \sqrt{n}$  and  $t_{\text{bin}} = \frac{\sqrt{n}}{2} - O(n^{1/4})$ . As building blocks, we use any (not necessarily traceable) threshold KEM with ciphertext size O(1), denoted by TE, and the naive t-traceable scheme  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$  with ciphertext size O(n) (discussed in Appendix D).

During key generation, we set up one instance of  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$  and one instance of TE in each of the  $\sqrt{n}$  bins. Then, to encrypt, one samples a random bin  $i \in [\sqrt{n}]$ , and encrypts as in the  $\Pi_{\text{1-part}}$  compiler, but using the instance of  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$  in the *i*'th bin, and the instances of TE in all remaining bins. Clearly, since in each bin we instantiate with  $n_{\text{bin}} = \sqrt{n}$ , and only one bin contains the  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$  ciphertext, the overall ciphertext size is  $\Theta(\sqrt{n})$ , assuming that  $C_{\text{TE}}$  is constant in n. Denote with type-i ciphertexts a ciphertext where  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$  is in the *i*'th bin.

{9}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-9-2"></span>**Tracing Intuition.** Suppose, for now, that D is perfect, i.e., that it always decrypts correctly. Then, D also perfectly decrypts type-i ciphertexts for all  $i \in [\sqrt{n}]$ . Similarly to  $\Pi_{1\text{-part}}$ , we can transform D into a decoder  $D_i$  that is good against the  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$  instance in each bin  $i \in [\sqrt{n}]$ , and tracing  $D_i$  in  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$  will yield  $t_{\text{bin}}$  traitors. As bins are disjoint, we find  $t = \sqrt{n} \cdot t_{\text{bin}} = n/2 - o(n)$  traitors in total.

The above argument assumes that the decoder D is perfect, and thus successfully decrypts ciphertexts of all types. However, a decoder may choose to decrypt only ciphertexts of, say, a single type (corresponding to a fixed bin i), in which case tracing would succeed for only one bin.

To address this issue, we first consider decoders that are  $\varepsilon$ -good for some constant  $\varepsilon \in (0, 1/2)$ . We show by a counting argument that such a decoder must successfully decrypt type-i ciphertexts for sufficiently many bins i. As an example, if  $\varepsilon = 1/4$ , we show that there must be at least  $5\sqrt{n}/11$  bins i, where  $D_i$  is at least  $\varepsilon' = 1/24$  good. Tracing in these bins yields  $\frac{5n}{22} - o(n)$  traitors.

Finally, to remove the limitation that decoders must be at least  $\varepsilon \geq 1/4$ -good, we can apply the limit elimination compiler (Theorem B.2) due to [51, 28], which upgrades traceability to hold for decoders with arbitrarily small non-negligible advantage. We extend it straightforwardly to the multi-traitor and insider setting in Appendix B. The parameter sizes increase only by a constant amount.

Unfortunately, this approach achieves at most  $O(\sqrt{n})$ -robustness. Applying multi-partition expansion does not help, because, to achieve n/2-cn robustness, one would need to instantiate  $\Theta(\sqrt{n})$  partitions. As each partition already has ciphertext size  $\Theta(\sqrt{n})$ , the overall ciphertext size would again be  $\Theta(n)$ .

<span id="page-9-1"></span>**Theorem 2.7** There exists a  $\frac{5n}{22}$ -traceable ramp TT-KEM with  $O(\sqrt{n})$ -robustness and ciphertext size  $O(\sqrt{n})$ .

The traceability proof essentially extends that of the 1-partition compiler by the analysis that any good decoder must be sufficiently good when running against  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$  in sufficiently many bins, hence we defer formalization to Section 8.

## <span id="page-9-0"></span>**2.6** Compiling from 1-traceability to $\Theta(n^a)$ -traceability for $a \in (0,1)$

So far, we have shown how to trace a constant number of traitors with strong robustness (Section 2.4), and how to identify  $\Theta(t)$  traitors with limited robustness (Section 2.5). Our goal is now to combine these guarantees: namely, to achieve robustness close to n/2 while tracing a non-constant number of traitors (e.g.,  $\Theta(\sqrt{n})$ ), with only sublinear ciphertext blowup. Moreover, we aim to maintain almost maximal resilience against partial insiders whenever the underlying scheme itself is maximally resilient.

A natural approach is to instantiate the  $\Pi_{1\text{-part}}$  compiler with  $K=\sqrt{n}$  bins, which yields  $\Theta(\sqrt{n})$  traitors. However, maintaining (n/2-cn)-robustness requires multi-instance expansion with  $\sqrt{n}$  partitions (by Corollary 2.6), resulting in linear ciphertext blowup, since we incur  $\sqrt{n}$  partitions  $\times \sqrt{n}$  bins. The first attempt to achieve stronger robustness is to require threshold decryption across bins within a single partition (rather than K-out-of-K). This, however, comes at the cost of reduced resilience against partial insiders. Achieving  $(\frac{n}{2}-cn)$ -robustness in this manner provides resilience against at most cn partial insiders, for  $c \in (0,1/2)$ . For example, achieving  $\frac{n}{3}$ -robustness is possible only if the scheme tolerates at most  $\frac{n}{6}$  partial insiders. Hence, our next goal is to achieve both (n/2-cn)-robustness and (n/2-cn)-resilience against partial insiders, for  $small\ c \in (0,1/2)$  at the same time, while tracing  $\Theta(n^a)$  traitors with sublinear ciphertext blowup.

To avoid the linear blowup incurred by large partitions, we reduce the number of bins per partition, K, to  $o(\sqrt{n})$ , specifically to a constant. In  $\Pi_{1\text{-part}}$ , however, K directly bounds the number of traitors found (assuming a base scheme with  $\tau = 1$ ). Hence, the challenge is to find  $\Theta(\sqrt{n})$  traitors while using partitions with only a constant number of bins. The key idea is to trace across  $\ell = \Theta(\sqrt{n})$  partitions, rather than relying on a single partition with  $\Theta(\sqrt{n})$  bins.

Achieving this requires forcing the decoder to succeed in many partitions. We do so by sharing the KEM key in a threshold manner across partitions, rather than using the 1-out-of- $\ell$  approach of the multi-instance expansion compiler. Concretely, with an  $\ell/2$ -out-of- $\ell$  threshold, any good decoder must succeed in at least  $\ell/2$  partitions, enabling tracing in each. Our first challenge is to show that any good decoder can be converted into one that is good in at least  $\ell/2$  partitions, so that tracing can be applied to each. This introduces subtle technical challenges, which we resolve in Section 9.

Even after resolving the previous challenge and tracing across  $\Theta(\sqrt{n})$  partitions, a critical issue remains: most traced traitors may be duplicates. For example, if K=2 with each bin of size n/2, a party appears (in expectation) in half of the partitions in the first bin and half in the second. An adversary

{10}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-10-2"></span>can then select a small set S (of constant size) whose members appear in *both* bins in  $\ell/2$  partitions, so tracing may yield only |S| distinct traitors.

To overcome this, the key idea is to use unbalanced partitions, consisting of a small bin of size  $\sqrt{n}$  and a large bin of size  $n-\sqrt{n}$ . By a simple probabilistic argument, a party can appear in the small bin only in a small fraction  $2/\sqrt{n}$  of partitions. Consequently, tracing a traitor from the small bin in  $\ell/2$  partitions (for  $\ell = \Theta(\lambda \cdot \sqrt{n})$ ) guarantees that at least  $\frac{0.5\ell}{2\ell/\sqrt{n}} = \frac{\sqrt{n}}{4}$ , of the traced traitors are distinct. We rigorously develop the above idea in Section 9, where we explain why this construction achieves robustness and how to handle partial insiders, resulting in the following theorem.

<span id="page-10-1"></span>Theorem 2.8 (Unbalanced-Partitions Compiler (Informal)). Let  $\Pi$  be a 1-traceable TT-KEM. For all constants  $a \in (0,1)$  and  $c \in (0,1/2)$  there exists a  $(0.3n^{1-a})$ -traceable TT-KEM with (n/2-cn)-robustness and parameter sizes  $(P'(n),S'(n),C'(n))=\ell\cdot(P_\Pi(n^a),S_\Pi(n^a),C_\Pi(n^a))$ , where  $\ell=\Theta(\lambda n^{1-a}/c^2)$ . If  $\Pi$  is maximally partial-insider resilient, the resulting scheme is (n/2-cn)-partial-insider resilient.

## <span id="page-10-0"></span>2.7 A Maximally Resilient Construction against Partial Insiders

Next, we show how to extend one of the constructions in [12] to achieve traceability against up to (t-1) partial insiders. We then present how this construction can be used within the compilers introduced in the previous sections.

Tracing in [12] builds on the non-threshold traitor tracing construction of [10, 4], which samples a fingerprinting code [15] with codewords  $(\mathsf{cw}^{(1)}, \dots, \mathsf{cw}^{(n)}) \in \{0, 1\}^{\ell}$ . For each position  $j \in [\ell]$  and bit  $v \in \{0, 1\}$ , a key pair  $(sk_{j,v}, pk_{j,v})$  is generated using a standard public key encryption scheme. User i receives the secret keys corresponding to its codeword  $\{\mathsf{sk}_{j,\mathsf{cw}_j^{(i)}}\}_{j\in[\ell]}$ . To encrypt, a random position  $j \in [\ell]$  is chosen and the same KEM key is encrypted under both left and right public keys  $\mathsf{pk}_{j,0}$  and  $\mathsf{pk}_{j,1}$ , yielding a ciphertext  $((c_0, c_1), j)$ , where  $c_0$  is the left ciphertext, and  $c_1$  is the right ciphertext.

A decoder that holds only left (resp. right) keys at position j can decrypt only  $c_0$  (resp.  $c_1$ ). This asymmetry allows extraction of a codeword from any successful decoder, which can then be traced using the fingerprinting code. The work of [12] extends this approach to the threshold setting via a bipartite threshold KEM (BT-KEM) satisfying two properties: Two-sided correctness ensures that any t parties, possibly holding mixed keys, can decrypt. One-sided security ensures that adversaries who only hold keys from one side at position j cannot distinguish if the ciphertext of the other side is consistent with the given KEM-key (i.e., valid) or not (i.e., invalid).

Our new BT-KEM. We now extend the result of [12] to consider decoders that receive partial decryptions. To handle this, we allow the tracer to simulate partial decryptions and define two new security properties, replacing one-sided security:

**Property 1 (Right-SPD Security)**ensures that adversaries who hold only right keys for a position j cannot decrypt the KEM-key encapsulated in the *left ciphertext*, even if they obtain up to t-1 simulated partial decryptions consistent with the left ciphertext.

**Property 2 (Left-SPD Security)** ensures that adversaries with only left keys for a position j cannot distinguish whether left and right ciphertexts encapsulate *the same* key, even if they receive up to t-1 simulated partial decryptions consistent with the left ciphertext.

Realizing these notions is nontrivial. In BT-KEM, left and right keys are correlated, so partial decryptions of one side may leak information about the other. Indeed, the DDH-based construction in [12, Section 5.1] fails to realize our first property. To see this, let us consider the construction. For simplicity, we assume that  $\ell=1$ . The public key is  $Y=g^y, Z=g^z, X=g^\alpha$  for random  $\alpha,y,z\in\mathbb{Z}_p$ . The left key for the *i*-th party is  $s_i/y$  and the right key is  $s_i/z$ , where  $s_1,\ldots,s_n$  are *t*-out-of-*n* secret shares of  $\alpha$ . To encrypt, sample a random r and let  $c_0=Y^r, c_1=Z^r$ . The encapsulated KEM key is  $k=X^r=g^{\alpha r}$ .

To see why Right-SPD security is broken, consider an *invalid ciphertext*, i.e.,  $c_0 = Y^r$ ,  $c_1 = Z^{r'}$  for independent random  $r, r' \in \mathbb{F}_p$ , and suppose the decoder holds all right keys but no left keys. Further, suppose that the decoder receives a simulated partial decryption  $d_i = g^{s_i r}$  of the left encapsulated key on behalf of a party i, whose right key is  $s_i/z$ . It can then compute  $d_i^{z/s_i} = g^{zr} = Z^r$  using the right key  $z/s_i = (s_i/z)^{-1}$ . As  $Z^r$  is an encapsulation of the KEM key  $g^{\alpha r}$  in a right ciphertext, the adversary can then recover  $g^{\alpha r}$  using only the right keys, breaking Right-SPD security.

Fortunately, this issue does not appear in the second construction of [12], which uses pairings: secret keys live in the source group while partial decryptions are target group elements, which prevents the

{11}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-11-4"></span>adversary from performing the nonlinear operations on the secret keys required by the above attack. We formally prove in Section [10.2](#page-46-0) that the second construction of [\[12\]](#page-63-4) is Left-SPD and Right-SPD secure with only minimal modifications. Our proof relies on an interactive assumption, proven in the GGM [\[49,](#page-66-3) [45\]](#page-66-4).

**Partial-Insider Resilient Tracing from** PD**-**BT**-**KEM**.** We next explain our tracing algorithm. Suppose the adversary outputs a decoder *D* and a set of partial insiders Ipd with |Ipd| *< t*. On a high level, tracing follows the pattern of [\[10,](#page-63-8) [12\]](#page-63-4), i.e., we extract for which positions the decoder holds left (resp. right) keys, and then feed this information into the fingerprinting code to identify a traitor. Now, however, we need to simulate partial decryptions for Ipd when querying the decoder, relying on our new properties of Right-SPD and Left-SPD security.

For each position *j*, the tracer queries *D* on valid and invalid ciphertexts while simulating partial decryptions consistent with the left ciphertext. For each position, we count how many times the decoder outputs a key that is consistent with the left part. Let *p<sup>j</sup>* count the number of trials where this happens for valid ciphertexts, and *q<sup>j</sup>* for invalid ciphertexts, respectively. We then extract information by the following two arguments:

- (1) If *q<sup>j</sup> >* 0, i.e., if the decoder outputs the key encapsulated in the left ciphertext *at least once* for invalid ciphertexts, we conclude that it must have a left secret key in position *j*. Otherwise, the decoder would break Right-SPD security (Property 1).
- (2) If *q<sup>j</sup>* and *p<sup>j</sup>* are significantly far apart, we conclude that the decoder must have a right secret key in position *j*, since valid and invalid ciphertexts are indistinguishable without right keys due to Left-SPD security (Property 2).

Traceability then follows by reduction to these properties, extending the analysis of [\[10\]](#page-63-8). We formalize the above approach in Section [10,](#page-43-0) where we establish the following theorem.

<span id="page-11-3"></span>**Theorem 2.9 (**(*t*−1)**-Partial Insiders (Informal)).** *There exists a* 1*-traceable* TT-KEM *that is secure against* (*t* − 1)*-partial insiders, where t is the threshold. The scheme has secret key size O*(*n* 2 )*, and constant-size public keys and ciphertexts. Partial insider resilience holds against decrypting decoders.*

Unfortunately, our construction only guarantees partial-insider resilience for *decrypting decoders*: While Right-SPD security prevents adversaries without left keys from decrypting the left ciphertext, simulated partial decryptions may still allow them to *distinguish* valid from invalid ciphertexts. Achieving maximal partial-insider resilience for *distinguishing decoders* is an interesting direction for future work.

**Instantiations.** We next present instantiations of our compilers using the maximally partial-insider resilient scheme from Theorem [2.9.](#page-11-3) A comparison between these instantiations for a concrete set of parameters can be found in Figure [1.](#page-3-3) Instantiating the 1-partition compiler together with a multi-instance expansion for robustness (Theorems [2.2](#page-6-1) and [2.5\)](#page-8-1) yields:

<span id="page-11-1"></span>**Corollary 2.10** *For any constant K and c* ∈ (0*,* 1*/*2) *there exists a* TT-KEM *that is* (*K*−*x*)*-traceable against* (*x*·*t*bin +*t*bin −1) *partial insiders for t*bin = *n/*2*K* −*O*( p *n/K*) *for all x < K. The scheme achieves* (*n/*2−*cn*)*-robustness and parameter sizes* (*P*(*n*)*, S*(*n*)*, C*(*n*)) = 2*K*<sup>2</sup> (1−*c*) *c* <sup>2</sup> · *O*(1)*, O*(*n* <sup>2</sup>*/K*<sup>2</sup> )*, O*(1) *. Partial insider resilience is guaranteed against decrypting decoders.*

Instantiating our unbalanced compiler (Theorem [2.8\)](#page-10-1) with Theorem [2.9](#page-11-3) yields:

<span id="page-11-2"></span>**Corollary 2.11** *For arbitrary a* ∈ (0*,* 1) *and c* ∈ (0*,* 1*/*2)*, there exists a* TT-KEM *that is* (0*.*3*n* 1−*a* ) *traceable with n/*2 − *cn robustness and sizes* (*P*(*n*)*, S*(*n*)*, C*(*n*)) = *ℓ* · *O*(1)*, O*(*n* 2*a* )*, O*(1) *, for ℓ* = Θ(*λn*<sup>1</sup>−*<sup>a</sup>/c*<sup>2</sup> )*. Furthermore, it is resilient against* (*n/*2) − *cn partial insiders for decrypting decoders.*

#### <span id="page-11-0"></span>**2.8 Additional Related Work and Future Research**

**Related Work.** Traitor tracing, introduced by Chor et al. [\[26\]](#page-64-6) for piracy protection, has been extensively studied in broadcast encryption. Notably, Zhandry [\[52\]](#page-66-5) constructed a pairing-based scheme with parameters independent of the number of users, with similar results via LWE [\[38,](#page-65-7) [25\]](#page-64-9) and iO-based techniques [\[17,](#page-64-10) [39\]](#page-65-8).

To the best of our knowledge, traceability in the presence of insiders was not studied previously. Existing work addresses related but weaker settings. In [\[12\]](#page-63-4), decoders can receive partial decryptions, but

{12}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-12-1"></span>they consider only small coalitions of < t traitors, and only decoders that work for any set of partial decryptions, rather than allowing the adversary to specify a designated set of insiders. In [21], the authors construct a multi-traitor tracing scheme that achieves amortized constant-size ciphertexts in an epoch-based setting, but linear size in the worst case; in contrast, our compilers always achieve sublinear ciphertext size.

Other works study traitor tracing in related primitives such as secret sharing [13, 41], and threshold VRFs [14], however, they work only with small coalitions, do not consider insider attacks, and it is unclear how to extend these techniques to traceable threshold encryption. Notably, [40] studies reconstruction boxes for specific sets of shares, but, again, only for small coalitions and for secret sharing.

In [32, 19], the authors propose an entirely different collusion model where committee members can collude in MPC (without a decoder). However, their constructions are currently limited to secret-sharing schemes and rely on a strong, non-standard assumption, in particular, the existence of MPC-hard functions.

In [42], the authors use related techniques to construct silent ramp threshold encryption. In their construction, a ciphertext is encrypted to a small random committee of size  $O(\lambda)$ , independent of n. Security and robustness are then derived from the fact that the exact committee is hidden: without a party's secret key, it is infeasible to determine whether that party belongs to the committee. We view it as an interesting direction for future work to consider collusion resilience in this setting, especially given that even a small coalition of constant size  $\ll t$  can adaptively compromise secrecy and robustness on a per-ciphertext basis.

**Extensions and Future Work.** A particularly interesting direction for future work is improving the robustness of our O(n)-traceable construction.

So is exploring the relationship between multi-traitor traceability and traceability against insiders. While we show the former implies the latter, whether the converse holds is open. More broadly, alternative approaches to traceability against insiders are worth investigating.

Our compilers assume reconstruction threshold n/2. However, they can be easily extended to any threshold cn for  $c \in (0,1)$  by appropriately adjusting the per-bin threshold  $t_{\text{bin}}$  relative to t and handling edge cases.

## <span id="page-12-0"></span>3 Preliminaries

**Notation.** We denote the security parameter by  $\lambda$ , and write  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$  (resp.  $\operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$ ) for functions that are negligible (resp. polynomials) in  $\lambda$ . We denote the number of parties by n. When describing asymptotic properties of our constructions, we ignore factors other than n in the O-notation. For integers  $a, b \in \mathbb{N}$ , we denote the set  $\{1, \ldots, b\}$  by [b] and the set  $\{a, \ldots, b\}$  by [a, b]. We write  $y \leftarrow A(x; r)$  to execute the algorithm A on input x with randomness r and assign the result to y. If A is executed with uniform randomness, we abbreviate  $y \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} A(x)$  to indicate that A is probabilistic. We write  $S \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \binom{M}{t}$  to denote the process of sampling a subset S of size t uniformly at random from a set M.

**Lagrange Interpolation.** Some of our constructions rely on Lagrange interpolation. Denote with  $\lambda_{i,S}^{(x)} = \prod_{j \in S \setminus \{i\}} \frac{x-j}{i-j}$  the Lagrange coefficient for interpolating a polynomial at position x using index i among the set of indices S. Then, given points  $y_i$  for  $i \in S$ , the polynomial

$$p(x) = \sum_{i \in S} \lambda_{i,S}^{(x)} \cdot y_i$$

is of degree |S|-1, and  $p(i)=y_i$  for all  $i \in S$ .

**Shamir's Secret Sharing.** We use Shamir's secret sharing (Share, Rec) to share a secret s across a threshold t of n shares.

**Definition 3.1** (Shamir Secret Sharing). For a field  $\mathbb{F}$ , Shamir's t-out-of-n secret sharing is a tuple of algorithms (Share, Rec).

{13}------------------------------------------------

- <span id="page-13-3"></span>•  $\{s_i\}_{i\in[n]} \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Share}(s,n,t)$ . To share a secret  $s\in\mathbb{F}$  into  $n\in\mathbb{N}$  shares with threshold  $0< t\le n$ , the dealer samples a uniformly random polynomial p of degree t-1 over  $\mathbb{F}$  such that p(0)=s and sets  $s_i=p(i)$ .
- $s \leftarrow \text{Rec}(t, S, \{s_i\}_{i \in S})$ . To reconstruct the secret s from at least t shares  $\{s_i\}_{i \in S}$  corresponding to the set of indices S with  $|S| \ge t$ , one interpolates  $s = p(0) = \sum_{i \in S} \lambda_{i,S}^{(0)} \cdot s_i$ .

Any set of t-1 shares does not leak information about the secret and appears uniformly random over  $\mathbb{F}^{t-1}$ .

**Tail Bounds.** Throughout this work, we use the following formulation of the Chernoff bound [47].

**Lemma 3.2** (Chernoff Bound). Let  $X_1, \ldots, X_n$  be independent random variables taking values in  $\{0, 1\}$ . Let  $X = \sum_{i=1}^n X_i$ , and  $\mu = \mathbb{E}[X]$ . Then, for any  $\delta > 0$  it holds that

$$\Pr[X \ge (1+\delta)\mu] \le \exp\left(-\frac{\delta^2\mu}{2+\delta}\right),$$

and for  $0 < \delta < 1$  it holds that

$$\Pr[X \le (1 - \delta)\mu] \le \exp\left(-\frac{\delta^2 \mu}{2}\right).$$

We also make use of Hoeffding's inequality.

**Lemma 3.3** (Hoeffding Inequality). Let  $X_1, \ldots, X_n$  be independent random variables taking values in [a,b]. Let  $X = \sum_{i=1}^{n} X_i$ , and  $\mu = \mathbb{E}[X]$ . Then, for any t > 0 it holds that

$$\Pr[X \leq \mu - t] \leq \exp\left(-\frac{2t^2}{n(b-a)^2}\right) \ and \ \Pr[X \geq \mu + t] \leq \exp\left(-\frac{2t^2}{n(b-a)^2}\right).$$

#### <span id="page-13-0"></span>3.1 Partitions of Parties

**Definition 3.4** ((K, n)-Partition). For  $K, n \in \mathbb{N}$ , a (K, n)-partition of a set of n parties is a collection  $P = (B_1, \ldots, B_K)$  of K pairwise disjoint subsets of parties, i.e.,  $B_i \subseteq [n]$  for all  $i \in [K]$ ,  $B_i \cap B_j = \emptyset$  for all  $i \neq j \in [K]$ , and  $\bigcup_{i \in [K]} B_i = [n]$ . We refer to each subset  $B_i$  as a bin. We say that P is balanced if all bins have the same size, i.e.,  $|B_i| = |B_j|$  for all  $i, j \in [K]$ . Otherwise, P is said to be unbalanced. We denote bin(P, i) = j to indicate that  $i \in B_j$  in partition P.

We will frequently need to argue whether a given set of parties controls sufficiently many members of a bin, a partition, or a set of partitions. For this, we introduce the following notation.

<span id="page-13-2"></span>**Definition 3.5** (Domination). Let  $K, n \in \mathbb{N}$  and let  $P = \{B_j\}_{j \in [K]}$  be a (K, n)-partition. For  $S \subseteq [n]$ ,  $r, \beta, \ell \in \mathbb{N}$ , and  $\mathbf{r} = (r_1, \ldots, r_K) \in \mathbb{N}^K$ , we say that

- S r-dominates a bin B if  $|S \cap B| \ge r$ .
- S **r**-dominates the partition P if the set S  $r_j$ -dominates  $B_j$  for all  $j \in [K]$ .
- $S(\beta, \mathbf{r})$ -dominates a collection of  $\ell(K, n)$ -partitions  $\{P_i\}_{i \in [\ell]}$  if there exist indices  $i_1, \ldots, i_\beta \in [\ell]$  such that S **r**-dominates partition  $P_{i_j}$  for all  $j \in [\beta]$ .
- S **r**-blocks partition P if there exists a  $j \in [K]$  such that S  $r_j$ -dominates  $B_j$ .
- $S(\beta, \mathbf{r})$ -blocks a collection of  $\ell(K, n)$ -partitions  $\{P_i\}_{i \in [\ell]}$  if there exist indices  $i_1, \ldots, i_\beta \in [\ell]$  such that S  $\mathbf{r}$ -blocks partition  $P_{i_j}$  for all  $j \in [\beta]$ .

### <span id="page-13-1"></span>3.2 (Traceable) Threshold KEMs

Next, we define TT-KEMs. A Definitions of traceability is deferred to Section 4.

**Definition 3.6** ((Traceable) Threshold KEMs). A traceable threshold KEM is a tuple of algorithms TT-KEM = (Setup, KGen, Enc, PDec, Combine, Trace). It has key-space  $\mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$ , randomness space  $\mathcal{R}_{\lambda}$ , and for  $n \in \mathbb{N}$ , it supports reconstruction thresholds in  $\mathcal{S}_n \subseteq [n]$ . For all  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$  it supports security thresholds  $t(r) \leq r$ . In more detail, let

{14}------------------------------------------------

- $pp \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$  generates parameters that are public input to all algorithms.
- $(\mathsf{pk}, \{sk_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{tk}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$ . Given the number of parties n, reconstruction threshold  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$ , security threshold  $0 < t \le t(r)$ , and the limit  $\varepsilon$ ,  $\mathsf{KGen}$  outputs a public key  $\mathsf{pk}$ , n secret key shares  $\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in [n]}$ , and a tracing key  $\mathsf{tk}$ . Sometimes we replace n with a concrete set of parties for convenience.
- $(k, c) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}; r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ . Given public key  $\mathsf{pk}$  and randomness  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}} \in \mathcal{R}_{\lambda}$ , the encryption algorithm outputs a KEM key  $k \in \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$  and a ciphertext c.
- $d_i/\bot \leftarrow \mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i, c)$ . Given a secret key  $\mathsf{sk}_i$  and a ciphertext c, the partial decryption algorithm outputs a partial decryption  $d_i$  or  $\bot$  to indicate failure.
- $k/\perp \leftarrow \mathsf{Combine}(\mathsf{pk}, S, \{d_i\}_{i \in S}, c)$ . Given the public key  $\mathsf{pk}$  and decryption shares  $d_i$  from a set of at least r parties S on a ciphertext c, the  $\mathsf{Combine}$  algorithm returns a KEM key  $k \in \mathcal{K}$  or  $\perp$  to indicate failure.
- $\mathcal{T}' \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Trace}^D(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{tk},\varepsilon,\ldots)$ . The Trace algorithm will be defined in Section 4.

We consider a non-traceable threshold KEM T-KEM to be a TT-KEM without the parts highlighted in gray, i.e., where  $\varepsilon = \mathsf{tk} = \bot$  and Trace is undefined. We say that a TT-KEM has no ramp if t(r) = r.

We move on to define correctness, robustness, and security.

**Definition 3.7** (Perfect Correctness). We say that a TT-KEM  $\Pi$  is perfectly correct if for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all  $\mathsf{pp} \in \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda)$ , all  $n \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , all  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$ , and all  $0 < t \le t(r)$ , all  $\varepsilon \in (0, 1/2]$ , all  $\mathsf{pp} \in \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda)$ , all  $(\mathsf{pk}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{tk}) \in \mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$ , all  $(k, c) \in \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk})$ , and all  $S \subseteq [n]$  with  $|S| \ge r$  it holds that

$$\mathsf{Combine}\big(\mathsf{pk}, S, \{\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i, c)\}_{i \in S}, c\big) = k.$$

Some of our constructions offer only probabilistic correctness. Intuitively, probabilistic correctness states that a random authorized set can decrypt with overwhelming probability.

**Definition 3.8** (Probabilistic Correctness). We say that a TT-KEM  $\Pi$  is probabilistically correct if there exists a negligible function negl such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  there exists some  $n_0 \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , such that for all  $\mathsf{pp} \in \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$ , all  $n \geq n_0$ , all  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$ , and all  $0 < t \leq t(r)$ , all  $\varepsilon \in (0, 1/2]$ , all  $\mathsf{pp} \in \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$ , all  $(\mathsf{pk}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{tk}) \in \mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$ , all  $(k, c) \in \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk})$ , and all r' with  $r \leq r' \leq n$ , it holds that

$$\Pr_{S \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \binom{[n]}{r'}} \left[ \mathsf{Combine}(\mathsf{pk}, S, \{\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i, c)\}_{i \in S}, c) = k \right] \geq 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

We also define robustness against up to  $\boldsymbol{c}$  maliciously chosen crash failures. The definition below argues that no PPT adversary can cause decryption to fail by crashing at most  $\boldsymbol{c}$  parties, except with negligible probability.

**Definition 3.9** (c-robustness). For a function c(n,r) with  $0 \le c(n,r) \le n-r$ , we say that a TT-KEM is robust against c maliciously chosen crash failures if for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function negl such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  there exists some  $n_0 \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  such that for all  $n \ge n_0$ , all  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$ , and all  $0 < t \le t(r)$ , all  $\varepsilon \in (0, 1/2]$ , it holds that

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{c} |S| \geq n - \mathsf{c}(n,r) \; \wedge \\ \mathsf{Combine}\left(\mathsf{pk}, S, \left\{\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i, c)\right\}_{i \in S}, c\right) \neq k \end{array} \right. : \begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{tk}) \; \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, r, t, \varepsilon) \\ (k, c) \; \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}) \\ S \; \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \; \mathcal{A}(1^\lambda, n, r, t, \mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk}, \varepsilon, \mathsf{tk}, \mathsf{c}) \end{array} \right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

We say that a TT-KEM is *perfectly* robust, if it is (n-r)-robust.

For simplicity, we only consider semantic security with static corruptions in this paper.

**Definition 3.10** (Semantic Security). A TT-KEM  $\Pi$  is semantically secure if for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all  $n \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , all  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$ , all  $0 < t \le t(r)$ , and all  $\varepsilon \in (0, 1/2]$  it holds that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Game\text{-}IND\text{-}CPA}^{\Pi}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda},n,r,t,\varepsilon) = 1] \leq \frac{1}{2} + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where Game-IND-CPA is defined in Figure 2.

{15}------------------------------------------------

Figure 2: Semantic security game of TT-KEMs

<span id="page-15-2"></span>**Definition 3.11** (Perfect TT-KEM). We say that a (traceable) threshold KEM is *perfect* if it is perfectly correct, perfectly robust, semantically secure, has no ramp, and  $S_n = [n]$ .

# <span id="page-15-0"></span>4 Selling Decoders to Insiders – a Stronger Definition

In this section, we outline our definition of traceability against insiders. First, we define the Trace algorithm to have the following syntax:

 $\mathcal{T}' \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \operatorname{Trace}^{D(\cdot)}(\operatorname{pk},\operatorname{tk},\varepsilon,\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{sk}},\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{pd}},\{\operatorname{sk}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{sk}}\cup\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{pd}}})$ . The tracing algorithm receives the public key  $\operatorname{pk}$ , the tracing key  $\operatorname{tk}$ , the limit  $\varepsilon$ , the set of full insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{sk}}$ , the set of partial insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{pd}}$ , the secret keys of all insiders, as well as black-box access to a decoder algorithm D. It outputs a set of traitors  $\mathcal{T}'$ .

Consider Definition 4.1 and the corresponding experiment in Figure 3. Our definition generalizes and strengthens the existing definition due to [12] in the following ways:

- We parameterize the number of traitors that must be identified by a parameter  $\tau$ , modifying the event GoodTr accordingly. The definition of [12] corresponds to the special case  $\tau = 1$ .
- We explicitly capture security against insiders. In Game-Tr, the adversary specifies two sets,  $\mathcal{I}_{sk}$  and  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ , along with a decoder D. The set  $\mathcal{I}_{sk}$  consists of full insiders who provide their secret keys, while  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$  consists of partial insiders who supply partial decryptions of a given ciphertext to the decoder. For a given scheme, the parameters  $\sigma$  and  $\rho$  denote the maximum number of permissible full insiders and partial insiders, respectively.
- The challenger runs the tracing algorithm on D, which makes use of the secret keys of both full and partial insiders, to determine the set of traitors found  $\mathcal{T}'$ .
- We refine the event GoodDec to reflect this notion of traceability. A decoder D is  $\varepsilon$ -good if it has an advantage  $\varepsilon$  when run with insider information from the specified sets  $\mathcal{I}_{sk}$  and  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ . In GoodTr, the identified traitors may include insiders, but only if they are also traitors, i.e., if they were corrupted by the adversary via the  $\mathcal{O}^{corrupt}$  oracle. This models collusions where some traitors act as insiders to deflect blame.
- We optionally grant the adversary access to a partial decryption oracle for honestly generated ciphertexts  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$ . Unlike the standard corruption oracle  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}$ , which allows the adversary to adaptively corrupt traitors, this oracle models the scenario where colluding parties might observe partial decryptions of ciphertexts from honest parties (i.e., non-traitors). This captures a very realistic threat in certain scenarios (such as encrypted mempools where all committee members publish their partial decryptions every block), and essentially requires schemes to guarantee that learning partial decryptions from non-traitors does not allow colluders to frame honest parties. Additionally, it models that the adversary could decide on who to corrupt as traitor based on information leaked through partial decryptions. Unless stated otherwise, statements about traceability apply to traceability with and without  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$  access.

{16}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-16-1"></span>Overall, if we say that a scheme is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders, informally speaking, this means that tracing a decoder that works for a set of at most  $\sigma$  full insiders and  $\rho$  partial insiders is guaranteed to identify at least  $\tau$  traitors.

| $Game-Tr_{\mathcal{A},\varepsilon,\sigma,\rho}(1^\lambda,n,r,t)$                                                                                                           | $\mathcal{O}^{corrupt}(i)$                                                                  |  |  |
|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|--|--|
| $\mathcal{T} \leftarrow \emptyset$                                                                                                                                         | $\overline{\mathbf{if}\ i \not\in [n]\ \mathbf{return}\ \bot}$                              |  |  |
| $pp \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\scriptscriptstyle\$}{\leftarrow} Setup(1^{\lambda})$                                                                                           | $\mathbf{Update}\mathcal{T} \leftarrow \mathcal{T} \cup \{i\}$                              |  |  |
| $\left  \left( pk, \left\{ sk_i \right\}_{[n]}, tk \right) \xleftarrow{\$} KGen(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t, \varepsilon) \right $                                                | ${\bf return} \; {\sf sk}_i$                                                                |  |  |
| $(D,(\mathcal{I}_{sk},\mathcal{I}_{pd})) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{A}^{\mathcal{O}^{corrupt}, \boxed{\mathcal{O}^{P\overline{Dec}}, \boxed{Hon}}}(1^{\lambda},pk)$ | $\mathcal{O}^{PDec\text{-}Hon}(i,r_{Enc})$                                                  |  |  |
| $\mathbf{assert}\ \mathcal{I}_{sk} \cap \mathcal{I}_{pd} = \emptyset \wedge  \mathcal{I}_{sk}  \leq \sigma \wedge  \mathcal{I}_{pd}  \leq \rho$                            | $   \overline{\mathbf{if} \ r_{Enc} \not\in \mathcal{R}_{Enc} \ \mathbf{return} \perp }   $ |  |  |
| $\mathcal{T}' \xleftarrow{\$} Trace^D(pk, tk, \varepsilon, \mathcal{I}_{sk}, \mathcal{I}_{pd}, \left\{sk_i\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{sk} \cup \mathcal{I}_{pd}})$        | $(k,c) \leftarrow Enc(pk;r_{Enc})$                                                          |  |  |
| $\mathbf{return} \ (pk, D, \mathcal{T}, \mathcal{T}', \mathcal{I}_{sk}, \mathcal{I}_{pd}, \left\{sk_i\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{sk} \cup \mathcal{I}_{pd}})$             | $\begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{return} \ d_i \leftarrow PDec(sk_i, c) \end{bmatrix}$              |  |  |

Figure 3: Traceability game for TT-KEM. Changes accommodating insider attacks are marked in gray. Note that, optionally, the adversary has access to the  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$  oracle. If the assertion fails, the game sets D to the Dummy decoder that always outputs 0.

<span id="page-16-0"></span>**Definition 4.1** ( $\tau$ -Traceability against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -Insiders with Limit  $\varepsilon$ ). Let  $\varepsilon(\lambda) \in (0, 1/2]$ ,  $\tau(\lambda)$ ,  $\sigma(\lambda)$ , and  $\rho(\lambda)$  be functions in the security parameter. A TT-KEM scheme is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders, if for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all  $n \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , all  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$ , all t with  $0 < t \le t(r)$  and  $\tau + \sigma + \rho \le t$ , and all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  such that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}] \ge \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \quad \text{ and } \quad \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}] \le \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where  $(\mathsf{pk}, D, \mathcal{T}, \mathcal{T}', \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}}) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Game-Tr}_{\mathcal{A}, \varepsilon, \sigma, \rho}(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t)$  (Fig. 3), and the three events  $\mathsf{GoodTr}$ ,  $\mathsf{BadTr}$  and  $\mathsf{GoodDec}$  are defined as follows:

- GoodTr occurs if  $|\mathcal{T}'| \geq \tau$  and  $\mathcal{T}' \subseteq \mathcal{T}$ , which indicates that at least  $\tau$  real traitors were traced and no non-traitors were framed by the tracing algorithm. In particular, this means that insiders may only be framed if they are originally corrupted by the adversary (i.e., if they are also in  $\mathcal{T}$ ).
- BadTr occurs if at least one non-traitor was framed in the tracing algorithm  $(\mathcal{T}' \neq \emptyset)$  and  $\mathcal{T}' \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}$ .
- GoodDec indicates that the decoder is good for pk, when given the secret keys of parties in  $\mathcal{I}_{sk}$  and partial decryptions on behalf of parties in  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ . More formally, when  $P(D) \geq 1/2 + \varepsilon(\lambda)$  where

$$P(D) \coloneqq \Pr \left[ D\Big(c, k_b, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}}, \{d_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}} \Big) = b: \quad \forall i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \colon d_i \leftarrow \mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i, c) \\ k_1 \xleftarrow{\$} \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}, b \xleftarrow{\$} \{0, 1\} \right].$$

In particular, P(Dummy) = 1/2, hence if the assertion in Game-Tr fails, then GoodDec = 0.

**Testing Decoders.** Throughout this paper, we frequently test decoders. As shorthand, we define the algorithm TestDec in Figure 4. This algorithm makes Q queries to a decoder D, supplying a KEM key k, ciphertext c, and secret keys and partial decryptions on behalf of full and partial insiders. It then counts the number of times that D correctly guesses whether k is embedded in c. It returns 1, if D guesses correctly in at least a fraction  $1/2 + \varepsilon$  of trials, indicating that, at least empirically, the decoder D has advantage  $\varepsilon$ .

**Insiders' Secret Keys are Required for Tracing.** Observe that we grant the tracer access to the full-insiders' secret keys during tracing, which we show to be *necessary* by an impossibility result in Section 4.1. We also grant the tracer access to the *partial insiders'* secret keys, thereby viewing *partial insiders as a relaxation of full insiders*. Under this choice, full-insider resilience implies partial-insider

{17}------------------------------------------------

```
TestDecD
        Π

            pk, Isk, Ipd, {ski}i∈Isk∪Ipd
                                       , Q, ε
Initialize ctr ← 0 and repeat Q times
  (k0, c) ←$ Π.Enc(pk); k1 ← K$

                             , c)

        0, 1}
  b
   ′ ←$ D

           kb, c, {ski}i∈Isk
                          , {di}i∈Ipd

  if b
      ′ = b then ctr ← ctr + 1
```

Figure 4: The TestDec algorithm to test a decoder *D*.

resilience (Theorem [5.3\)](#page-22-0). While one could instead require the tracer to simulate partial decryptions for partial insiders without their secret keys, it is unclear whether such a model preserves this implication, since we would be removing information from the tracer (i.e., the partial insiders' secret keys) that may have been crucial for tracing to succeed with full insiders in the first place.
