**Proving Corollary 2.6.** To arrive at Corollary 2.6, we apply multi-instance expansion (Theorem 2.5) to a 1-partition scheme (Theorem 2.2). It remains to show that the resulting scheme is robust.

Consider instantiating the 1-partition compiler scheme across K bins. Let  $\Pi$  be the resulting scheme.

**Lemma 7.2** For any constant  $c \in (0, 1/2)$ , let  $\ell = 2(1-c)K/c^2$ , let  $\Pi'$  be a multi-instance expansion (Construction 4) of  $\Pi$  across  $\ell = 2(1-c)K/c^2$  partitions. Then,  $\Pi'$  is (n/2-cn)-robust.

*Proof.* An adversary can break robustness only by causing decryption to fail in all  $\ell$  partitions. This requires the adversary to identify a set  $\mathsf{Cor} \subseteq [n]$  with  $|\mathsf{Cor}| \le n/2 - cn$  such that  $\mathsf{Cor}$  is  $(\ell, \mathbf{r})$ -blocking where  $\mathbf{r} = (n/K - t_{\mathsf{bin}}, \dots, n/K - t_{\mathsf{bin}})$ . By Lemma 6.2, the probability that such a set exists is negligible. This completes the proof.

# <span id="page-31-0"></span>8 On $\Theta(t)$ -Traceability

In this section, we prove Theorem 2.7. We start by presenting the following construction, which can be viewed as compiler that reduces the ciphertext from any t-traceable threshold KEM.

Construction 5. Let  $\mathcal{K} = \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ ,  $\Pi_{t-tr}$  be a perfect t-traceable threshold KEM, and TE any perfect (non-traceable) threshold KEM. The construction is parameterized by  $\gamma \in (0,1)$ .

 $\Pi'$ .KGen $(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$ : Set  $K := \sqrt{n}$  and sample a uniformly random (K, n)-balanced partition  $P = \{B_1, \ldots, B_K\}$  of [n]. Let  $n_{\mathsf{bin}} := n/K = \sqrt{n}$  and  $t_{\mathsf{bin}} := n_{\mathsf{bin}}/2 - \sqrt{\lambda n'}$ . Let  $\varepsilon' = 1/2 - \frac{0.5 - \varepsilon}{\gamma}$  For each  $j \in [K]$ , run

$$(\mathsf{pk}_1^{(j)}, \{\mathsf{sk}_{1,i}\}_{i \in B_j}, \mathsf{tk}_j) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}.\mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, |B_j|, t_{\mathsf{bin}}, \varepsilon'/4),$$
 
$$(\mathsf{pk}_2^{(j)}, \{\mathsf{sk}_{2,i}\}_{i \in B_j}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, |B_j|, t_{\mathsf{bin}}).$$

{32}------------------------------------------------

- Π′ *.*Enc(pk)**:** Sample *u* ←\$ [*K*]. For *j* ≠ *u*, compute (*k<sup>j</sup> , c<sup>j</sup>* ) ←\$ TE*.*Enc(pk(*j*) 2 ); for *j* = *u*, compute (*ku, cu*) ←\$ Πt-tr*.*Enc(pk(*u*) 1 ). Set *k* := L *j k<sup>j</sup>* and *c* := (*c*1*, . . . , cK*). Return (*k,*(*c, u*)).
- Π′ *.*PDec(sk*<sup>i</sup> ,*(*c, u*))**:** Parse sk*<sup>i</sup>* as (sk1*,i,*sk2*,i*). If *i* ∈ *Bu*, output Πt-tr*.*PDec(sk1*,i, cu*); otherwise output TE*.*PDec(sk2*,i, c<sup>j</sup>* ) where *j* = bin(*P, i*).
- Π′ *.*Combine(*S,*(*c, u*)*,* {*di*}*i*∈*S*)**:** Verify |*S* ∩ *B<sup>j</sup>* | ≥ *t*bin for all *j* ∈ [*K*]. For *j* = *u*, compute *k<sup>u</sup>* ← Πt-tr*.*Combine(*S* ∩ *Bu,* {*di*}*i*∈*S*∩*B<sup>u</sup>* ); for *j* ̸= *u*, compute *k<sup>j</sup>* ← TE*.*Combine(*S* ∩ *B<sup>j</sup> ,* {*di*}*i*∈*S*∩*B<sup>j</sup>* ). Output *k* := L *j k<sup>j</sup>* .
- Π′ *.*Trace*D*(·) (pk*,*tk*, ε*)**:** Let *ε* ′ = 1*/*2 − 0*.*5−*ε γ* . Given black-box access to a decoder *D* we do the following:
  - Let *Q* = 8*λ/ε*′<sup>2</sup> and initialize a counter *ctr* ← 0.
  - For every *u* ∈ [*K*]:
    - **–** Do *Q* times: Sample (*k*0*,*(*c, u*)) ←\$ Π′ *.*Enc(pk; *u*) and *k*<sup>1</sup> ← K \$ as well as a random bit *b*. That is, the random position is fixed to *u*.
    - **–** Set test*<sup>u</sup>* = 1 if *ctr* ≥ *Q* · (1*/*2 + *ε* ′*/*2) and test*<sup>u</sup>* = 0 otherwise.
  - Otherwise, construct the decoder *D<sup>u</sup>* as follows: On input (*ku, cu*), where *k<sup>u</sup>* is a KEM key and *c<sup>u</sup>* is a ciphertext in Πt-tr, compute for all *j* ∈ [*K*] \ {*u*}:

$$(k_j, c_j) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_2^{(j)}, B_j, t_{\mathsf{bin}}).$$

Compute *k* ← L *j*∈[*K*] *k<sup>j</sup>* and return *D*(*k,*((*c*1*, . . . , cu, . . . , cK*)*, u*)) as *Du*'s output.

Finally, for all *j* ∈ [*K*], where test*<sup>j</sup>* = 1, run T*<sup>j</sup>* ←\$ Π*.*Trace*D<sup>j</sup>* (·) (pk*<sup>j</sup> ,*tk*<sup>j</sup> , ε*′*/*4). For all *j* ∈ [*K*] where test*<sup>j</sup>* = 0, set T*<sup>j</sup>* ← ∅. Output T ← S *j*∈[*K*] T*<sup>j</sup>* .

<span id="page-32-0"></span>**Theorem 8.1** *Let ε, ε*′ ∈ (0*,* 0*.*5) *such that ε* ′ *< ε and γ* = 0*.*5−*ε* 0*.*5−*ε* ′ *. If* Πt-tr *is t*bin*-traceable with limit ε* ′*/*4 *then* Π′ *is* (1 − *γ*)*Kt*bin*-traceable with limit ε.*

**The Proof of Theorem [8.1.](#page-32-0)** The proof follows the argument of Theorem [6.4,](#page-25-1) with one key difference: we can no longer guarantee the existence of a decoder *D<sup>i</sup>* for every bin *i*. Nevertheless, Lemma [8.2](#page-32-1) shows that decoders can be extracted for at least (1 − *γ*)*K* of the bins. The theorem then follows by demonstrating that tracing succeeds for each of these decoders, using an argument similar to that in Theorem [6.4.](#page-25-1) The full proof is presented in Appendix [C.2.](#page-75-0)

Before discussing Lemma [8.2,](#page-32-1) we introduce the following definition.

We say that *D* is *ε* ′ -good for poisition *u* if it holds that:

$$P_{\Pi',u} := \Pr[D(k^b, ((c_1,\ldots,c_K),u)) = b] \ge \frac{1}{2} + \varepsilon',$$

where (*ku, cu*) ←\$ Πt-tr*.*Enc(pk(*u*) 1 *, Bu*), (*k<sup>j</sup> , c<sup>j</sup>* ) ←\$ TE*.*Enc(pk(*j*) 2 *, B<sup>j</sup>* ) for all *j* ∈ [*K*] \ {*u*}, *k* <sup>0</sup> ←\$ L*<sup>K</sup> <sup>j</sup>*=1 *k<sup>j</sup>* , *k* <sup>1</sup> ← K\$ , and *b* ← { \$ 0*,* 1}.

<span id="page-32-1"></span>**Lemma 8.2** *Suppose that D is ε-good and let γ* = 0*.*5−*ε* 0*.*5−*ε* ′ *. Then, for any ε* ′ *< ε it holds that D is ε* ′ *-good for at least* (1 − *γ*) · *K positions j* ∈ [*K*]*.*

*Proof of Lemma [8.2.](#page-32-1)* We first note that if *D* is *ε*-good, then it must hold that

$$P_{\Pi'}(D) = \Pr \Big[ D(k^b,c) = b \colon (k^0,c) \xleftarrow{\$} \Pi'.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}), \ k^1 \xleftarrow{\$} \mathcal{K}, \ b \xleftarrow{\$} \{0,1\} \Big] \ge \frac{1}{2} + \varepsilon.$$

Assume towards contradaction that *D* is *ε* ′ -good for less than (1 − *γ*) · *K* indices, then it holds that:

$$P_{\Pi'}(D) < (1-\gamma) \cdot 1 + \gamma \cdot (\frac{1}{2} + \varepsilon') = 1 - \gamma(\frac{1}{2} - \varepsilon') = 1 - \frac{0.5 - \varepsilon}{0.5 - \varepsilon'}(0.5 - \varepsilon') = \frac{1}{2} + \varepsilon.$$

This contradicts the assumption that *P*Π′ (*D*) ≥ 1 <sup>2</sup> + *ε*. 

{33}------------------------------------------------

**Putting it all together.** By Lemma 8.2, choosing  $\varepsilon = 1/4$  and  $\varepsilon' = 1/24$  ensures that D is  $\varepsilon'$ -good for at least  $\frac{5}{11} \cdot K$  of the bins. Instantiating Theorem 8.1 with the t-traceable threshold KEM that has O(n) ciphertexts (see Appendix D) and setting  $K = \sqrt{n}$  yields a 5n/11-traceable threshold KEM with ciphertext size  $O(\sqrt{n})$  and  $O(\sqrt{n})$ -robustness. This scheme has a limit  $\varepsilon$ . We remove this limt by applying the generalized limit elimination compiler from Appendix B, thereby proving Theorem 2.7.

# <span id="page-33-0"></span>9 On $\Theta(n^a)$ -Traceability for $a \in (0,1)$

In this section, we prove Theorem 2.8. That is, we show a compiler to achieve  $\Theta(n^a)$ -traceability. The compiler is depicted in Construction 6, whose properties are summarized in the following theorem.

<span id="page-33-3"></span>**Theorem 9.1** Let  $a \in (0,1)$  and  $c_1, c_2 \in (0,1/2)$ . Further, let  $\delta_1 := 6n^{-a/2}$ ,  $\delta_2 := 6\sqrt{n}/(n-\sqrt{n})$ ,  $t_1 := (1-\delta_1)n^a/2$ , and  $t_2 := (1-\delta_2)(n-n^a)/2$ . Let

$$\ell \coloneqq \max \left\{ 3\lambda n^{1-a}, \frac{10n^{1-a}(1-c_1)}{c_1^2}, \frac{20n^{1-a}(2-3c_2)}{c_2^2} \right\}, \qquad t_{\mathsf{parts}} \coloneqq 0.8\ell.$$

Let  $\Pi$  be a perfect limitless 1-traceable TT-KEM that offers a  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}$  oracle and let  $\mathsf{TE}$  a perfect T-KEM. Then,  $\Pi'$  (Construction 6) is probabilistically correct, semantically secure,  $(n/2-c_1n)$ -robust, and  $(0.3n^{1-a})$ -traceable for reconstruction threshold r=n/2 and security threshold  $t=t_1+t_2=n/2-o(n)$ . If  $\Pi$  is resilient against  $(t_1-1)$ -partial insiders and offers a  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$  oracle, then  $\Pi'$  is resilient against  $(n/2-c_2n)$  partial insiders and offers a  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$  oracle.

Before we introduce our unbalanced construction, we state a lemma that will be useful in proving its correctness, robustness, and traceability. A proof of the following Lemma can be found in Appendix A.3.

<span id="page-33-2"></span>**Lemma 9.2** Let  $P_j = \{B_1^j, B_2^j\}$  for  $j \in [\ell]$  be a collection of  $\ell$  independently and uniformly sampled (2, n)-unbalanced partitions, where for  $a \in (0, 1)$ ,  $|B_1^j| = n^a$  and  $|B_2^j| = n - n^a$  for every  $j \in [\ell]$ . Let  $\delta_1 = 6n^{-a/2}$ ,  $\delta_2 = 6\frac{\sqrt{n}}{n-n^a}$ ,  $t_1 = (1-\delta_1)\frac{n^a}{2}$ ,  $t_2 = (1-\delta_2)\frac{(n-n^a)}{2}$ . Then,

- 1. If  $\ell \geq 6\lambda$ , for uniformly distributed  $S \subseteq [n]$  where  $|S| \geq n/2$ , it holds that S  $(0.8\ell, (t_1, t_2))$ -dominates the partitions  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  with overwhelming probability.
- 2. Let  $c \in (0,0.5)$ ,  $\ell \geq 10n^{1-a}(1-c)/(c^2)$ ,  $\mathbf{r}_1 = (n^a t_1,0)$  and  $\beta = 0.2\ell$ . Then, the probability that there exists a subset  $S \subseteq [n]$ , such that  $|S| \leq n/2 cn$  and  $(\beta, \mathbf{r}_1)$ -blocks  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  is negligible in  $\lambda$ .
- 3. Let  $c \in (0,0.5)$ ,  $\ell \geq 20n^{1-a}(2-3c)/c^2$ ,  $\mathbf{r}_2 = (t_1,0)$ , and  $\beta = 0.2\ell$ . Then, the probability that there exists a subset  $S \subseteq [n]$ , such that  $|S| \leq n/2 cn$  and  $(0.2\ell, \mathbf{r}_2)$ -dominates  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  is negligible in  $\lambda$ .
- 4. If  $\ell \geq 3n^{1-a}\lambda$ , then the probability that there exists a  $u \in [n]$  such that  $u \in B_1^j$  for more than  $2\ell/n^{1-a}$  indices j is negligible.

<span id="page-33-1"></span>Construction 6. Let  $\ell \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  and  $0 < t_{\mathsf{parts}} \le \ell$ . Let  $\Pi$  be a perfect 1-traceable TT-KEM against  $\rho$  partial insiders. Let TE be a perfect threshold KEM. Let the key-space of both  $\Pi$  and TE be  $\{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . We construct  $\Pi'$ , parameterized by a constant  $a \in (0,1)$ , and functions  $\delta_1(\lambda,n,a)$  and  $\delta_2(\lambda,n,a)$ .

 $\Pi'.\mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, r \coloneqq n/2, t, \varepsilon)$ : For key generation, we sample  $\ell$  random (2, n)-unbalanced partitions of [n] parties  $\{P_1, \ldots, P_\ell\}$  such that for all partitions  $j \in [\ell]$  it holds that  $P_j = \{B_1^{(j)}, B_2^{(j)}\}$ , where  $|B_1^{(j)}| = n^a$  and  $|B_2^{(j)}| = n - n^a$ . Let  $t_1 \coloneqq \frac{n^a}{2} \cdot (1 - \delta_1)$  and  $t_2 \coloneqq \frac{n - n^a}{2} \cdot (1 - \delta_2)$  and assert that  $t \le t_1 + t_2$ . Let  $\varepsilon' = \varepsilon/16\ell$ . In each partition, run key generation of  $\Pi$  for parties in  $B_1^{(j)}$  with threshold  $t_1$  and in  $B_2^{(j)}$  we run key generation of  $\Pi$  with threshold  $t_2$ :

$$\forall j \in [\ell] \colon (\Pi.\mathsf{pk}^{(j)}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)}\}_{i \in B_*^{(j)}}, \mathsf{tk}^{(j)}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, B_1^{(j)}, t_1, t_1, \varepsilon')$$

$$\forall j \in [\ell] \colon (\mathsf{TE.pk}^{(j)}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)}\}_{i \in B_2^{(j)}}) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{TE.KGen}(1^\lambda, B_2^{(j)}, t_2, t_2)$$

$$\text{Set } \mathsf{pk} \coloneqq \{(\Pi.\mathsf{pk}^{(j)}, \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{pk}^{(j)})\}_{j \in [\ell]}, \mathsf{sk}_i \coloneqq \{\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)}\}_{j \in [\ell]}, \text{ and } \mathsf{tk} \coloneqq \{\mathsf{tk}^{(j)}\}_{j \in [\ell]}. \text{ Output } (\mathsf{pk}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{tk}).$$

{34}------------------------------------------------

- Π'.Enc(pk): Sample KEM key  $k \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}$  and share  $(s_1, \ldots, s_\ell) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Share}(k, \ell, t_{\mathsf{parts}})$ . Next, for all  $j \in [\ell]$ , do the following:
  - Generate  $(c_j^{\Pi}, k_j^{\Pi}) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\Pi.\mathsf{pk}^{(j)})$  and  $(c_j^{\mathsf{TE}}, k_j^{\mathsf{TE}}) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{pk}^{(j)})$  and assemble  $c_j \coloneqq (c_j^{\Pi}, c_j^{\mathsf{TE}})$ .
  - Compute the mask  $u_j \leftarrow s_j \oplus k_i^{\Pi} \oplus k_i^{\mathsf{TE}}$ .

Finally, assemble and output  $(k, c := \{(c_j, u_j)\}_{j \in [\ell]})$ .

- $\Pi'.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i,c) \colon \text{For all } j \in [\ell], \text{ do the following: If } i \in B_1^{(j)}, \text{ then compute } d_i^{(j)} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i,c_j^\Pi), \text{ else, compute } d_i^{(j)} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i,c_j^{\mathsf{TE}}). \text{ Finally, assemble and output } d_i \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \{d_i^{(j)}\}_{j \in [\ell]}.$
- Π'.Combine(pk, S,  $\{d_i\}_{i \in S}$ , c): Initialize  $M = \emptyset$ . For all  $j \in [\ell]$ , let  $S_1^{(j)} = S \cap B_1^{(j)}$  and  $S_2^{(j)} = S \cap B_2^{(j)}$  and do the following:
  - If  $|S_1^{(j)}| < t_1$  or  $|S_2^{(j)}| < t_2$ , continue with the next index.
  - Otherwise, compute

$$k_j^\Pi \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{Combine}\Big(S_1^{(j)}, \{d_i^{(j)}\}_{i \in S_1^{(j)}}, c_j^\Pi\Big) \ \text{ and } k_j^\mathsf{TE} \leftarrow \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Combine}\Big(S_2^{(j)}, \{d_i^{(j)}\}_{i \in S_2^{(j)}}, c_j^\mathsf{TE}\Big) \,.$$

- Undo the mask  $s_j \leftarrow u_j \oplus k_j^{\Pi} \oplus k_j^{\mathsf{TE}}$ .
- Insert  $M \leftarrow M \cup \{j\}$ .

Finally, if  $|M| \ge t_{\mathsf{parts}}$ , reconstruct and output  $k \leftarrow \mathsf{Rec}(t_{\mathsf{parts}}, M, \{s_j\}_{j \in M})$ . Return  $\bot$  otherwise.  $\Pi'.\mathsf{Trace}^{D(\cdot)}(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{tk}, \varepsilon, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}})$ : See Figure 5 (and explained in Section 9.1).

Parameter Sizes.  $\Pi'$  has ciphertext size  $C'(n) = \ell \cdot (C_{\Pi}(n^a) + C_{\mathsf{TE}}(n - n^a))$ , public key size  $P'(n) = \ell \cdot (P_{\Pi}(n^a) + P_{\mathsf{TE}}(n - n^a))$ , and secret key size  $S'(n) = \ell \cdot \max(S_{\Pi}(n^a), S_{\mathsf{TE}}(n - n^a))$ .  $\Pi'$  has key space  $\mathcal{K}_{\lambda} = \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$ .

Correctness and Security. For correctness, we show in Lemma 9.2 that, using a Chernoff bound, when  $\ell = \Omega(\lambda)$ , a random subset S with  $|S| \geq n/2$   $(t_1, t_2)$ -dominates (Definition 3.5) at least  $0.8\ell$  of the partitions except with negligible probability, implying correctness. Semantic security follows straightforwardly from that of the underlying schemes. The formal proof is deferred to Appendix C.3.

<span id="page-34-2"></span>**Lemma 9.3** (Robustness of Construction 6). If  $\Pi$  and TE are perfectly robust, construction  $\Pi'$  is  $(n/2-c_1n)$ -robust for arbitrary  $c_1 \in (0,1/2)$ .

*Proof.* An adversary can only break robustness by causing decryption to fail in at least  $\ell - t_{\mathsf{parts}} = 0.2\ell$  partitions. This requires the adversary to find a set  $\mathsf{Cor} \subseteq [n]$  with  $|\mathsf{Cor}| \le n/2 - c_1 n$  such that  $\mathsf{Cor}(0.2\ell, \mathbf{r})$ -blocks the set  $\{P_i\}_{i \in [\ell]}$  for  $\mathbf{r} = (n^a - t_1, 0)^9$ .

We show via a probabilistic argument that, for an appropriate choice of  $\ell$  (specified in Theorem 9.1), the probability that such a set exists is negligible. Suppose for now the adversary fixes Cor ahead of time, then the expected number of parties from Cor that fall into the first bin of a given partition is  $(\frac{1}{2} - c_1)n^a$ . For Cor to block the first bin, it must cover more than  $n^a/2$  parties in that bin, which requires a deviation of  $\Theta(c_1n^a)$  from the expectation. By a standard Chernoff bound, the probability of such a deviation for a fixed partition is  $O(\exp(-n^a))$ . To account for an adversary who chooses Cor after seeing the partitions, we apply a union bound over all possible choices of Cor to show that the probability that there exists a set Cor for which this event occurs in at least  $0.2\ell$  partitions is negligible. Detailed probability analysis is provided in Part 2 of Lemma 9.2.

#### <span id="page-34-0"></span>9.1 The Tracing Algorithm

The tracing algorithm is depicted in Figure 5. We next give an overview.

Let us fix  $t_{\mathsf{parts}} = 0.8\ell$ , i.e., during encryption, we share the KEM key across partitions in a  $0.8\ell$ -out-of- $\ell$  manner. Thus, intuitively, any successful decoder must decrypt in at least  $0.8\ell$  of the partitions, which will allow us to identify one traitor from the small bin of each such partition. While these traitors may

<span id="page-34-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>9</sup>We consider only the first bin, since preventing decryption from the second bin requires  $O(n-n^a)$  parties, which, for sufficient large n, is larger than n/2-cn for any constant c.

{35}------------------------------------------------

not all be distinct, we later use a counting argument to bound the maximum number of duplicate traitors to achieve  $\tau'$  unique traitors found as promised in Theorem 9.1. We elaborate on this in the proof later, and focus here on how to find a traitor from sufficiently many partitions.

**Notation.** For each step i, let  $\mathsf{Ord}_i = (v_{i,1}, v_{i,2}, \dots, v_{i,\ell})$  be an ordering over the partition indices such that  $v_{i,j} \in [\ell]$ , and  $v_{i,j} \neq v_{i,q}$  for all  $j \neq q$ . For all  $x \in [\ell]$ , we define access to the x-th element  $\mathsf{Ord}_i[x] = v_{i,x}$ , and the prefix  $\mathsf{Ord}_i[\leq x] = (v_{i,1}, v_{i,2}, \dots, v_{i,x})$ , and analogous notation for operators  $<, \geq, >$ .

We proceed in up to  $0.8\ell$  steps. In each step i, we run the subroutine TrStep, which outputs a set  $\mathcal{T}'_i$  of traitors found in step i, and bookkeeping information  $\mathsf{Found}_i$ , which denotes the  $\mathit{first}$  partition, from which a traitor was found.

**Tracing in the** *i***-th Step.** On a high level, in TrStep we apply a PLBE-style tracing procedure similar to the multi-instance expansion compiler (Theorem 2.5), but with two crucial modifications: (1) we carefully control the *order in which partitions are revoked*, and (2) we make use of the fact that the key is shared in a  $0.8\ell$ -out-of- $\ell$  manner instead of 1-out-of- $\ell$ .

In step i, we revoke partitions in the order fixed in  $\operatorname{Ord}_i$ . This is achieved by designing decoders in a manner similar to multi-instance expansion tracing (see Theorem 2.5). That is, we construct decoders  $D_j$  for  $j \in [\ell]$  by revoking all partitions  $\operatorname{Ord}_i[< j]$ . Informally, to revoke a partition  $q \in \operatorname{Ord}_i[< j]$ , we replace the  $u_q$  element of the ciphertext, which is supposed to mask the partition  $P_q$ 's share of the KEM key k, with a uniform random value. Thus, decrypting in partition  $P_q$  can no longer contribute to decrypting the overall ciphertext.

Due to threshold secret sharing the KEM key k, the PLBE argument guarantees the existence of a good decoder among the first  $0.2\ell+1$  partitions, since decryption advantage drops to 0 already after revoking  $0.2\ell+1$  partitions. Thus, we will find 1 traitor from the first  $0.2\ell+1$  partitions, i.e., Found<sub>i</sub>  $\in$  Ord<sub>i</sub>[ $0.2\ell+1$ ]. In the tracing algorithm, we denote this cut-off position as Cut  $\leftarrow 0.2\ell+1$ .

To ensure that we find a traitor from a different partition in step i+1, we let  $\mathsf{Ord}_{i+1}$  be the order after swapping the partition  $\mathsf{Found}_i$  where a traitor was found to the trailing  $0.8\ell-1$  segment of the ordering.

This procedure is repeated for a total of  $0.8\ell$  steps, such that we find a traitor from the first  $0.2\ell+1$  partitions in every step. By the end, all partitions where a traitor was found will occupy the trailing  $0.8\ell$  positions in the order of partitions.

**Determining** Ord<sub>1</sub> and Dealing with Partial Insiders. Next, we aim to leverage the fact that the underlying scheme  $\Pi$  is already resilient against  $t_1-1$  partial insiders. Then, partitions that are  $t_1$ -dominated by partial insiders cannot be traced in TrStep. We denote such partitions as  $\overline{\mathcal{P}} = \{j \in [\ell]: |B_1^{(j)} \cap \mathcal{I}_{pd}| \geq t_1\}$ . To ensure that we never trace decoders for partitions  $\overline{\mathcal{P}}$  in TrStep, we set up Ord<sub>1</sub> such that  $\overline{\mathcal{P}}$  occupies the trailing positions, i.e.,  $\overline{\mathcal{P}}$  is a suffix of Ord<sub>1</sub>. This is done in the Initialization phase of  $\Pi'$ .Trace. As a result, tracing now proceeds in  $d = 0.8\ell - |\overline{\mathcal{P}}|$  steps, as  $|\overline{\mathcal{P}}|$  of the trailing positions are already occupied.

Requiring an  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}$  Oracle. In one part of the traceability proof for Construction 6 (in particular, Lemma 9.6), we will argue that in each step i of the tracing algorithm, the tracer finds a partition  $\mathsf{Found}_i \neq \bot$  and that the traitors identified from  $\mathsf{Found}_i$  must be real traitors. To prove this, we execute a hybrid argument over the different steps. The reduction in the hybrid argument must initially guess a step  $i^*$  and a partition  $j^*$  and embeds its instance of  $\Pi$  into partition  $j^*$ . The issue is that, in order to simulate up to step  $i^*$ , the reduction must simulate execution of all steps  $i < i^*$  to come up with  $\mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}$ . If, however, it holds that  $j^* \in \mathsf{Ord}_i[\le \mathsf{Cut}]$  for any step  $i < i^*$ , then simulating that step requires the reduction to run tracing on the challenge partition  $j^*$ . This is clearly problematic whenever  $\Pi$  is not a public tracing scheme. However, we make the following observation, which allows us to instantiate even with private tracing schemes, should they offer a certain additional property:

Observe that, in order to simulate up to Step  $i^*$ , the reduction does not actually need to learn  $\mathcal{T}'_i(j^*)$  in full. It only needs to decide whether to shuffle partition  $j^*$  to the back in the subsequent step, i.e., whether  $j^* = \mathsf{Found}_i$ . To do so, the reduction does not require the full  $\mathcal{T}'_i(j^*)$ , but merely requires to learn the predicate, whether  $\mathcal{T}'_i(j^*) = \emptyset$ . This is a very relevant distinction, because in some private-tracing

Figure 5: The tracing algorithm of Construction 6. In Init, we test the decoder for the same reason as in the 1-partition compiler (Section 2.4). TestDec is defined in Figure 4.

<span id="page-36-1"></span>schemes, in particular in fingerprinting-based schemes, any party can check whether tracing a given decoder would yield an empty set or not  $without\ access\ to\ the\ tracing\ key\ tk.$ 

$$\boxed{ \frac{\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}(\widetilde{D},\widetilde{\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}})}{\mathbf{if} \; \mathsf{Trace}^{\widetilde{D}}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{tk},\varepsilon,\widetilde{\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}},\left\{\mathsf{sk}_{i}\right\}_{i\in\widetilde{\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}}) = \emptyset \; \mathbf{return} \; 1 \; \mathbf{else} \; \mathbf{return} \; 0} }$$

Figure 6: The  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}$  oracle that returns 1, if tracing a decoder D returns an empty set and 0 otherwise.

We model this property by granting the adversary in Game-Tr (Figure 3) access to the  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}$  oracle introduced in Figure 6. To prove traceability of Construction 6, we require additionally, that  $\Pi$  offers  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}$ . We demonstrate, at the example of our scheme with *native* partial insider resilience in Section 10, how fingerprinting-based schemes can provide such an oracle.

Remark 9.4 In the above compiler, if the decoder D has decryption advantage  $\varepsilon$ , we trace  $D_j^{(i)}$  using  $\varepsilon' = \varepsilon/16\ell$  in each step. Since  $\ell$  is not a constant, this necessitates that the base scheme  $\Pi$  be limitless, meaning that it can trace decoders with arbitrarily small but noticeable decryption advantage. To ensure this property, we generalize the limit-elimination compiler of [51, 28] to the insider setting (Theorem B.2). This limit-elimination compiler should be applied to  $\Pi$  prior to our compiler. We emphasize that it incurs only a constant ciphertext overhead, independent of the number of parties n.

{37}------------------------------------------------

Proof of Traceability (Theorem 9.1). Our proof comprises 4 major parts.

Part 1: At Most  $0.2\ell$  Partitions are Dominated by Partial Insiders. We show that if the adversary specifies at most  $\rho' = n/2 - c_2 n$  partial insiders, then at most  $0.2\ell$  partitions can be  $t_1$ -dominated in the first bin by partial insiders. As a result, there must be at least  $d \ge 0.6\ell$  steps in tracing.

<span id="page-37-1"></span>**Lemma 9.5** If  $|\mathcal{I}_{pd}| \leq \rho'$ , then  $|\overline{\mathcal{P}}| \leq 0.2\ell$  except with negligible probability.

Proof of Lemma 9.5. During key generation, Construction 6 independently samples a collection of  $\ell$  uniform random  $(2,\ell)$ -unbalanced partitions, such that  $|B_1^{(j)}| = n^a$  for all  $j \in [\ell]$ . We show via a probabilistic argument that, for an appropriate choice of  $\ell$  (specified in Theorem 9.1), the probability that there exists a set of size at most  $\rho'$  that  $(0.2\ell, (t_1, 0))$ -dominates  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  is negligible. To see this, note that the expected number of parties from an a priori fixed set of size  $\rho'$  in the first bin of a given partition is  $(\frac{1}{2} - c_2)n^a$ . Thus, to dominate a partition,  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$  must deviate from this expectation by  $\Theta(c_2n^a)$ . The probability that this deviation occurs in  $\geq 0.2\ell$  partitions is  $\exp(-\Theta(0.2\ell c_2n^a))$ . The argument then proceeds as in the proof of Lemma 9.3: To account for adaptive choice of  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ , we apply a union bound over all possible choices of  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ , and conclude that such  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$  exist only with negligible probability. The full probabilistic argument is provided in Part 3 of Lemma 9.2.

Part 2: Tracing Succeeds in Every Step. We show that in each step  $i \in [d]$ , a partition from  $\operatorname{Ord}_i[\leq \operatorname{Cut}]$  is found, and that honest parties cannot be framed in any step. To formalize this intuition, we first define what it means that tracing  $\operatorname{succeeds}$  in a step  $i \in [d]$ . Recall that  $\mathcal{T}'_i(j)$  denotes the traitors found from partition j in step i. Denote with  $\operatorname{GoodStep}_i$  for  $i \in [d]$  the following event:

$$\mathsf{GoodStep}_i = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } \mathsf{Found}_i \neq \bot \text{ and } \mathcal{T}_i'(\mathsf{Found}_i) \subseteq \mathcal{T} \cap B_1^{(\mathsf{Found}_i)} \\ 0 & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

Denote with GoodSteps the event that  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_i = 1$  for all  $i \in [d]$ . Also, denote with  $\mathsf{BadStep}_i$  the event that any non-traitor is framed as traitor in step i, and with  $\mathsf{BadSteps}$  the event that there exists an  $i \in [d]$  such that  $\mathsf{BadStep}_i = 1$ .

<span id="page-37-0"></span>**Lemma 9.6** Let  $\Pi$  be a 1-traceable threshold KEM against  $\rho$  partial insiders. For all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  against Game-Tr (Figure 3) of  $\Pi'$  there exists a negligible function negl such that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ \ and \ \ \Pr[\mathsf{BadSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Lemma 9.6 does the heavy-lifting in the traceability proof, so, for better clarity, we defer its proof to Section 9.2 and continue with the proof outline for now.

Part 3:  $\tau'$  unique traitors are found. We show that if we find at least one traitor from the first bin of  $0.6\ell$  partitions, then  $\tau'$  of the traitors found must be distinct.

<span id="page-37-2"></span>**Lemma 9.7** If traitors are identified in  $d \ge 0.6\ell$  steps, then except with negligible probability it holds that  $|\mathcal{T}'| \ge \tau' = 0.3n^{1-a}$ .

*Proof of Lemma 9.7.* Suppose traitors are found in at least  $0.6\ell$  steps. Recall that after each step, the first partition in which a traitor is found is moved to the back and cannot contribute in subsequent steps. Therefore, traitors are found in the first bin of at least  $0.6\ell$  distinct partitions.

For any fixed party, the expected number of partitions in which it appears in the first bin is  $\ell/n^{1-a}$ . By a standard Chernoff bound and a union bound over all n parties, the probability that any party appears in the first bin of more than  $2\ell/n^{1-a}$  partitions is negligible in  $\lambda$  when  $\ell = \Theta(\lambda n^{1-a})$ . (See Part 4 of Lemma 9.2). Consequently, among the traitors identified from distinct partitions, at least  $\frac{0.6\ell}{2\ell/n^{1-a}} = 0.3n^{1-a}$  must be distinct.

{38}------------------------------------------------

**Part 4: Putting it all together.** Equipped with Lemma 9.6, we can easily conclude unframability of  $\Pi'$ , i.e.,  $\Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] = \Pr[\mathsf{BadSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

Next, we look at GoodTr. Following Lemma 9.5, it must hold that  $|\overline{\mathcal{P}}| \leq 0.2\ell$ , and thus tracing carries out at least  $d \geq t_{\mathsf{parts}} - 0.2\ell = 0.6\ell$  steps with overwhelming probability. Now suppose that GoodSteps happens, which implies that we found traitors in all  $d \geq 0.6\ell$  steps. We apply Lemma 9.7 to argue that in this case we must have found  $|\mathcal{T}'| \geq \tau' = 0.3n^{1-a}$  unique real traitors, which implies GoodTr = 1. Finally, we apply Lemma 9.6 and get that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}'(\lambda),$$

for negligible functions negl and negl'. This concludes the proof of Theorem 9.1.

### <span id="page-38-0"></span>9.2 A Proof of Lemma 9.6

Proof of Lemma 9.6. Let  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$  be the set of partial insiders output by  $\mathcal{A}$  and  $\overline{\mathcal{P}}$  defined as in the Trace algorithm. Assume w.l.o.g. that  $|\overline{\mathcal{P}}| < t_{\mathsf{parts}}$ , since otherwise the lemma holds trivially as tracing would carry out d=0 steps.

**Proof Outline.** Our proof roughly proceeds as follows: First, we construct a reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  to 1-traceability against  $(t_1 - 1)$  partial insiders of  $\Pi$ , and use  $\mathcal{B}$  to upper bound  $\Pr[\mathsf{BadSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}]$ .

Then, we show that  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  through a hybrid argument over the maximum number of steps  $t_{\mathsf{parts}}$  executed during tracing. In the hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_i$  for  $i \in [t_{\mathsf{parts}}]$ , we essentially set  $\mathsf{GoodSteps} \leftarrow \mathsf{test}$ , if there exists a step  $u \leq i$  such that  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_u = 0$ .

We prove  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{test}]$  to hold in the final hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_{t_{\mathsf{parts}}}$ . A probabilistic argument shows that test is implied by  $\mathsf{GoodDec}$  except with negligible probability, and thus  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  holds in  $\mathcal{H}_{t_{\mathsf{parts}}}$ .

To close the proof, it remains to show that we increased  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}]$  at most by a negligible amount between  $\mathcal{H}_0$  and  $\mathcal{H}_{t_{\mathsf{parts}}}$ . For this, we show that in subsequent hybrids, the increase in  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}]$  can be bound by the advantage of a reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  against traceability of  $\Pi$ . To do so, we first show by a PLBE-style argument that if D is good, then in every step i, at least one of the decoders  $D_j^{(i)}$  must be good, where  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_i[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$ . Similar to the reduction for the BadSteps part,  $\mathcal{B}$  initially guesses a step  $i^*$  and a partition  $j^*$  into which it embeds its challenge instance of  $\Pi$ . We modify  $\mathcal{B}$  to only ever output a good decoder whenever  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  is the first good decoder in  $\mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$ . This allows us to prove that  $\mathsf{Pr}[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}] \geq \mathsf{Pr}[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}]/\ell$ . Recall that dummy decoders cannot be traced except with negligible probability.  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs a dummy decoder whenever it guesses  $j^*$  incorrectly (i.e., not the first good decoder in step  $i^*$ ), and therefore  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  can only ever happen if  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses  $j^*$  correctly. In this case, it holds that  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  implies  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_i$ , and thus  $\mathsf{Pr}[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}] \leq \mathsf{Pr}[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}]/\ell$ . We can conclude that

$$\Pr[\neg \mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \land \mathsf{test}] \leq \ell \cdot (\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}]),$$

conditioned on the event that  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses  $i^* = i$ . This inequality bounds the increase in  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}]$  between  $\mathcal{H}_{i-1}$  and  $\mathcal{H}_i$  by  $\mathcal{B}$ 's advantage against traceability of  $\Pi$ .

**Proving Pr[BadSteps**<sub> $\mathcal{A}$ </sub>]  $\leq$  **negl**( $\lambda$ ). We will start our proof with the latter part of the lemma and show that for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  to 1-traceability against  $\rho$  partial insiders of  $\Pi$  such that  $\Pr[\mathsf{BadSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \ell \cdot t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}]$ .

Reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  to traceability of  $\Pi$ . The reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  initially samples a random guess for a step  $i^* \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} [t_{\mathsf{parts}}]$  and a partition  $j^* \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} [\ell]$ . The reduction runs against Game-Tr of  $\Pi$  and simulates Game-Tr of  $\Pi'$  to  $\mathcal{A}$  by embedding the challenge public key into the first bin of partition  $j^*$ , simulating all remaining partitions according to protocol. Denote with  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$  and  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$  the random variables corresponding to the corrupted parties of  $\mathcal{A}$  and  $\mathcal{B}$ . Note that this means that  $\mathcal{B}$  knows all secret keys and tracing keys except for partition  $j^*$ . In partition  $j^*$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  knows only the TE secret keys of parties in  $B_2$ . When  $\mathcal{A}$  issues a corruption query for party p and  $p \in B_1^{(j^*)}$ , then  $\mathcal{B}$  queries its own corruption oracle on p to learn  $\mathsf{sk}_p^{(j^*)}$  of  $\Pi$ . All remaining secret keys of party p (i.e., those of the threshold encryption scheme TE and the  $\Pi$  keys of party p in other partitions) are known to  $\mathcal{B}$  from the simulation. In particular, this means

{39}------------------------------------------------

that  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}} \cap B_1^{(j^*)} = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$ . To simulate  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec\text{-Hon}}}(i, r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  simply queries  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec\text{-Hon}}}$  of its own challenger, whenever  $i \in B_1^{(j^*)}$ .

When  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs a decoder D and a set of partial insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  needs to construct decoder  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  (Fig. 5). Since the construction of this decoder relies on the ordering  $\operatorname{Ord}_{i^*}$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates the tracing procedure up to step  $i^*$ . To do so,  $\mathcal{B}$  must first simulate all decoders  $D_j^{(i)}$  in each step  $i \leq i^*$  and then also trace them to obtain the ordering  $\operatorname{Ord}_{i+1}$  for the subsequent step. Denote for all  $j \in [\ell]$  with  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}^{(j)} = \mathcal{I}_{pd} \cap B_1^{(j)}$  the set of partial insiders in the first bin of partition j. To simulate decoders as constructed by  $\Pi'$ . Trace for  $j \neq j^*$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  must provide partial decryptions on behalf of  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}^{(j^*)}$  for honestly generated ciphertexts, which it can obtain through its challenger's  $\mathcal{O}^{\operatorname{PDec-Hon}}$  oracle. More formally, to simulate  $D_j^{(i)}$  for  $j \neq j^*$  given  $\operatorname{Ord}_i$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  does the following: To simulate  $D_j^{(i)}\left(k_j^\Pi, c_j^\Pi, \{d_i^{(j)}\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{pd}}^{(j)}}\right)$ ,

- Generate  $(c_q^\Pi, k_q^\Pi) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\Pi.\mathsf{pk}^{(q)})$  for all  $q \in [\ell] \setminus \{j, j^*\}$ . Compute  $d_i^{(q)} \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{(q)}, c_q^\Pi)$  for all  $q \in [\ell] \setminus \{j, j^*\}$  and  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \cap B_1^{(q)}$ .
- Sample  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j^*)} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  and compute  $c_{j^*}^{\Pi} \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\Pi.\mathsf{pk}^{(j^*)}; r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j^*)})$ . For all  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j^*)}$  obtain partial decryptions  $d_i \leftarrow \mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}(i, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j^*)})$  making use of the challenger's oracle.
- Generate  $(c_q^{\mathsf{TE}}, k_q^{\mathsf{TE}}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{TE.pk}^{(q)})$  for all  $q \in [\ell]$ . Compute  $d_i^{(q)} \leftarrow \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{(q)}, c_q^{\mathsf{TE}})$  for all  $q \in [\ell]$  and  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \cap B_2^{(q)}$ .
- Sample  $(k^0, k^1) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}^2$  as well as a random bit  $b \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}$ . Compute  $\{s_q\}_{q \in [\ell]} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Share}(\ell, t_{\mathsf{parts}}, k^0)$ .
- For  $q \in \mathsf{Ord}_i[< j]$ , sample  $u_q \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}$ , for  $q \in \mathsf{Ord}_i[\geq j]$  (in particular, for q = j), set  $u_q \leftarrow s_q \oplus k_q^\Pi \oplus k_q^{\mathsf{TE}}$ .
- Assemble  $c = \{((c_q^{\Pi}, c_q^{\mathsf{TE}}), u_q)\}_{q \in [\ell]}$  as well as  $d_i \leftarrow \{d_i^{(q)}\}_{q \in [\ell]}$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}$ . Derive the output of  $D_j^{(i)}$  as  $b \oplus D(k^b, c, \{d_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}})$ .

Next, consider the case that partition  $j^*$  is already in  $\operatorname{Ord}_i[\leq \operatorname{Cut}]$  for some  $i < i^*$ . In this case,  $\mathcal{B}$  cannot simulate tracing the decoder  $D_{j^*}^{(i)}$ , since  $\mathcal{B}$  does not know  $\operatorname{tk}_{j^*}$ . However,  $\mathcal{B}$  can query its challenger's oracle  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}(D_{j^*}^{(i)}, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j^*)})$  to learn whether tracing  $D_{j^*}^{(i)}$  would return an empty set or not, and thus update the ordering in step i accordingly. Having obtained  $\operatorname{Ord}_{i^*}$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  constructs  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  and returns it to its challenger alongside  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j^*)}$ . If  $i^* > d$  or  $j^* \notin \operatorname{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \operatorname{Cut}]$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  just outputs a dummy decoder.

**Analysis.** Whenever  $\mathcal{A}$  triggers BadSteps, there exists  $i \in [t_{\mathsf{parts}}]$  and  $j \in [\ell]$  such that tracing  $D_i^{(j)}$  outputs at least one non-traitor. Since  $\mathcal{A}$ 's probability of triggering BadSteps is independent of  $\mathcal{B}$ 's choice of  $j^*$  and  $i^*$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses i and j correctly with probability at least  $1/\ell t_{\mathsf{parts}}$ . If  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses correctly, then  $\mathcal{T}'_{\mathcal{B}} = \mathcal{T}'_{i^*}(j^*) \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}(j^*) = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$ , and thus  $\Pr[\mathsf{BadSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \ell \cdot t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}]$ .

**Proving Pr[GoodSteps**<sub> $\mathcal{A}$ </sub>]  $\geq$  **Pr[GoodDec**<sub> $\mathcal{A}$ </sub>] - **negl**( $\lambda$ ). Next, we show that for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  it holds that Pr[GoodSteps]  $\geq$  Pr[GoodDec] $_{\mathcal{A}}$ ] - negl( $\lambda$ ). We define a series of hybrids  $\mathcal{H}_i$  for  $i \in [0, t_{\mathsf{parts}}]$  such that  $\mathcal{H}_0$  is identical to the real game. In hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_i$ , however, we modify the definition of events as follows: If for any  $u \leq i$  it holds that  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_u = 0$ , then we overwrite  $\mathsf{GoodSteps} \leftarrow \mathsf{test}$ , i.e., if the adversary manages that in any step  $u \leq i$  either no partition is found or non-traitors are framed from the found partition, then we just set  $\mathsf{GoodSteps}$  to 1, iff the decoder D tests good ( $\mathsf{test} = 1$ ).

Denote with  $GoodSteps^{(i)}$  a random variable representing the event GoodSteps in hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_i$ . First, observe that in the last hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_{t_{parts}}$  it trivially holds that for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function negl such that

$$\Pr\Big[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(t_{\mathsf{parts}})}\Big] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

since, by a simple Hoeffding inequality, if a decoder is good, it will only test bad with negligible probability.<sup>10</sup> It remains to show that for all PPT adversaries there exists a negligible function negl such

<span id="page-39-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>10</sup>We proved an identical statement in Claim 6.6 using Hoeffding's inequality.

{40}------------------------------------------------

that

<span id="page-40-0"></span>
$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] \ge \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(t_{\mathsf{parts}})}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \tag{1}$$

Before we prove Equation 1, we must first argue that whenever D tests good (test = 1), then in every step  $i \in [d]$ , there exists a decoder  $D_j^{(i)}$  with  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_i[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$  that is at least  $\varepsilon/8\ell$ -good against the instance of  $\Pi$  in partition j.

<span id="page-40-1"></span>**Claim 9.8** If test = 1, then for all  $i \in [d]$  there must exist a  $j \in \text{Ord}_i \leq \text{Cut}$  such that

$$P_{\Pi_j}(D_j^{(i)}) \ge 1/2 + \varepsilon/8\ell,$$

except with negligible probability, where  $P_{\Pi_j}$  is with respect to instance  $\Pi_j$  and partial insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j)}$ .

*Proof of Claim 9.8.* Suppose test happens. By a simple Hoeffding inequality, we can show that if test happens, then D is at least  $\varepsilon/4$ -good with overwhelming probability.<sup>11</sup> We next apply a thresholdized PLBE-style argument.

Suppose that the decoder D is  $\varepsilon/4$ -good. Then by definition, it holds that

<span id="page-40-3"></span>Suppose that the decoder 
$$D$$
 is  $\varepsilon/4$ -good. Then by definition, it holds that 
$$b \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}, (k^0, k^1) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}^2$$

$$(s_1, \dots, s_\ell) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \text{Share}(k_0, \ell, t_{\mathsf{parts}})$$
For all  $j \in [\ell]$ :
$$(c_j^\Pi, k_j^\Pi) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi. \mathsf{Enc}(\Pi. \mathsf{pk}^{(j)})$$

$$\{d_i^{(j)} \leftarrow \Pi. \mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)}, c_j^\Pi)\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \cap B_2^{(j)}}$$

$$\{d_i^{(j)} \leftarrow \mathsf{TE}. \mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)}, c_j^\mathsf{TE})\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \cap B_2^{(j)}}$$

$$u_j \leftarrow s_j \oplus k_j^\Pi \oplus k_j^\mathsf{TE}$$

Let  $X_j^{(i)}(b)$  for  $j \in [0, \mathsf{Cut}]$  be a series of distributions on  $\left(\left\{(c_q, u_q)\right\}_{q \in [\ell]}, k^b, \left\{d_i^{(q)}\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}, q \in [\ell]}\right)$ , where  $X_0^{(i)}(b)$  is as in the experiment in Equation 2, but fixing the bit b. For all  $j \in [Cut]$ , we define  $X_j^{(i)}(b)$  to be the same distribution, except that for all  $q \in \operatorname{Ord}_i(\leq j)$ ,  $u_q$  is replaced by a uniformly sampled element from  $\mathcal{K}$ , respectively. We define the probability

$$p_j^{(i)} \coloneqq \Pr\left[D\left(X_j^{(i)}(b)\right) = b \colon b \xleftarrow{\$} \{0, 1\}\right].$$

Then, by definition, it holds that  $p_0^{(i)} \geq 1/2 + \varepsilon/4$ . Now observe that in  $X_{\mathsf{Cut}}^{(i)}(b)$ , a total of  $\mathsf{Cut} + 1$ elements  $u_j$  are uniformly random from  $\mathcal{K}$ , and thus  $X_{\mathsf{Cut}}^{(i)}(b)$  only depends on  $t_{\mathsf{parts}} - 1$  shares of  $k^0$ , which is unconditionally independent of  $k^0$ . As a result  $X_{\mathsf{Cut}}^{(i)}(0) \equiv X_{\mathsf{Cut}}^{(i)}(1)$  and thus  $p_{\mathsf{Cut}}^{(i)} = 1/2$ . As a consequence, there must be at least one  $j \in [\mathsf{Cut}]$  such that  $p_j^{(i)} \leq p_{j-1}^{(i)} - \varepsilon/(4 \cdot \mathsf{Cut})$ .

Let  $j^*$  be such j in step i and consider the decoder  $D_{j^*}^{(i)}$  on input  $\left(c_{j^*}^{\Pi}, k_{j^*}^{\Pi}, \{d_i^{(j^*)}\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j^*)}}\right)$ . Suppose  $k_{j^*}^{\Pi}$  is the correct key, i.e.,  $(c_{j^*}^{\Pi}, k_{j^*}^{\Pi})$  is sampled from  $\Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\Pi.\mathsf{pk}^{(j^*)})$ . In this case  $D_{j^*}^{(i)}$  samples a random bit b and outputs  $b \oplus D(X_{j^*-1}^{(i)}(b))$ , and thus, under the condition that  $k_{j^*}^{\Pi}$  is correct,  $D_{j^*}^{(i)}$  outputs 0 whenever D correctly guesses b, which happens with probability  $p_{j^*-1}^{(i)}$ . If, however,  $k_{j^*}^{\Pi}$  is invalid, then  $D_{j^*}^{(i)}$  runs D on distribution  $X_{j^*}^{(i)}(b)$  for a random bit b and outputs 1, whenever D incorrectly guesses b. Hence  $D_{j^*}^{(i)}$  correctly guesses that  $k_{j^*}^{\Pi}$  is invalid with probability  $1 - p_{j^*}^{(i)}$ . Resolving the conditional probabilities, we get that  $D_{j^*}^{(i)}$  correctly *predicts* the validity of  $k_{j^*}^{\Pi}$  with the following probability:

$$\frac{1}{2} \left( p_{j^*-1}^{(i)} + 1 - p_{j^*}^{(i)} \right) = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{p_{j^*-1}^{(i)} - p_{j^*}^{(i)}}{2} \ge \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon}{8\ell}$$

<span id="page-40-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>11</sup>An identical statement was shown to hold in the proof of Claim 6.7.

{41}------------------------------------------------

To prove Equation 1, we will show that for all  $i \in [t_{\mathsf{parts}}]$ ,  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}^{(i-1)}]$  is negligible. To do so, as explained in the outline, we must modify the reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  as to only produce a good decoder whenever it guesses  $j^* \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$  to be the *first* partition where  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  is good. For this purpose, we will let  $\mathcal{B}$  test all decoders in step  $i^*$ .

Modifying the reduction  $\mathcal{B}$ . Consider the reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  we constructed for the BadSteps case. Recall that the reduction initially samples random  $i^* \not = [t_{\mathsf{parts}}]$  and  $j^* \not = [\ell]$ . We modify  $\mathcal{B}$  to carry out an additional test on each decoder  $D_j^{(i^*)}$  for  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$ . For this,  $\mathcal{B}$  uses the simulated decoders as described in the BadSteps proof above. Let  $Q' := 512\ell^2\lambda/\varepsilon^2$ , and simulate for all  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$ : test $_j \not = \mathsf{TestDec}^{D_j^{(i^*)}}(\Pi.\mathsf{pk}^{(j)},\emptyset,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j)},\{\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)}\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j)}},Q',\frac{3\varepsilon}{32\ell})$ . Observe that for  $j=j^*$ , the reduction does not know  $\{\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j^*)}\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j^*)}}$ . However, these secret keys are only used during testing for generating partial decryptions for honestly generated ciphertexts, and thus  $\mathcal{B}$  can instead simulate these using its  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$  oracle. More formally,  $\mathcal{B}$  can compute test $_{j^*}$  as follows:

- Sample  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j^*)} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{j^*}.\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{Enc}}$ , compute  $(c,k^0) \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\Pi.\mathsf{pk}^{(j^*)};r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j^*)})$ , and obtain partial decryptions by querying the oracle  $d_i \leftarrow \mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}(i,r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j^*)})$  for all parties  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j^*)}$ .
- Sample  $k^1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}$ , and  $b \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\}$ .
- Run  $b' \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} D_j^{(i^*)}(c, k^b, \{d_i^{(j)}\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{nd}^{(j)}})$ . If b' = b, increment ctr.
- If  $ctr \geq Q' \cdot (1/2 + 3\varepsilon/32\ell)$ , set the flag  $\mathsf{test}_{j^*} \leftarrow 1$ . Otherwise, set  $\mathsf{test}_{j^*} \leftarrow 0$ .

Next, we make the following modification to the decoder that is returned by  $\mathcal{B}$ : If  $\mathsf{test}_{j^*} = 1$  and  $\mathsf{test}_j = 0$  for all  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[< j^*]$  (i.e., if  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  is the *first* decoder that tested good) then  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  and the set  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}^{(j^*)}$  to the challenger as before. Otherwise,  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs a Dummy decoder that always returns 0 instead.

Analysing  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}]$ . Given the modified reduction  $\mathcal{B}$ , we show that whenever  $\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}$  happens, then  $\mathcal{B}$  produces an  $\varepsilon/16\ell$ -good decoder against  $\Pi_j$  with probability almost  $1/\ell$ . More formally, we show the following claim.

<span id="page-41-0"></span>Claim 9.9 For all PPT adversaries A, it holds that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \ell \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Proof of Claim 9.9. We want to show that, if  $\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}$  happens, then  $\mathcal{B}$  produces a good decoder with probability almost  $1/\ell$  First, we apply Claim 9.8 to argue that, if  $\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}$  happens, then it holds that  $P_{\Pi_j}(D_j^{(i^*)}) \geq 1/2 + \varepsilon/8\ell$  for at least one  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$ , except with negligible probability. Since  $\mathcal{B}$  tests all decoders  $D_j^{(i^*)}$ , by a Hoeffding bound, the probability that there exists any j that is  $\varepsilon/8\ell$ -good, but fails  $\mathsf{test}_j$  is negligible. To see this, recall that  $\mathcal{B}$  tests  $D_j^{(i^*)}$  for all  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$  across Q' repetitions, and sets  $\mathsf{test}_j = 1$ , if the empirical advantage is at least  $3\varepsilon/32\ell$ . We can apply a Hoeffding bound to show that for  $Q' \geq 512\ell^2 \lambda/\varepsilon^2$ , a decoder that is  $\varepsilon/8\ell$  good, will only ever test bad with probability  $\exp(-\lambda)$ . A union bound over all Cut decoders that are  $\mathsf{test}_j$  shows that if  $\mathsf{test}$  happens, then  $\mathsf{test}_j$  happens for at least one  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$ , except with negligible probability.

Additionally, we must rule out that  $\mathsf{test}_{j^*} = 1$ , but  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  is not actually  $\varepsilon/16\ell$ -good against  $\Pi_{j^*}$ , or in other words, that  $\mathsf{test}_{j^*}$  is a false positive. An analogous Hoeffding bound shows that if  $\mathsf{test}_{j^*}$  happens, then  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  must be  $\varepsilon/16\ell$ -good against  $\Pi_{j^*}$ , except with negligible probability.

then  $D_{j^*}^{(i^*)}$  must be  $\varepsilon/16\ell$ -good against  $\Pi_{j^*}$ , except with negligible probability. Let  $\kappa_j$  be the probability that  $\mathsf{test}_j$  happens but  $\mathsf{test}_q$  does not happen for any  $q \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[< j]$ . The above implies that

$$\sum_{j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]} \kappa_j \geq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

and that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] \geq (1/\ell) \cdot \sum_{j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]} \kappa_j - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

{42}------------------------------------------------

Combining both, we get that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \ell \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Bounding  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}^{(i-1)}]$ . Finally, in order to get the desired bound of Equation 1, we want to bound  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}^{(i)}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}^{(i-1)}_{\mathcal{A}}]$  by  $\mathcal{B}$ 's advantage against traceability of  $\Pi$ . In other words, we aim to show that in subsequent hybrids,  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}]$  can increase by at most a negligible amount. To do so, we first show the following claim.

<span id="page-42-0"></span>Claim 9.10 For all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  and all  $i \in [t_{\mathsf{parts}}]$  there exists a negligible function such that  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)}] \leq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

Proof of Claim 9.10. First, observe that the only way for  $\mathcal{A}$  to cause  $\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)} = 1$ , but  $\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)} = 0$  is to set  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_i = 0$ , and  $\mathsf{test} = 1$ . Hence, we get that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)}] \leq \Pr[\neg \mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \wedge \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}].$$

We can apply the law of total probability (twice) to get

$$\begin{split} \Pr[\neg \mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \land \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] &= \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \land \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] \\ &= \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \land \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}} = 0] \end{split}$$

W.l.o.g., we can assume that  $\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}} = 0] > 0$ , and thus

$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \wedge \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}} = 0] \\ &\leq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \mid \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}} = 0]. \end{split}$$

Observe that  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_i$ , can only happen if tracing in step i identifies at least one non-empty set. If  $\mathsf{test} = 0$ , however, the tracing algorithm will trace only dummy decoders in every step, and thus we get

$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \mid \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}} = 0] \\ &\leq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + \Pr[\Pi.\mathsf{Trace}^{\mathsf{Dummy}(\cdot)}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{tk},\varepsilon') \neq \emptyset]. \end{split}$$

Finally, due to traceability of  $\Pi$ , tracing a dummy decoder can only return a non-empty set with negligible probability, and we get the claim:

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)}] \leq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Next, we need to show that

<span id="page-42-1"></span>
$$\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] \leq \ell \cdot (\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i]) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \tag{3}$$

In Claim 9.9, we have already shown that  $\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \ell \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Hence, to prove Equation 3, it remains to show the following claim.

<span id="page-42-2"></span>Claim 9.11 For all PPT adversaries A, it holds that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i..A}] \ge \ell \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Proof of Claim 9.11. Observe that the respective events that  $\mathsf{test}_j$  happen are independent of  $\mathcal{B}$ 's choice of  $j^*$ . Hence, given that  $\mathsf{test}_j = 1$  for at least one  $j \in \mathsf{Ord}_{i^*}[\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  will pick this instance with probability at least  $1/\ell$ . If  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses incorrectly, then  $\mathcal{B}$  will output a dummy decoder, in which case  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  can only happen with negligible probability. If, however,  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses correctly, then  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  implies  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_i$ , because  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}} = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}(j^*)$ , and thus

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] \leq \frac{\ell-1}{\ell} \cdot \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) + \frac{\Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}]}{\ell}.$$

{43}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-43-2"></span>Together, Claims 9.9, 9.10, and 9.11 imply that

$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)}] \\ &\leq \ell \cdot (\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i]) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \end{split}$$

Equipped with this analysis, we get that

$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(t_{\mathsf{parts}})}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] \\ &= \sum_{i=1}^{t_{\mathsf{parts}}} \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)}] \right) \\ &\leq \sum_{i=1}^{t_{\mathsf{parts}}} \left( \ell \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] \right) + \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda) \right) \\ &= \sum_{i=1}^{t_{\mathsf{parts}}} \frac{\ell \cdot \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \land \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \land \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] \right)}{\Pr[\mathcal{B}.i^* = i]} + t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda) \\ &= \ell \cdot t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \sum_{i=1}^{t_{\mathsf{parts}}} \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \land \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \land \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] \right) + t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda) \\ &= \ell \cdot t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}] \right) + t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda) \\ &\leq \ell \cdot t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) + t_{\mathsf{parts}} \cdot \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda). \end{split}$$

Putting it all together, assuming traceability of  $\Pi$ , and simplifying negligibilities, we get that

$$\begin{split} \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] &= \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] \\ &\geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(t_{\mathsf{parts}})}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}'(\lambda). \end{split}$$

This concludes the proof for Lemma 9.6.

Remark 9.12 Part 2 (Lemma 9.6) was proved for arbitrary  $t_{\mathsf{parts}}$  and  $\rho$ , and essentially shows that in every step  $i \in [d]$ , a traitor is found from at least one unique partition. Hence, one could redo the analysis in parts 1, 3, and 4 for different  $t_{\mathsf{parts}}$  values and assumed partial-insider resilience  $\rho$  of  $\Pi$ , to get different tradeoffs between partial-insider resilience, numbers of traitors found, and robustness.

# <span id="page-43-0"></span>10 Achieving Maximal Partial Insider Resilience

To construct a traceable threshold KEM that is natively resilient against partial insiders, we follow the fingerprinting approach and introduce a modified version of a bipartite threshold KEM that allows the tracer to simulate up to t-1 partial decryptions for both valid and malformed ciphertexts.

For this, we first define the building block PD-BT-KEM, as well as the requirements for one-sided security in the presence of simulated partial decryptions, which will be essential for tracing. Then, we show that we can instantiate our new building block with a variant of the pairing-based BT-KEM scheme introduced in [12] with minimal modifications.

Finally, we show how to generically transform any PD-BT-KEM scheme to a 1-traceable TT-KEM that is natively resilient against up to t-1 partial insiders (i.e., maximally partial-insider resilient), with the restriction that our transformation only achieves traceability against decrypting decoders instead of distinguishing decoders. We leave it as an interesting problem for future work to achieve native partial insider resilience for distinguishing decoders.

#### <span id="page-43-1"></span>10.1 Bipartite Threshold Encryption with Simulated Partial Decryptions

We adjust the BT-KEM definition due to [12] by adding a SimPD algorithm to the syntax.

**Definition 10.1** (PD-BT-KEM). A PD-BT-KEM scheme with keyspace  $\mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$  is a tuple of algorithms  $\Pi = (\mathsf{Setup}, \mathsf{KGen}, \mathsf{Enc}, \mathsf{PDec}, \mathsf{SimPD}, \mathsf{Combine})$  with the following syntax.

{44}------------------------------------------------

- <span id="page-44-1"></span>• pp  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup(1 $^{\lambda}$ ). Given the security parameter  $\lambda$ , the Setup algorithm outputs public parameters pp that are implicit input to all subsequent algorithms.
- $\left(\mathsf{pk}, \left\{\left(\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)}, \mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)}\right)\right\}_{i \in [n], j \in [\ell]}\right) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda}, n, t, \ell)$ . KGen receives the security parameter  $\lambda$ , the number of parties  $n \in \mathbb{N}$ , the threshold  $0 < t \le n$ , and the number of positions  $\ell \in \mathbb{N}$ . It outputs a public key  $\mathsf{pk}$ , and for every party  $i \in [n]$  and every position  $j \in [\ell]$  a left secret key share  $\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)}$  and a right secret key share  $\mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)}$ . As shorthand, we denote the set of all secret key shares  $\mathsf{sk}$ , and the set of all secret key shares for party i as  $\mathsf{sk}_i$ .
- $(k, c := (c_0, c_1)) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, j)$ . Given the public key  $\mathsf{pk}$  and a position  $j \in [\ell]$ , the encryption algorithm outputs a KEM key  $k \in \mathcal{K}_{\lambda}$  and a ciphertext c that consists of a left component  $c_0$  and a right component  $c_1$
- $d_i \leftarrow \mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_{i,b}^{(j)}, (c_0, c_1), j)$ ). Given the secret key share  $\mathsf{sk}_{i,b}^{(j)}$  of party i at position j for side  $b \in \{0, 1\}$ , a ciphertext  $(c_0, c_1)$ , and the position j, this  $\mathsf{PDec}$  algorithm outputs a partial decryption  $d_i$ .
- $d_i \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{SimPD}(\mathsf{pk}, i, (c_0, c_1), j, r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ . Given a participant index i, a ciphertext  $(c_0, c_1)$ , the ciphertext position  $j \in [\ell]$ , and randomness used during encryption  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}$ , the SimPD algorithm outputs a simulated partial decryption  $d_i$  on behalf of party i.
- $k/\bot \leftarrow \mathsf{Combine}(\mathsf{pk}, S, \{d_i\}_{i \in S}, (c_0, c_1), j)$ . Given the public key  $\mathsf{pk}$ , the set of partial decryptions  $\{d_i\}_{i \in S}$  from parties in  $S \subseteq [n]$  with  $|S| \ge t$ , the ciphertext  $(c_0, c_1)$ , and the position j, output the KEM key k or  $\bot$  to indicate failure.

A PD-BT-KEM must additionally achieve the following properties. Like [12], we require that one can mix left and right shares to decrypt for a given position j.

**Definition 10.2** (Two-Sided Correctness). A PD-BT-KEM scheme is two-sided correct if for all  $n,t,\ell\in \operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$  the following holds: For all  $\lambda\in\mathbb{N},\ j\in[\ell],\ S\subseteq[n]$  with  $|S|\geq t,$  all  $\operatorname{pp}\in\operatorname{Setup}(1^\lambda),$  all  $\left(\operatorname{pk},\{(\operatorname{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)},\operatorname{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)})\}_{i\in[n],j\in[\ell]}\right)\in\operatorname{KGen}(1^\lambda,n,t,\ell),\ (k,c)\in\operatorname{Enc}(\operatorname{pk},j),$  and all n-bit string  $s\in\{0,1\}^n$  it holds that

$$\mathsf{Combine}\bigg(\mathsf{pk}, S, \left\{\mathsf{PDec}\left(\mathsf{sk}_{i,s_i}^{(j)}, c, j\right)\right\}_{i \in S}, c, j\bigg) = k.$$

We introduce an additional property of simulability of partial decryptions, which states that for honestly generated ciphertexts, the partial decryptions output by SimPD must be identical to partial decryptions generated with the left and right key. In particular, this implies that for honestly generated ciphertexts, partial decryptions with the left and right key must be equal. We summarize both properties in a single definition.

<span id="page-44-0"></span>**Definition 10.3** (Perfect Partial Decryption Simulation for PD-BT-KEM). We say that a PD-BT-KEM scheme achieves simulability of partial decryptions if for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}, \ 0 < t \le n \in \mathbb{N}$ , and for all  $i \in [n]$  it holds that

$$\mathsf{SimPD}\left(\mathsf{pk}, i, (c_0, c_1), j, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}\right) = \mathsf{PDec}\Big(\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)}, (c_0, c_1), j\Big) = \mathsf{PDec}\Big(\mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)}, (c_0, c_1), j\Big)\,,$$

 $\text{for all pp} \in \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda), \ \left(\mathsf{pk}, \{(\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)}, \mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)})\}_{i \in [n], j \in [\ell]}\right) \in \mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, t, \ell), \ \text{all} \ r_{\mathsf{Enc}} \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{Enc}} \ \text{for} \ (k, (c_0, c_1)) = \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, j; r_{\mathsf{Enc}}).$ 

Diverging further from [12], we define our own version one-sided security in the presence of up to t-1 simulated partial decryptions. Crucially, we pose different requirements for left and right one-sided security.

Left-Sided Security with Simulated Partial Decryptions (LSS-SPD). On a high level, LSS-SPD states that an adversary who has only left keys (i.e., no right keys) for a chosen position  $u \in [\ell]$  cannot distinguish whether or not the right side  $c_1^*$  of a given ciphertext  $c^* = (c_0, c_1^*)$  fits with the left side and the key k, even if he obtains up to t-1 simulated partial decryptions with the randomness fitting the

{45}------------------------------------------------

```

                                                                                                                                                                Game-OSS-Right _{4}(1^{\lambda})

                                                                                                                                                                (\ell, u, \mathsf{st}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{A}_1(1^{\lambda})
 (pk, sk) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} KGen(1^{\lambda}, n, t, \ell)
                                                                                                                                                                (\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda},n,t,\ell)

                                                                                                                                                                return 0
```

Figure 7: Game-OSS-Left and Game-OSS-Right for PD-BT-KEM.

left side and the key. More formally, suppose that  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}} \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{Enc}}, \ (k^{(0)}, c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(0)}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, u; r_{\mathsf{Enc}}),$  and  $(k^{(1)}, c_0^{(1)}, c_1^{(1)}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, u),$  then an adversary who for position u only has access to left keys, cannot significantly distinguish bewteen

$$\left(k^{(0)}, (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(0)}), \left\{\mathsf{SimPD}\Big(\mathsf{pk}, i, (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(0)}), u, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}\Big)\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\right)$$

and

$$\left(k^{(0)}, (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)}), \left\{\mathsf{SimPD}\Big(\mathsf{pk}, i, (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)}), u, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}\Big)\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\right),$$

for any chosen  $\mathcal{I}_{pd} \subseteq [n]$  with  $|\mathcal{I}_{pd}| < t$ .

Right-Sided Security with Simulated Partial Decryptions (RSS-SPD). For RSS-SPD, we instead argue that an adversary who for index u has only right keys is unable to compute the key  $k^{(0)}$  fitting the left part of the ciphertext  $c_0^{(0)}$  given malformed ciphertext  $(c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)})$ , even if it obtains less than t simulated partial decryptions using the randomness  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  belonging to the left ciphertext and key. More formally, suppose that  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{Enc}}$ ,  $(k^{(0)}, c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(0)}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, u; r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ , and  $(k^{(1)}, c_0^{(1)}, c_1^{(1)}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, u)$ , then an adversary that does not have any left keys for position u cannot compute  $k^{(0)}$  given

$$\left(k^{(0)}, (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)}), \left\{\mathsf{SimPD}\Big(\mathsf{pk}, i, (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)}), u, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}\Big)\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\right),$$

except with negligible probability.

In both games, we require that the set  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$  can be chosen by the adversary *adaptively*, i.e., even after seeing the challenge ciphertext. This allows us to prove an even stronger variant of traceability against *adaptive* partial insiders, i.e., where the decoder can select a set of partial insiders after seeing the ciphertext.

**Definition 10.4** (One-Sided Security against Simulated Partial Decryptions (OSS-SPD)). A PD-BT-KEM scheme is said to achieve one-sided security against simulated partial decryptions if for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function negl such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) = 1] \leq \frac{1}{2} + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ \ \mathrm{and} \ \ \Pr[\mathsf{Game-OSS-Right}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) = 1] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

{46}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-46-4"></span><span id="page-46-1"></span>where Game-OSS-Left and Game-OSS-Right are defined in Figure 7. We say that a PD-BT-KEM scheme is Left-SPD secure and Right-SPD secure when referring to security with respect to the two games, respectively.

Figure 8: The semantic security game of PD-BT-KEM.

<span id="page-46-3"></span>**Definition 10.5** (Semantic Security of PD-BT-KEM). A PD-BT-KEM scheme is said to achieve semantic security if for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function negl such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Game\text{-}IND\text{-}BT\text{-}CPA}_{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) = 1] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where Game-IND-BT-CPA is defined in Figure 8.

## <span id="page-46-0"></span>10.2 A PD-BT-KEM from Pairings

We construct a PD-BT-KEM scheme by making minor modifications to the pairings-based BT-KEM construction in [12, Section 5.2]. In particular, we add an "individual public key" for each party to the public key and instantiate a SimPD algorithm. Modifications over the BT-KEM in [12, Section 5.2] are highlighted in gray.

Construction 7 (A PD-BT-KEM  $\mathcal{X}$  following [12, Section 5.2]). Let  $E = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, e, g_1, g_2, p) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{G}(1^{\lambda})$  be public parameters.

- $\mathcal{X}.\mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda},n,t,\ell)$  Sample  $\alpha,y,z \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  as well as a Shamir t-out-of-n sharing  $s_1,\ldots,s_n$  of  $\alpha$  and compute  $X \leftarrow g_1^{\alpha yz}, \; \{X_i \leftarrow e(g_1,g_2)^{s_iyz}\}_{i\in[n]}, \; Y \leftarrow g_1^y, \; \text{and} \; Z \leftarrow g_1^z.$  Set the public key to  $\mathsf{pk} \coloneqq (X,\{X_i\}_{i\in[n]},Y,Z).^{12}$  For all  $i\in[n]$  and  $j\in[\ell]$ , do the following
  - Compute  $k_0^{(0)} \leftarrow H(j)^{zs_i}$  and  $k_1^{(0)} \leftarrow g_2^{zs_i}$ . Assign  $\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)} \leftarrow (k_0^{(0)}, k_1^{(0)}) \in \mathbb{G}_1 \times \mathbb{G}_2$ .
  - Compute  $k_0^{(1)} \leftarrow H(j)^{ys_i}$  and  $k_1^{(1)} \leftarrow g_2^{ys_i}$ . Assign  $\mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)} \leftarrow (k_0^{(1)}, k_1^{(1)}) \in \mathbb{G}_1 \times \mathbb{G}_2$ .

Output  $(\mathsf{pk}, \{(\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)}, \mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)})\}_{i \in [n], j \in [\ell]}).$ 

 $\mathcal{X}$ .Enc(pk, j): Parse pk as (X, \*, Y, Z) and do the following:

- Sample  $r, t_0, t_1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ .
- Set  $k \leftarrow e(X, g_2)^r = e(g_1, g_2)^{\alpha yzr}$ .
- Compute  $c_0 \leftarrow (g_2^{t_0}, Y^r \cdot H(j)^{t_0})$  and  $c_1 \leftarrow (g_2^{t_1}, Z^r \cdot H(j)^{t_1})$ .
- Output  $(k, (c_0, c_1))$ .

<span id="page-46-2"></span> $<sup>^{12}</sup>$ In [12, Section 5.2] the public key does not contain the  $X_i$  elements. Note that we only use these elements for SimPD, hence encrypting users do not need to store them.

{47}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-47-2"></span> $\mathcal{X}.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_{i,b}^{(j)},(c_0,c_1),j)$ : Parse  $\mathsf{sk}_{i,b}^{(j)}$  as  $(k_0,k_1)$  and  $c_b$  as (u,v). Then, compute and return

$$d_i \leftarrow \frac{e(v, k_1)}{e(k_0, u)} [= e(g_1, g_2)^{s_i y z r}].$$

 $\mathcal{X}.\mathsf{Combine}(j,c,S,\{d_i\}_{i\in[S]})$ : Compute the Lagrange coefficients  $\{\lambda_{i,S}^{(0)}\}_{i\in S}$  for interpolating among set S at position 0. Then, compute and output  $k\leftarrow\prod_{i\in S}d_i^{\lambda_{i,S}^{(0)}}$ .

 $\mathcal{X}.\mathsf{SimPD}(\mathsf{pk},i,(c_0,c_1),j,r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ : Parse  $X_i \ [= e(g_1,g_2)^{s_iyz}]$  from the public key and  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  as  $(r,t_0,t_1)$ . Then, compute and output  $d_i \leftarrow X_i^r \ [= e(g_1,g_2)^{s_iyzr}]$ .

**Theorem 10.6** The construction  $\mathcal{X}$  is a secure PD-BT-KEM scheme under the two variants of the ABDDH assumption ([12, Definition 8], and Assumption 2), and the t-ABCDH assumption (Assumption 1).

<span id="page-47-0"></span>**Lemma 10.7** (Left-SPD Security of  $\mathcal{X}$ ). The PD-BT-KEM  $\mathcal{X}$  is Left-SPD secure under the augmented bilinear decisional Diffie-Hellman assumption [12, Definition 8].

Proof of Lemma 10.7. Our Game-OSS-Left instantiated with  $\mathcal{X}$  is identical to Game-OSS instantiated with the construction in [12, Section 5.2] for d=0 except for the additional public key elements  $\{X_i\}_{i\in[n]}$  and the simulated partial decryptions for the challenge ciphertext. The proof is almost identical to that in [12] for d=0. In particular, observe that their reduction samples  $\alpha$  as well as a sharing  $s_1,\ldots,s_n$  of  $\alpha$  itself. Thus, we can simulate the additional outputs without any additional assumptions as follows:

Simulating  $\{X_i\}_{i\in[n]}$ . Since the reduction knows the field elements  $s_i$  for the sharing of  $\alpha$ , it can simulate  $X_i$  as  $e(g_1^{yz}, g_2)^{s_i} = e(g_1, g_2)^{s_iyz}$  for all  $i \in [n]$ , where  $g_1^{yz}$  is already part of the reductions ABDDH challenge.

<span id="page-47-1"></span>Simulating SimPD(pk,  $i, c^*, j, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^*$ ). The challenge here is that the reduction does not know the field element r corresponding to the challenge  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^*$ . However, the reduction learns  $g_1^{yr}$  and  $g_2^z$  as part of its ABDDH challenge and thus can compute  $d_i \leftarrow e(g_1^{yr}, g_2^z)^{s_i}$  on behalf of all  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}$ , which is consistent with the challenge ciphertext.

Game Int-ABCDH<sub> $\mathcal{A},t$ </sub>(1 $^{\lambda}$ )  $E := (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, e, g_1, g_2, p) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{G}(1^{\lambda})$   $S \leftarrow \{0, 1, \dots, t-1\}$   $\forall k \in S : \text{Sample } \alpha_k \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$   $h_0, h_1, y, z, r, v \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$   $\text{view}_1 \leftarrow \left(g_1^y, g_1^z, g_1^{yr+h_0v}, g_1^{h_0}, g_1^{h_1}, g_1^{\alpha_0yz}\right)$   $\{g_1^{\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}, \{g_1^{h_0y\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}, \{g_1^{h_1y\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}, \{g_1^{h_1z\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S})$   $\text{view}_2 \leftarrow \left(g_2^v, \{g_2^{y\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}, \{g_2^{z\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}\right)$   $(\mathcal{I}, \text{st}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda}, E, \text{view}_1, \text{view}_2)$   $\text{if } \mathcal{I} \not\subseteq \mathbb{N} \setminus \{0\} \vee |\mathcal{I}| \geq t \text{ then return } 0$   $\text{view}_T \leftarrow \left\{e(g_1, g_2)^{yzr} \sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_{k,S}^{(i)} \cdot \alpha_k \right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}}$   $K \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{A}(\text{st}, \text{view}_T)$   $\text{if } K = e(g_1, g_2)^{\alpha_0 yzr} \text{ then return } 1$  else return 0

Figure 9: The Int-ABCDH experiment.

{48}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-48-0"></span>Assumption 1 (Interactive t-Augmented Bilinear Computational Diffie-Hellman (t-ABCDH)). We say that the interactive t-ABCDH problem is hard with respect to a bilinear group generation algorithm  $\mathcal{G}$  and  $t \in \mathbb{N}$  if for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function negl such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Int}\text{-}\mathsf{ABCDH}_{\mathcal{A},t}(1^{\lambda})=1] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where the Int-ABCDH experiment is defined in Figure 9.

We prove that the t-ABCDH assumption holds in the GGM in Appendix E.
