<span id="page-48-1"></span>**Lemma 10.8** (Right-SPD Security of  $\mathcal{X}$ ). The PD-BT-KEM  $\mathcal{X}$  is Right-SPD secure under the t-ABCDH assumption (Assumption 1).

Proof of Lemma 10.8. Let  $\lambda, n, t \in \mathbb{N}$  and  $0 < t \le n$ . Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary against Game-OSS-Right (Figure 7) instantiated with  $\mathcal{X}$ . Let  $L \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  be an upper bound on the number of positions  $\ell$  in PD-BT-KEM. We construct a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  that uses  $\mathcal{A}$  to win the Int-ABCDH<sub>t</sub> experiment (Figure 9). In the beginning,  $\mathcal{B}$  receives the pairing ensemble  $E = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, e, g_1, g_2, p)$  as well as the following challenge group elements:

$$\begin{aligned} \mathrm{view}_1 &= \left(g_1^y, g_1^z, g_1^{yr+h_0v}, g_1^{h_0}, g_1^{h_1}, g_1^{\alpha_0yz}, \{g_1^{\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}, \{g_1^{h_0y\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}, \{g_1^{h_1y\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}, \{g_1^{h_1z\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S} \right) \\ & \\ \mathrm{view}_2 &= \left(g_2^v, \{g_2^{y\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S}, \{g_2^{z\alpha_k}\}_{k \in S} \right), \end{aligned}$$

where S = [0, t-1]. Intuitively,  $\mathcal{B}$  embeds the challenge into its simulation of Game-OSS-Right such that  $\alpha \coloneqq \alpha_0$  and  $s_i \coloneqq \sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_{k,S}^{(i)} \alpha_k$ . This will cause all  $s_i$  to lie on a degree t-1 polynomial f with  $f(k) = \alpha_k$  for  $k \in S$ . First,  $\mathcal{B}$  samples a random  $j^* \in [L]$  and runs  $\mathcal{A}_1(1^{\lambda})$ , while simulating the random oracle H(j) as follows:

- If  $\mathcal{A}$  queries for  $j = j^*$ , output  $g_1^{h_0}$  from the t-ABCDH challenge.
- If  $\mathcal{A}$  queries for  $j \neq j^*$ , sample a random  $w_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  and output  $(g_1^{h_1})^{w_j}$ . Output consistently for subsequent identical queries.

As a result,  $\mathcal{B}$  receives  $(\ell, u)$  from  $\mathcal{A}$  and aborts if  $u \neq j^*$ . Otherwise,  $\mathcal{B}$  samples  $w_j$  for all remaining  $j \in [\ell]$  and proceeds to simulate Game-OSS-Right to  $\mathcal{A}$  as follows:

**Public key pk:**  $\mathcal{B}$  embeds the challenge  $X = g_1^{\alpha_0 yz}$ ,  $Y = g_1^y$ , and  $Z = g_1^z$ . It simulates  $X_i$  as  $\prod_{k=0}^{t-1} e(g_1^y, g_2^{z\alpha_k})^{\lambda_{k,S}^{(i)}}$  for all  $i \in [n]$ , which is consistent with  $e(g_1, g_2)^{s_i yz}$  for our simulation of  $s_i$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  assembles  $\mathsf{pk} \leftarrow (X, \{X_i\}_{i \in [n]}, Y, Z)$ .

Both secret keys for  $j \neq u$ : For all  $j \in [\ell] \setminus \{u\}$  and  $i \in [n]$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  needs to simulate both left and right keys. It simulates

$$\begin{split} \operatorname{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)} &\leftarrow \left(\prod_{k=0}^{t-1} (g_2^{z\alpha_k})^{\lambda_{k,S}^{(i)}}, \left(\prod_{k=0}^{t-1} (g_1^{h_1z\alpha_k})^{\lambda_{k,S}^{(i)}}\right)^{w_j}\right) \ \left[=\left(g_2^{zs_i}, (g_1^{w_jh_1})^{zs_i}\right) = (g_2^{zs_i}, H(j)^{zs_i})\right] \\ \operatorname{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)} &\leftarrow \left(\prod_{k=0}^{t-1} (g_2^{y\alpha_k})^{\lambda_{k,S}^{(i)}}, \left(\prod_{k=0}^{t-1} (g_1^{h_1y\alpha_k})^{\lambda_{k,S}^{(i)}}\right)^{w_j}\right) \ \left[=\left(g_2^{ys_i}, (g_1^{w_jh_1})^{ys_i}\right) = (g_2^{ys_i}, H(j)^{ys_i})\right]. \end{split}$$

**Right secret keys for** j = u: For j = u, the reduction is only required to simulate right keys for all parties  $i \in [n]$ . It does so as follows:

$$\mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(u)} \leftarrow \left( \prod_{k=0}^{t-1} (g_2^{y\alpha_k})^{\lambda_{k,S}^{(i)}}, \prod_{k=0}^{t-1} (g_1^{h_0y\alpha_k})^{\lambda_{k,S}^{(i)}} \right) \ \left[ = \left( g_2^{ys_i}, (g_1^{h_0})^{ys_i} \right) = (g_2^{ys_i}, H(u)^{ys_i}) \right].$$

Note that the reduction would not be able to simulate left keys for j=u, since it does not have access to any  $g_1^{h_0z\alpha_k}$  in the t-ABCDH challenge.

{49}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-49-3"></span>Challenge Ciphertext: Simulate the challenge ciphertext for  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^* = (r, v, t_1^{(0)}) \equiv (r^{(0)}, t_0^{(0)}, t_1^{(0)})$ , where r and v correspond to the respective elements in the ABCDH challenge and  $t_1^{(1)}$  is irrelevant (since elements depending on it would only appear in  $c_1^{(0)}$ , which is outside the adversary's view). More formally, compute  $c_0^{(0)} \leftarrow (g_2^v, g_1^{yr+h_0v})$ , which is a valid left ciphertext, since  $H(u) = g_1^{h_0}$ . Additionally, sample  $r', t_1^{(1)} \Leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p$  and compute  $c_1^{(1)} \leftarrow (g_2^{t_1^{(1)}}, (g_1^z)^{r'} \cdot (g_1^{h_0})^{t_1^{(1)}})$ , which corresponds to a random right ciphertext that is inconsistent with the key  $K \equiv e(g_1, g_2)^{\alpha_0 yzr}$ . Assemble  $c^* \leftarrow (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)})$ .

 $\mathcal{B} \text{ assembles shares} \leftarrow \left(\{(\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)},\mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)})\}_{i \in [n], j \in [\ell] \setminus \{u\}}, \{\mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(u)}\}_{i \in [n]}\right) \text{ and runs } \mathcal{A}_2(\mathsf{st},\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{shares},c^*) \text{ to obtain } \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}.$  Let us assume from now on that  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \subseteq [n]$  and  $|\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}| < t$  since  $\mathcal{A}$  would otherwise trivially lose Game-OSS-Right. Next, the reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  forwards  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}$  to its challenger and receives

$$\mathsf{view}_T \leftarrow \left\{Q_i = e(g_1, g_2)^{yzr} \sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_{k,S}^{(i)} \cdot \alpha_k \right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}$$

as a response. Recall that  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates such that, conceptually,  $s_i = \sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_{k,S}^{(i)} \cdot \alpha_k$  for all  $i \in [n]$ , and thus  $Q_i = e(g_1, g_2)^{yzr} \sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_{k,S}^{(i)} \cdot \alpha_k = e(g_1, g_2)^{s_i yzr}$ .

**Simulating SimPD:**  $\mathcal{B}$ , sets the simulated partial decryptions to  $Q_i$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}$ , which are consistent with  $e(g_1, g_2)^{s_i y z r}$  for  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^* = (r, \ldots)$ .

 $\mathcal{B}$  runs  $\mathcal{A}_3(\mathsf{st}, \{Q_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}})$  to receive a group element K, which it returns to its own challenger. Note that, if  $\mathcal{B}$  initially guesses  $j^* = u$  correctly (which  $\mathcal{B}$  does with probability 1/L), then  $\mathcal{B}$ 's simulation to  $\mathcal{A}$  is perfect. Further,  $\mathcal{A}$  wins Game-OSS-Right, whenever  $K = e(g_1, g_2)^{\alpha yzr} = e(g_1, g_2)^{\alpha_0 yzr}$  in which case  $\mathcal{B}$  wins the Int-ABCDH experiment, and thus

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Game}\text{-}\mathsf{OSS}\text{-}\mathsf{Right}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda})=1]=L\cdot\Pr[\mathsf{Int}\text{-}\mathsf{ABCDH}_{\mathcal{B},t}(1^{\lambda})=1],$$

which is negligible as per the interactive t-ABCDH assumption.

The following assumption appears identically in an earlier version of [11].

<span id="page-49-0"></span>Assumption 2 (Augmented Bilinear Decisional Diffie-Hellman (ABDDH)). We say that the augmented bilinear decisional Diffie-Hellman (ABDDH) problem is hard with respect to a bilinear group generation algorithm  $\mathcal{G}$  if for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  the following function

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{abddh}}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{G}} \coloneqq |\Pr[\mathcal{A}(E,\mathsf{view},e(g_1,g_2)^{\alpha yzr}) = 1] - \Pr[\mathcal{A}(E,\mathsf{view},e(g_1,g_2)^v]|$$

is negligible in  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , where  $E = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, e, g_1, g_2, p) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{G}(1^{\lambda}), (\alpha, r, y, z, v) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p^5$ , and

view 
$$\leftarrow (g_1^y, g_1^z, g_1^{yr}, g_1^{zr}, g_1^{\alpha yz}, g_2^y, g_2^z)$$
.

The semantic security of our construction  $\mathcal{X}$  follows from the same assumption as in [11].<sup>13</sup> Our modification of adding the individual public keys  $X_i = e(g_1, g_2)^{s_i y z}$ , however, causes a problem when trying to simulate the adaptive corruption oracle because publishing  $X_i$  for all parties  $i \in [n]$  as part of the public key commits the reduction ahead of time to the shares  $s_i$  of  $\alpha$ . To avoid this issue, we restrict ourselves to proving semantic security in the *static* setting, where the adversary is required to pick a set of corrupted parties Cor before observing the public key. We expect that adaptive semantic security would be provable in an idealized model (such as the GGM [49, 45] or AGM [35]) or with respect to a more tailored interactive assumption that is justifiable in such a model. Importantly, we note that this does not affect traceability, i.e., we can prove traceability even against adversaries who adaptively corrupt parties.

<span id="page-49-2"></span>**Lemma 10.9** (Semantic Security of  $\mathcal{X}$ ). The PD-BT-KEM  $\mathcal{X}$  is semantically secure (Definition 10.5) under ABDDH assumption (Assumption 2) against static corruptions.

<span id="page-49-1"></span> $<sup>^{13}</sup>$ Note that in this paper, we work with a version of  $\mathcal{X}$  that is semantically secure under a variant of decisional bilinear Diffie-Hellman, similar to an earlier version of [11] for simplicity. This construction can be transformed to be secure under the computational equivalent of this assumption by deriving the final KEM key through a random oracle evaluation on the current KEM key.

{50}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-50-1"></span>*Proof of Lemma 10.9.* Our proof follows very closely that of [11]. Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be an arbitrary PPT adversary against semantic security of PD-BT-KEM. We construct a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  that uses  $\mathcal{A}$  to break the ABDDH assumption (Definition 2).

The adversary receives as input an ABDDH tuple

$$({\rm view},T) = ((g_1^y,g_1^z,g_1^{yr},g_1^{zr},g_1^{\alpha yz},g_2^y,g_2^z)\,,T)\,,$$

where either  $T = e(g_1, g_2)^{\alpha yzr}$  or  $T = e(g_1, g_2)^v$  for a random  $v \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ . The reduction runs  $\mathcal{A}$  to obtain the number of positions  $\ell$  as well as the set of corrupted parties Cor while simulating the random oracle as follows: On input  $j \in \mathbb{N}$ , sample  $w_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  and consistently reply with  $g_1^{w_j}$  for this subsequent queries on the same input j. Then, the reduction samples all random oracle values  $w_j$  for inputs  $j \in [\ell]$  that were not queried by  $\mathcal{A}$  so far. W.l.o.g. assume that  $\mathsf{Cor} \subseteq [n]$  and  $|\mathsf{Cor}| = t - 1$ .

Next,  $\mathcal{B}$  is tasked with simulating the public key material  $\mathsf{pk} = \left(X, \{X_i\}_{i \in [n]}, Y, Z\right)$  as well as the secret key for both sides on behalf of parties in Cor and the challenge ciphertext. To do so, the reduction samples a random  $s_i \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$  for all  $i \in \mathsf{Cor}$  and continues the simulation as follows:

**Public Key Material:** The reduction embeds its ABDDH challenge and sets  $X \leftarrow (g_1^{\alpha yz}), Y \leftarrow (g_1^y),$  and  $Z \leftarrow (g_1^z)$ . For all  $i \in \mathsf{Cor}$  it sets  $X_i \leftarrow e((g_1^y), (g_2^z))_i^s$  and for all  $j \in [n] \setminus \mathsf{Cor}$  it interpolates

$$X_j \leftarrow X^{\lambda_{0,\mathsf{Cor} \cup \{0\}}^{(j)}} \cdot \prod_{i \in \mathsf{Cor}} X_i^{\lambda_{i,\mathsf{Cor} \cup \{0\}}^{(j)}}.$$

The reduction assembles  $\mathsf{pk} \leftarrow (X, \{X_i\}_{i \in [n]}, Y, Z).$ 

**Secret Keys:** For all corrupted parties  $i \in \mathsf{Cor}$ , the reduction simulates the left and right secret keys for all positions  $j \in [\ell]$  as

$$\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)} \leftarrow ((g_1^z)^{w_j s_i}, (g_2^z)^{s_i}).$$

Challenge Ciphertext: To compute the challenge ciphertext, the reduction samples  $j^* \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} [\ell]$  and  $t_0, t_1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_p$ . It embeds the ABDDH tuple by setting

$$c_0 \leftarrow (g_2^{t_0}, (g_1^{yr})^{w_{j^*}t_0}) \text{ and } c_1 \leftarrow (g_2^{t_1}, (g_1^{zt})^{w_{j^*}t_1})$$

and assembling  $c^* \leftarrow (c_0, c_1)$ . The reduction also embeds the ABDDH challenge in the KEM key and sets  $k^* \leftarrow T$ .

The reduction runs  $\mathcal{A}(c^*, k^*, \mathsf{pk}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in \mathsf{Cor}})$ . Note that if the ABDDH challenge  $T = e(g_1, g_2)^{\alpha yzr}$ , then  $k^*$  corresponds to  $k_1$  and  $\mathcal{B}$  perfectly simulates Game-IND-BT-CPA for b = 1 to  $\mathcal{A}$ . If, however,  $T = e(g_1, g_2)^v$  for a random  $v \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , the  $k^*$  corresponds to  $k_0$  and  $\mathcal{B}$  perfectly simulates Game-IND-BT-CPA for b = 0 to  $\mathcal{A}$ . Hence, it holds that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Game\text{-}IND\text{-}BT\text{-}CPA}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) = 1] - \frac{1}{2} \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{abddh}_{\mathcal{B},\mathcal{G}}(1^{\lambda}),$$

which is negligible by assumption.

#### <span id="page-50-0"></span>10.3 From PD-BT-KEM to Traceability with Native Partial Insider Resilience

In this section, we present a transformation from PD-BT-KEM to a TT-KEM that is *natively* resilient against t-1 partial insiders. There is, however, one caveat: We can only guarantee partial insider resilience against *decrypting decoders*, i.e., decoders that do not just distinguish whether a given ciphertext-key pair (k,c) is correct, but instead, given a ciphertext c, output the corresponding KEM-key k. To this end, we adjust the traceability definition (Definition 4.1) to instead use the following version of the event GoodDec within this section:

**Definition 10.10** (GoodDec for Decrypting Decoders (GoodDDec)). For  $\varepsilon \in (0,1]$ , a TT-KEM  $\Pi$ , a public key  $\mathsf{pk}$ , secret keys  $\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in [n]}$ , and a set of partial insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \subseteq [n]$ , we say that a decrypting decoder D is good (i.e.,  $\mathsf{GoodDDec} = 1$ ), exactly if  $P^{\mathsf{Dec}}(D) \geq \varepsilon$ , where

$$P^{\mathsf{Dec}}(D) \coloneqq \Pr \left[ D(c, \left\{ d_i \right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}) = k \colon \begin{array}{c} (k, c) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}) \\ \forall i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \colon d_i \leftarrow \mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i, c) \end{array} \right].$$

{51}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-51-2"></span>Similar to [\[12\]](#page-63-4), our transformation requires a collusion-resistant fingerprinting code.

**Definition 10.11** (Noisy Words and Feasibility). For *ℓ* ∈ N, a word of length *ℓ* is a binary string *w* ∈ {0*,* 1} *ℓ* . For *j* ∈ [*ℓ*], we denote with *w<sup>j</sup>* the *j*'th bit of a word *w*. A *noisy* word is a ternary string *w* ∈ {0*,* 1*,* '?'}. For a set of words *W* = {*w* (1)*, . . . w*(*f*)}, we say that a noisy word *w* is feasible for *W*, if for all *j* ∈ [*ℓ*] either *w<sup>j</sup>* = '?' or there exists some *i* ∈ [*f*] such that *w<sup>j</sup>* = *w* (*i*) *j* . Denote with *F*(*W*) the set of words that is feasible for *W*. For 0 ≤ *δ* ≤ 1, we say that a word is *δ*-noisy or at most *δ*-noisy, if it contains '?' in at most *δ* · *ℓ* positions. We denote with *Fδ*(*W*) = {*w* ∈ *F*(*W*): *w* is *δ*-noisy} the set of words that are at most *δ*-noisy and feasible for *W*.

<span id="page-51-1"></span>

| Game-FCA(1λ<br>, n, δ)                                          | Oracle Ocodeword(i)       |
|-----------------------------------------------------------------|---------------------------|
| Cor ← ∅                                                         | if i ̸∈ [n] then return ⊥ |
| (i)<br>←\$ Gen(1λ<br><br>, ℓ,tk<br>Γ = {cw<br>}i∈[n]<br>, n, δ) | Cor ← Cor ∪ {i}           |
| w ← A\$ Ocodeword<br>(1λ<br>, n, δ)                             | (i)<br>return cw          |
| (i)<br>if w ̸∈ Fδ({cw<br>}i∈Cor) then return 0                  |                           |
| S ← Trace(tk, w)                                                |                           |
| if S = ∅ ∨ S ̸⊆ Cor then return 1                               |                           |
| return 0                                                        |                           |

Figure 10: Full Collusion-Resistance experiment for fingerprinting codes

**Definition 10.12** (Collusion-Resistant Fingerprinting Codes). A fingerprinting code F is a tuple of algorithms (Gen*,*Trace) with the following syntax:

- (Γ := {cw(*i*)}*i*∈[*n*] *, ℓ,*tk) ←\$ Gen(1*<sup>λ</sup> , n, δ*)*.* Gen is a code generation algorithm that receives the security parameter *λ*, the number of words *n >* 0 and a noise bound *δ*. It outputs a set of *n* words Γ = {cw(*i*)}*i*∈[*n*] , the codeword-length *ℓ*, and a tracing key tk, such that cw(*i*) ∈ {0*,* 1} *ℓ* , where *ℓ*(*λ, n, δ*) is a function of *λ*, *δ*, and *n*.
- *S* ← Trace(tk*, w*). Given the tracing key tk and a noisy word *w*, the tracing algorithm outputs a subset *S* ⊆ [*n*].

We say that a fingerprinting code F is fully collusion-resistant if there exists a negligible function negl(*λ*) such that for all PPT adversaries A, all *λ, n* ∈ N, and all *δ* ∈ [0*,* 1] it holds that

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Game}\text{-}\mathsf{FC}_{\mathcal{A}}^{\mathcal{F}}(1^{\lambda},n,\delta)=1\right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where Game-FC is defined in Figure [10.](#page-51-1)

<span id="page-51-0"></span>**Construction 8.** Let F be a fingerprinting code and ΠPD-BT be a PD-BT-KEM scheme. The resulting TT-KEM scheme Π is constructed as follows.

Π*.*KGen(1*<sup>λ</sup> , n, t, ε* ≥ 2*/* √ *λ*) : Let *δ* ← (1 − *ε*)*/*(1 − <sup>√</sup> 2 *λ* ) and sample a fingerprinting code (Γ*, ℓ,*tk) ←\$ F*.*Gen(1*<sup>λ</sup> , n, δ*). Generate PD-BT-KEM keys as

$$\left(\mathsf{pk}, \{(\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)}, \mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)})\}_{i \in [n], j \in [\ell]}\right) \xleftarrow{\$} \Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}.\mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda}, n, t, \ell)$$

and assign secret keys according to parties'codewords in Γ: For all *i* ∈ [*n*] assign sk*<sup>i</sup>* ← sk(*j*) *i,*cw (*i*) *j j*∈[*ℓ*] . Output (pk*,* {sk*i*}*i*∈[*n*] *,*tk).

Π*.*Enc(pk)**:** Sample *j* ←\$ [*ℓ*] and encrypt (*k,*(*c*0*, c*1)) ←\$ ΠPD-BT*.*Enc(pk*, j*). Output (*k,*((*c*0*, c*1)*, j*).

$$\Pi.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i, ((c_0, c_1), j))$$
: Output  $d_i \leftarrow \Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}.\mathsf{PDec}\left(\mathsf{sk}_{i, \mathsf{cw}_j^{(i)}}^{(j)}, (c_0, c_1), j\right)$ .

{52}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-52-1"></span>Π*.*Combine(*S,* {*di*}*i*∈*<sup>S</sup> ,*((*c*0*, c*1)*, j*))**:** Compute and output

$$k \leftarrow \Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}.\mathsf{Combine}(S, \left\{d_i\right\}_{i \in S}, ((c_0, c_1), j)).$$

Tr*D*(pk*, j, Q,* Ipd*, b*)**:** We define the subroutine Tr used in the tracing algorithm. Initialize a counter *ctr* ← 0 and repeat *Q* times:

- Sample *r*Enc ← R\$ Enc.
- Compute (*k* (0) *,*(*c* (0) 0 *, c* (0) 1 )) ← ΠPD-BT*.*Enc(pk*, j*; *r*Enc).
- Sample (*k* (1) *,*(*c* (1) 0 *, c* (1) 1 )) ←\$ ΠPD-BT*.*Enc(pk*, j*).
- Assemble *c* <sup>∗</sup> ← (*c* (0) 0 *, c* (*b*) 1 ).
- Compute {*d<sup>i</sup>* ←\$ ΠPD-BT*.*SimPD(pk*, i, c*<sup>∗</sup> *, j, r*Enc)}*i*∈Ipd .
- If *D*(*c* ∗ *,* {*di*}*i*∈Ipd ) = *k* (0), increment *ctr* ← *ctr* + 1.

After *Q* iterations, output *ctr*.

Π*.*Trace*D*(pk*,*tk*, ε,* Ipd)**:** If Ipd ̸⊆ [*n*] or |Ipd| ≥ *t*, abort and output ∅. Otherwise, continue as follows: Set *Q* ← 8*λ* 2 . Initialize an *ℓ*-length word *w*. For all *j* ∈ [*ℓ*], do:

- Compute *p<sup>j</sup>* ←\$ Tr*D*(pk*, j, Q,* 0*,* Ipd).
- Compute *q<sup>j</sup>* ←\$ Tr*<sup>D</sup>*(pk*, j, Q,* 1*,* Ipd).
- Assign the *j*'th position of the word *w* as

$$w_j \leftarrow \begin{cases} 0 & \text{if } q_j \neq 0, \\ 1 & \text{if } q_j = 0 \text{ and } p_j/Q \geq 1/\sqrt{\lambda}, \\ ? & \text{else.} \end{cases}$$

Finally, if *w* is not *δ*-noisy output T ′ ← ∅. Else, compute T ′ ← F \$ *.*Trace(tk*, w*). If T ′ ̸= ∅, output T ′ . Else, output [*n*].

**Checking for Empty Traces.** Observe that the Trace algorithm of Construction [8](#page-51-0) only ever outputs T ′ = ∅ if the word *w* produced during tracing is not *δ*-noisy. Hence, checking whether tracing a given decoder *D* returns an empty set, and thus simulating OTrace-Empty only requires one to compute *w* and check whether it is *δ*-noisy or not. Crucially, this can be done *without* access to the tracing key tk.

<span id="page-52-0"></span>**Theorem 10.13** *If the underlying* PD-BT-KEM *is two-sided correct, has simulable partial decryptions, is OSS-SPD secure, and semantically secure, then Construction [8](#page-51-0) is a perfect* 1*-traceable* TT-KEM *against t* − 1 *partial insiders with respect to decrypting decoders with limit ε* ≥ 2*/* √ *λ. Traceability holds in the presence of an* OTrace-Empty *and an* OPDec-Hon *oracle.*

*Remark 10.14* (Adaptive Partial Insider Resilience). Interestingly, we can even prove resilience against *adaptive* partial insiders: I.e., we can prove partial insider resilience in a model, where the adversary is not required to fix Ipd once initially, but where the decoder can adaptively ask for a set Ipd of its choice in every query after seeing the queried ciphertext *c*. This is possible because the left and right OSS-SPD security games allow our reduction to query adaptively for a set Ipd, on behalf of which to obtain simulated partial decryptions, *after seeing the challenge ciphertext*. We believe that properly formalizing and exploring adaptive partial insider resilience is a very interesting direction for future work, especially when motivated by the context-dependent scenario [\[7\]](#page-63-3), where some honest parties might regularly release partial decryptions.

Since we only modified the Trace algorithm, and did not modify correctness or semantic security requirements of PD-BT-KEM compared to standard BT-KEM, we refer to [\[12\]](#page-63-4) for proofs of correctness, robustness, and semantic security. Next, we prove 1-traceability against *t* − 1 partial insiders.

{53}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-53-0"></span>10.4 Traceability Proof for Construction 8

Proof of Theorem 10.13. To prove 1-traceability against t-1 partial insiders of Construction 8, we proceed in several steps, which we introduce next on a high level.

First, we will modify the experiment underpinning the event GoodDDec, replacing honestly computed partial decryptions with partial decryptions generated by the SimPD algorithm of the underlying PD-BT-KEM, which, for honestly generated ciphertexts, are identical to normal partial decryptions (Definition 10.3). Hence, in the following steps, we can assume that the decoder is good with respect to simulated partial decryptions.

Then we will argue that any decoder that is at least  $\varepsilon$ -good for  $\varepsilon \geq 2/\sqrt{\lambda}$ , must also be at least  $2/\sqrt{\lambda}$ -good for at least a fraction  $(1-\delta)$  of positions  $j \in [\ell]$ , where  $\delta$  is chosen as in Construction 8. This allows us to argue that whenever the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  produces a good decoder, the word w produced by the Trace algorithm can have a '?' in at most a fraction  $\delta$  of positions (i.e., the produced word is at most  $\delta$ -noisy). This holds because the Trace algorithm only ever sets  $w_j \leftarrow$ ? if the decoder fails to correctly decrypt at least a fraction of  $1/\sqrt{\lambda}$  of honestly generated ciphertexts  $((p/Q) \geq 1/\sqrt{\lambda})$ , and any decoder that is good for a given position j can only fail to do so with negligible probability. This essentially demonstrates that the word w produced by Trace is sufficiently noiseless for tracing using the underlying fingerprinting code.

Finally, we must show that the word w is feasible for the set of traitors corrupted by the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . We do so in two steps: First, we show by reduction to Right-SPD security of the underlying PD-BT-KEM that whenever the traitors have  $only\ right\ keys$  for a position  $j\in[\ell]$ , they cannot trick the Trace algorithm to set  $w_j\leftarrow 0$ . Then, by reduction to Left-SPD security of the PD-BT-KEM, we show that, vice-versa, if the traitors have  $only\ left\ keys$  for a position  $j\in[\ell]$  they cannot trick the Trace algorithm to set  $w_j\leftarrow 1$ . Note that in the reduction to both properties, the reduction receives simulated partial decryptions for the challenge ciphertext on behalf of the set  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ , which allows it to simulate tracing as required by the Tr subroutine.

By the above steps, it must hold that whenever the adversary produces a good decoder, the word produced by Trace is (a) at most  $\delta$ -noisy, and (b) must be feasible for the set of traitors corrupted by the adversary, and thus the tracing procedure of the fingerprinting code will identify at least one traitor. Crucially, this holds despite the fact that the adversary obtains access to the  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$  oracle prior to tracing, and that the decoder receives partial decryptions for up to t-1 partial insiders. In the following, we formalize the approach outlined above.

Similar to the event GoodDDec, for a given tuple  $(pk, D, \mathcal{I}_{pd}, \{sk_i\}_{i \in [\mathcal{I}_{pd}]})$ , define the event GoodDecSim to be 1, exactly if  $P^{\mathsf{Sim}}(D) \geq \varepsilon$ , where  $P^{\mathsf{Sim}}(D)$  is defined as

$$\Pr \left[ D(((c_0, c_1), j), \left\{d_i\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}) = k \colon \begin{array}{c} j \not\leftarrow^{\$} [\ell]; \ r_{\mathsf{Enc}} \not\leftarrow^{\$} \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{Enc}} \\ (k, (c_0, c_1)) \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, j; r_{\mathsf{Enc}}) \\ \forall i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \colon \\ d_i \leftarrow \mathsf{SimPD}(\mathsf{pk}, i, (c_0, c_1), j, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}) \end{array} \right],$$

and Enc and SimPD refer to the algorithms of  $\Pi_{PD\text{-BT}}$ . Note that due to the perfect partial decryption simulability of PD-BT-KEM (Definition 10.3), it holds that the events GoodDDec and GoodDecSim are *identical*. Henceforth, we will consider traceability with respect to the event GoodDecSim instead of GoodDDec.

Next, we argue that tracing any  $\varepsilon$ -good decoder will produce a word w that is at most  $\delta$ -noisy (i.e., has  $w_j = ?$  in at most  $\delta \ell$  positions  $j \in [\ell]$ ). Denote with  $w(\mathcal{A})$  the random variable for the word produced in the Game-Tr experiment instantiated with Construction 8 against a given adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . For a word w, denote with  $\delta$ -noisy(w) the event that w is at most  $\delta$ -noisy.

<span id="page-53-1"></span>Lemma 10.15 For all PPT adversaries A, it holds that

$$\Pr[\neg \delta \text{-noisy}(w(\mathcal{A})) | \mathsf{GoodDecSim}_{\mathcal{A}}]] \le \ell \cdot \exp(-\lambda)$$

Proof of Lemma 10.15. First, we argue that any  $\varepsilon$ -good decoder must also be "at least  $(2/\sqrt{\lambda})$ -good for at least a fraction  $(1-\delta)$  of positions  $j \in [\ell]$ ". Fix any  $\varepsilon \geq 2/\sqrt{\lambda}$  and let  $\delta = (1-\varepsilon)/(1-\frac{2}{\lambda})$ . We say that a decoder D is good for a position j, if  $P_j^{\mathsf{Sim}}(D) \geq 2/\sqrt{\lambda}$ , where  $P_j^{\mathsf{Sim}}$  is the same as  $P^{\mathsf{Sim}}$  except that we fix j.

{54}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-54-0"></span>Claim 10.16 For all decoders D with  $P^{\mathsf{Sim}}(D) \geq \varepsilon$ , there must exist at least  $(1 - \delta) \cdot \ell$  positions  $j \in [\ell]$  such that  $P_j^{\mathsf{Sim}} \geq 2/\sqrt{\lambda}$ .

Proof of Lemma 10.16. Assume towards contradiction that  $P_j^{\mathsf{Sim}}(D) < 2/\sqrt{\lambda}$  for a fraction of more than  $\delta$  positions  $j \in [\ell]$ . In this case, we can bound

$$\begin{split} P^{\mathsf{Sim}}(D) &< \delta \cdot \frac{2}{\sqrt{n}} + (1 - \delta) \cdot 1 \\ &= 1 + \delta \cdot \left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{\lambda}} - 1\right) \\ &= 1 + \left(\frac{\varepsilon - 1}{\frac{2}{\sqrt{\lambda}} - 1}\right) \cdot \left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{\lambda}} - 1\right) \\ &= 1 + \varepsilon - 1 \\ &= \varepsilon, \end{split}$$

which contradicts  $P^{\mathsf{Sim}}(D) \geq \varepsilon$ .

Equipped with Claim 10.16, we inspect how w is computed in the tracing procedure. In particular, observe that  $w_j$  is only ever set to '?' whenever  $p_j/Q < 1/\sqrt{\lambda}$ , where  $p_j$  is the result of the p-experiment in the j-th iteration of Trace  $(p_j \leftarrow \operatorname{Tr}^D(\operatorname{pk}, j, Q, 0))$ , which is essentially counting the number of successful decryptions by the decoder, when run in an experiment identical to that of  $P_j^{\operatorname{Sim}}$  across Q repetitions. Next, we show that for any index  $j \in [\ell]$  where the decoder is good (i.e.,  $P_j^{\operatorname{Sim}}(D) \geq 2/\sqrt{\lambda}$ ), we can bound the probability of  $p_j/Q < 1/\sqrt{\lambda}$  occurring to be negligible by an appropriate choice of Q.

<span id="page-54-1"></span>Claim 10.17 For all  $j \in [\ell]$  and all decoders D with  $P_j^{\mathsf{Sim}}(D) \geq 2/\sqrt{\lambda}$  it holds that

$$\Pr[p_j/Q < 1/\sqrt{\lambda}] \le \exp(-Q/4\sqrt{\lambda}).$$

Proof of Claim 10.17. The proof follows a simple Chernoff bound.

To summarize, we know that all  $\varepsilon$ -good decoders must be good for at least  $(1 - \delta)$  positions, and we will only set  $w_j = ?$  for a good position with probability at most  $\exp(-Q/4\sqrt{\lambda})$ . We apply a union bound over the maximum number of good positions  $\ell$  and conclude that

$$\Pr\left[\neg \delta\text{-noisy}(w(\mathcal{A}))|\mathsf{GoodDecSim}\right] \leq \ell \cdot \exp\left(\frac{-Q}{4\sqrt{\lambda}}\right) \overset{Q \geq 4\lambda^{3/2}}{\leq} \ell \cdot \exp(-\lambda).$$

Next, we use game hops to make two modifications to the Tr subroutine executed during tracing in Game-Tr. We call the modified Tr subroutines Tr' and Tr" respectively. Similar to  $w(\mathcal{A})$ , we denote with  $w'(\mathcal{A})$  and  $w''(\mathcal{A})$  the random variable for the word produced when tracing  $\mathcal{A}$  making use of Tr' and Tr" respectively. Further, denote with the random variable  $F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})$  the set of words that are feasible for the set of traitors  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$  corrupted by  $\mathcal{A}$  with respect to their codewords  $\{w^{(i)}\}_{i\in\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}}$ . We will show that our modifications do not noticeably decrease the probability that  $\mathcal{A}$  manages to cause the tracing algorithm to produce a codeword that is not feasible for  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$ . More formally, we will show that by reduction to SPD-security of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}$  that

$$\Pr[w(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] - \Pr[w'(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \le \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

as well as

$$\Pr[w'(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] - \Pr[w''(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \le \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Before we introduce our modifications in more detail, recall that for all positions j, the tracing algorithm runs the Tr subroutine *twice*. Once with input b = 0, testing how many *valid* ciphertexts the decoder decrypts correctly, and assigning the result to  $p_j$ , and once with input b = 1, testing how many

{55}------------------------------------------------

invalid ciphertexts of the form  $(c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)})$  the decoder decrypts to  $k^{(0)}$ , assigning the result to  $q_j$ . In both experiments, the decoder receives simulated partial decryptions for its respective ciphertexts.

Subroutine Tr': In this step, we modify the subroutine Tr when executed in Game-Tr for b = 1: Upon input (pk, j, Q, b), if b = 1 and all traitors  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$  have a right key for position j (i.e.,  $\mathsf{cw}_{j}^{(i)} = 1$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$ ), set  $ctr = q_{j} = 0$ . This change essentially enforces  $q_{j} = 0$  for all positions j where all traitors have right keys, which prevents us from ever setting  $w_{j} = 0$  (indicating that traitors would have (some) left keys in position j) whenever traitors have only right keys in position j. The changes are laid out in detail in Figure 11.

<span id="page-55-0"></span>

Figure 11: The Tr and Tr' subroutine for b = 1. The algorithms Enc and SimPD are invoked with respect to  $\Pi_{PD-BT}$ . Modifications are highlighted in gray.

<span id="page-55-1"></span>Claim 10.18 For all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  such that

$$\Pr[w(\mathcal{A}) \not \in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] - \Pr[w'(\mathcal{A}) \not \in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \leq \ell \cdot Q \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Right}}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda}).$$

Proof of Claim 10.18. Fix an arbitrary PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  against Game-Tr. We construct a reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  that uses  $\mathcal{A}$  to win against Game-OSS-Right instantiated with  $\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}$ . Initially,  $\mathcal{B}$  chooses a random  $u \in [\ell]$  and outputs  $(\ell, u)$  to its challenger. As a result,  $\mathcal{B}$  receives a public key  $\mathsf{pk}$  as well as  $\mathit{all}$  secret keys except for the left keys in position u:

$$\mathsf{shares} = \left( \left\{ (\mathsf{sk}_{i,0}^{(j)}, \mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)}) \right\}_{i \in [n], j \in [\ell] \backslash \{u\}}, \left\{ \mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(u)} \right\}_{i \in [n]} \right)$$

 $\mathcal{B}$  generates the code  $\Gamma = \{\mathsf{cw}^{(i)}\}_{i \in [n]}$  and runs  $\mathcal{A}(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{pk})^{\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}, \mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}, \mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}}$ , simulating the oracles as follows:

Simulating  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}(i)$ :  $\mathcal{B}$  checks whether party i is supposed to have a left key in position u according to  $\Gamma$  (i.e, whether  $\mathsf{cw}_u^{(i)} = 0$ ). If this is the case,  $\mathcal{B}$  aborts and returns a random group element to its challenger. Otherwise,  $\mathcal{B}$  knows all secret keys for i and forwards  $\left\{\mathsf{sk}_{i,\mathsf{cw}_j^{(i)}}^{(j)}\right\}_{i\in[\ell]}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

Simulating  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}(i, r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ :  $\mathcal{B}$  parses  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  as  $(j, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}})$  and computes  $(k, (c_0, c_1)) \leftarrow \Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, j; r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ . Then it computes and returns  $d_i \leftarrow \Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)}, (c_0, c_1), j)$ . Note that  $\mathcal{B}$  can compute this, because it knows all right keys  $\mathsf{sk}_{i,1}^{(j)}$  for all  $i \in [n]$  and  $j \in [\ell]$ . Further, note that  $\mathcal{B}$  always uses a right key for simulating partial decryption, even if party i is supposed to have a left key for index j. Nevertheless,  $\mathcal{B}$ 's simulation is perfect, because of the equality-of-partial-decryptions property of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}$  (Definition 10.3), which states that left and right partial decryptions are equal for honestly generated ciphertexts.

{56}------------------------------------------------

Simulating  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}(\widetilde{D}, \widetilde{\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}})$ : To simulate  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  runs  $\mathsf{Trace}^{\widetilde{D}}(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{tk}, \varepsilon, \widetilde{\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}})$  up to learning the word  $\widetilde{w}$ . If  $\widetilde{w}$  is not  $\delta$ -noisy,  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs 1, otherwise,  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs 0.

As a result,  $\mathcal{B}$  receives a decoder D and a set of insiders  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ . W.l.o.g., assume that  $\mathcal{I}_{pd} \subseteq [n]$  and  $|\mathcal{I}_{pd}| < t$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  forwards  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$  to its challenger and, as a result, receives a challenge ciphertext  $c^* = (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)})$  as well as a set of simulated partial decryptions  $\{d_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}}$ . Finally,  $\mathcal{B}$  queries  $D((c^*, u), \{d_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}})$  to receive a key k, which  $\mathcal{B}$  returns to its challenger.

**Analysis:** Observe that  $w(\mathcal{A}) \notin F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})$  but  $w'(\mathcal{A}) \in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})$  can only ever happen, if the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  triggers the modified condition, i.e. if there exists an index  $j \in [\ell]$  such that  $\mathsf{cw}_j^{(i)} = 1$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$ , for which  $q_j$  is not already 0 with respect to Tr. That means, if  $\mathcal{A}$  distinguishes, it must happen that in at least one out of the Q iterations of Tr that

<span id="page-56-0"></span>
$$D\left((c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)}, \left\{d_i \leftarrow \mathsf{SimPD}(\mathsf{pk}, i, (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(1)}), j, r_{\mathsf{Enc}})\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\right) = k^{(0)},\tag{4}$$

where  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}} \not \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{Enc}}$ ,  $(k^{(0)}, (c_0^{(0)}, c_1^{(0)})) \leftarrow \mathsf{PD-BT-KEM.Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, j; r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ , as well as  $(k^{(1)}, (c_0^{(1)}, c_1^{(1)})) \not \in \mathsf{PD-BT-KEM.Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, j)$ . In this case,  $\mathcal{B}$  will guess u to be this j with probability at least  $1/\ell$ . Further,  $\mathcal{B}$  will not abort (due to inability to simulate  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}$ ), since, by definition, all traitors in  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$  must have right keys. Finally, when  $\mathcal{B}$  queries the decoder D on its challenge ciphertext and the corresponding simulated partial decryptions obtained by its  $\mathsf{Game-OSS-Right}$  challenger, he simulates exactly the experiment in the above Equation 4 and wins, whenever the decoder succeeds in its experiment. As a result, we can bound

$$\Pr[w(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] - \Pr[w'(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \leq \ell \cdot Q \cdot \Pr\left[\mathsf{Game\text{-}OSS\text{-}Right}_{\mathcal{B}}^{\Pi_{\mathsf{PD\text{-}BT}}}(1^{\lambda}) = 1\right].$$

Subroutine Tr": In this step, we add an additional modification to the Tr' subroutine. Upon input (pk, j, Q, b), if b = 0 and all traitors  $\mathcal{T}_A$  have a left key for position j (i.e.,  $\mathsf{cw}_j^{(i)} = 0$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{T}_A$ ), we change the experiment to be equivalent to that of b = 1. In particular, if the above condition is met, we query the adversary with *invalid* ciphertexts instead of valid ones to determine  $p_j$ . Intuitively, this prevents us from setting  $w_j \leftarrow 1$  for some j, where all traitors have left keys. To see this, observe that we only ever set  $w_j \leftarrow 1$ , whenever  $q_j = 0$  and  $p_j/Q \ge 1/\sqrt{\lambda}$ . In Tr", however,  $q_j$  and  $p_j$  are derived from an identical experiment, whenever all traitors have left keys. Consequently, we will argue that the results of the experiments can only differ by that much with negligible probability for an appropriate choice of the number of repetitions Q. A detailed description of our modifications is shown in Figure 12.

<span id="page-56-1"></span>Claim 10.19 For all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal A$  there exists a PPT reduction  $\mathcal B$  such that

$$\Pr[w'(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] - \Pr[w''(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \leq \ell \cdot Q \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left}}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Intuitively, the claim follows from Left-SPD security of  $\Pi_{PD-BT}$ , because an adversary with only left keys should not be able to distinguish valid ciphertexts from ciphertexts with a malformed right component, even in the presence of simulated partial decryptions. A formal proof, however, is significantly more involved.

Proof of Claim 10.19. First, we introduce a variant of Game-OSS-Left that will simplify the upcoming analysis. Denote with Game-OSS-Left-Q an experiment that is identical to Game-OSS-Left, except that it returns Q challenge ciphertexts instead of one. A simple hybrid argument shows that for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left-}Q}_{\mathcal{A},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^\lambda) \leq Q \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left}}_{\mathcal{A},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^\lambda).$$

In the following, we will reduce Claim 10.19 to Game-OSS-Left-Q-security of  $\Pi_{PD-BT}$ .

Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be an arbitrary PPT adversary against Game-Tr. Denote with  $p'_j(\mathcal{A})$  the random variable representing  $p_j$  as it is computed, when  $\mathcal{A}$  is run in Game-Tr with respect to Tr'. Similarly, denote with  $p''_j(\mathcal{A})$  the random variable representing  $p_j$  as it is computed, when  $\mathcal{A}$  is run in Game-Tr with respect to Tr''. Henceforth, we say that a position  $j \in [\ell]$  is relevant, if the adversary hits the modified condition in

{57}------------------------------------------------

```
 \begin{array}{llllllllllllllllllllllllllllllllllll
```

Figure 12: The Tr' and Tr'' subroutine for b = 0. The algorithms Enc and SimPD are invoked with respect to  $\Pi_{PD-BT}$ . Modifications are highlighted in gray.

 $\mathsf{Tr}''$ , i.e., all traitors have left keys  $(\mathsf{cw}_j^{(i)} = 0 \text{ for all } i \in \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})$  at position j. Now, observe that the event that  $w'(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}(\mathcal{A}))$ , but  $w''(\mathcal{A}) \in F(\mathcal{T}(\mathcal{A}))$  can only ever happen if there exists a relevant position  $j \in [\ell]$  such that all of the following conditions are met.

- $q_j(A) = 0$ , since otherwise  $w_j$  would be set to 0 in both Tr' and Tr''. Recall that  $q_j$  counts successful decryptions when queried with invalid ciphertexts for index j.
- $p'_j(\mathcal{A})/Q \geq 1/\sqrt{\lambda}$ , since otherwise  $w'_j(\mathcal{A})$  would be set to '?', which implies that position j is irrelevant for the infeasibility of  $w'(\mathcal{A})$ . Recall that  $p'_j$  counts successful decryptions when queried with valid ciphertexts for index j.
- $p_j''(\mathcal{A})/Q < 1/\sqrt{\lambda}$ , since otherwise  $w_j''(\mathcal{A})$  would be set to 1, which implies that  $w''(\mathcal{A}) \notin F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})$ . Recall that  $p_j''$  counts successful decryptions when queried with invalid ciphertexts for index j.

Note that the first case implies the third case, except with negligible probability. I.e.,  $q_j(A) = 0$  but  $p_j''(A)/Q \ge 1/\sqrt{\lambda}$  can only happen with negligible probability for an appropriate choice of Q since the experiments  $q_j$  and  $p_j''$  are identical for relevant positions.

Denote with Hit the event that a relevant j exists that meets all three of the conditions above. By the above argument, it holds that

$$\Pr[w'(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] - \Pr[w''(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \le \Pr[\mathsf{Hit}_{\mathcal{A}}].$$

With this in mind, we construct a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  that internally runs  $\mathcal{A}$  to win Game-OSS-Left-Q of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}$ . The reduction samples a random index u, generates a code  $\Gamma$ , and embeds the public key  $\mathsf{pk}$  it receives from its challenger. It simulates the corruption, partial decryption, and empty-trace oracles to  $\mathcal{A}$  similarly to the reduction in the previous proof, except that when simulating the  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}(i)$  oracle,  $\mathcal{B}$  now aborts and outputs a random bit  $\beta'$  to its challenger, whenever  $\mathcal{A}$  queries a party that has a right key at position u ( $\mathsf{cw}_u^{(i)} = 1$ ). When  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs a decoder D alongside a set  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}} \subseteq [n]$  with  $|\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}| < t$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  forwards  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}$  to its challenger and receives a set of Q challenges  $\{(k_v^{(0)}, c_v^*, \{d_{i,v}\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\}_{v \in [Q]}\}$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  is tasked to guess the internal bit  $\beta$  of Game-OSS-Left-Q, i.e., whether the challenge ciphertexts are either all valid ( $\beta = 0$ ) or all invalid ( $\beta = 1$ ). Observe that if  $\beta = 0$ , the challenge is distributed identical to the experiments  $q_j(\mathcal{A})$  (i.e., valid ciphertexts), and if  $\beta = 1$  the challenge is distributed identical to the experiments  $q_j(\mathcal{A})$  and  $p_j''(\mathcal{A})$  (i.e., invalid ciphertexts).

{58}------------------------------------------------

First,  $\mathcal{B}$  computes  $q_j(\mathcal{A})$  as well as  $p'_j(\mathcal{A})$ , by running the respective experiments on D. If either  $q_j(\mathcal{A}) \neq 0$  or  $p'_j(\mathcal{A})/Q < 1/\sqrt{\lambda}$ , the reduction aborts and outputs a random bit  $\beta'$ . As a result, if  $\mathcal{B}$  does not abort, then  $\mathsf{Hit}_{\mathcal{A}}$  happens.

In this case,  $\mathcal{B}$  queries the decoder D on its Q challenge tuples and counts for how many  $v \in [Q]$  it holds that

 $D\left(c_v^*, \left\{d_{i,v}\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}}\right) = k_v^{(0)}.$ 

Let  $p^*(A)$  denote that count.

Finally,  $\mathcal{B}$  returns  $\beta' = 1$ , if  $p^*(\mathcal{A})/Q < 1/2\sqrt{\lambda}$ , i.e., if the challenge is closer to the experiment result  $q_j(\mathcal{A})$  with *invalid* ciphertexts, and  $\beta' = 0$  otherwise, i.e. if  $p^*(\mathcal{A})/Q \ge 1/2\sqrt{\lambda}$ , which indicates that the challenge is closer to the experiment result  $p_j'(\mathcal{A})$  with *valid* ciphertexts.

<span id="page-58-0"></span>Claim 10.20 If  $\beta = 1$ , it can only happen with negligible probability that  $q_j(A) = 0$ , and  $p^*(A)/Q \ge 1/2\sqrt{\lambda}$ .

Proof of Claim 10.20. Suppose that  $\beta=1$ . In this case,  $q_j$  and  $p^*$  are identically distributed experiments. For  $v\in [Q]$ , denote with the random variables  $X_v$  and  $Y_v$  the results in the v'th iteration of the experiment  $p^*$  and  $q_j$ , respectively (i.e.,  $X_v=1$ , if the decoder decrypts successfully in the v'th iteration of  $p^*$  and  $X_v=0$  otherwise). Also, let  $Z_v=X_v-Y_v$ . Observe that  $Z_v\in\{-1,0,1\}$ , all  $X_v$  and  $Z_v$  are i.i.d, and  $Z_v=\sum_{v=1}^Q Z_v=\sum_{v=1}^Q (X_v-Y_v)=q_j-p^*$ , and thus  $\mathbb{E}[Z]=0$ . We are interested in the case  $q_j=0$  and  $p^*\geq Q/2\sqrt{\lambda}$ , which implies that  $|Z|\geq Q/2\sqrt{\lambda}$ . We can use Hoeffding's inequality to bound Z as the sum of bounded, independent random variables  $Z_v$ :

$$\Pr\left[q_{j}(\mathcal{A}) = 0 \land p^{*}(\mathcal{A}) \geq \frac{Q}{2\sqrt{\lambda}} \mid \beta = 1\right]$$

$$\leq \Pr\left[Z \geq \frac{Q}{2\sqrt{\lambda}}\right] = \Pr\left[\sum_{v=1}^{Q} Z_{v} \geq \frac{Q}{2\sqrt{\lambda}}\right]$$

$$\leq \exp\left(\frac{-Q^{2}}{8\lambda \cdot Q}\right)$$

$$\stackrel{Q \geq 8\lambda^{2}}{\leq} \exp(-\lambda)$$

A similar argument shows that, if  $\beta = 0$ , it can only happen with negligible probability that  $p^*(\mathcal{A}) < Q/2\sqrt{\lambda}$  and  $p'_j(\mathcal{A}) \ge Q/\sqrt{\lambda}$ . Putting it all together, we get that if  $\mathsf{Hit}_{\mathcal{A}}$  happens,  $\mathcal{B}$ 's guess u is a relevant index that meets the three conditions with probability at least  $1/\ell$ . If  $\mathsf{Hit}_{\mathcal{A}}$  does not happen, or  $\mathcal{B}$ 's guess is not relevant,  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs a random bit and guesses  $\beta$  with probability 1/2. Finally, if  $\mathsf{Hit}_{\mathcal{A}}$  happens and  $\mathcal{B}$ 's guess is relevant,  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses  $\beta$  except with probability bound by the claims above. Hence, we get that

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left-}Q}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda}) &= \Pr[\beta' = \beta] - \frac{1}{2} \\ &\geq (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot \frac{\Pr[\mathsf{Hit}_{\mathcal{A}}]}{\ell} + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left(1 - \frac{\Pr[\mathsf{Hit}_{\mathcal{A}}]}{\ell}\right) - \frac{1}{2} \\ &\geq \frac{(1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda))}{2\ell} \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{Hit}_{\mathcal{A}}] \\ &\geq \frac{1}{2\ell} \cdot (\Pr[w'(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] - \Pr[w''(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})]) - \mathsf{negl}'(\lambda), \end{split}$$

for appropriate negligible functions  $negl(\lambda)$  and negl'. Together with

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left-}Q}_{\mathcal{A},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda}) \leq Q \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left}}_{\mathcal{A},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda}),$$

we conclude that

$$\Pr[w'(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] - \Pr[w''(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \leq \ell \cdot Q \cdot \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left}}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

for a negligible function negl.

{59}------------------------------------------------

Taking into account our modifications to Tr, we next prove that  $w''(A) \notin F(\mathcal{T}_A)$  can only happen with negligible probability.

<span id="page-59-1"></span>Claim 10.21 For all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  it holds that

$$\Pr[w''(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \le \ell \cdot \exp(-\lambda).$$

Proof of Claim 10.21. By definition,  $w''(A) \notin F(\mathcal{T}_A)$  happens exactly if there exists a position  $j \in [\ell]$  where (a) all traitors hold left keys and  $w''_j(A) = 1$ , or (b) all traitors hold right keys and  $w''_j(A) = 0$ . Case (b) can clearly never happen since we always return  $q_j = 0$  in Tr'', for positions where all traitors hold right keys. Case (a), on the other hand, can only happen if  $q_j = 0$  and  $p''_j \ge Q/\sqrt{\lambda}$ . Note that by the nature of our modifications  $q_j$  and  $p''_j$  are identical experiments in case (a), hence we can make a very similar probabilistic argument as in the proof of Claim 10.20 to show that, for any given j, if all adversaries hold left keys

$$\Pr\left[q_j = 0 \ \land \ p_j'' \ge Q/\sqrt{\lambda}\right] \le \exp\left(-\frac{Q}{2\lambda}\right) \stackrel{Q \ge 2\lambda^2}{\le} \exp(-\lambda).$$

A union bound over all  $\ell$  positions completes the proof.

Applying Claims 10.18, 10.19, and 10.21 and simplifying negligibilities, we get that

$$\Pr[w(\mathcal{A}) \not \in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] \leq \ell \cdot Q \cdot \left(\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Right}}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left}}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda})\right) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

To conclude the proof, we apply full collusion resilience of the fingerprinting code. Full collusion resilience tells us that whenever tracing is run on a word that is feasible and at most  $\delta$ -noisy for a set S, the resulting set of traitors will be non-empty and a subset of S except with probability  $2^{-\lambda}$ . Note that, importantly, the reduction to the fingerprinting code needs to simulate the partial decryption oracle  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$  on behalf of non-traitors, for which it does not know the codeword (i.e., it does not know whether that party is supposed to have a left or a right key in a given position j, and hence it does not know what key to use). Due to the equality of partial decryptions property of the underlying PD-BT-KEM (Definition 10.3), however, the reduction can just issue a partial decryption using any key (i.e., honest partial decryptions do not leak the side of the key that was used). The  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{Trace-Empty}}(\widetilde{D}, \widetilde{\mathcal{I}}_{\mathsf{pd}})$  oracle can be simulated simply by computing the word  $\widetilde{w}$  for decoder  $\widetilde{D}$  and partial insiders  $\widehat{\mathcal{I}}_{\mathsf{pd}}$ , and outputting 1 if  $\widetilde{w}$  is not  $\delta$ -noisy.

Putting it all together, we can bound the advantage of any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  as follows

$$\begin{split} \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] & \leq \Pr[w(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] + 2^{-\lambda} \\ & \leq \ell \cdot Q \cdot \left(\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Right}}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left}}(1^{\lambda})\right) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda), \end{split}$$

which is negligible by assumption, as well as

 $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}]$ 

- $\geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDecSim}_{\mathcal{A}}] \Pr[\neg \delta \mathsf{-noisy}(w(\mathcal{A})) | \mathsf{GoodDecSim}_{\mathcal{A}}] \Pr[w(\mathcal{A}) \not\in F(\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}})] 2^{\lambda}$
- $\geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] \ell \cdot \exp(-\lambda) \ell \cdot Q \cdot \left(\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Right}}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Game-OSS-Left}}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi_{\mathsf{PD-BT}}}(1^{\lambda})\right) \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) 2^{-\lambda}$
- $\geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] \mathsf{negl}'(\lambda),$

which holds by assumption for an appropriate negligible function negl'.

# <span id="page-59-0"></span>11 Generic Threshold Traitor Tracing and Gap Elimination Compiler

In this section, we present a simple compiler that both formalizes the generic transformation from traceable broadcast to traceable threshold encryption, and serves to eliminate the gap between the security threshold t and the reconstruction threshold r of any ramp traceable threshold KEM, without

{60}------------------------------------------------

compromising traceability guarantees. This compiler is useful for the schemes presented in Sections 6, 8, and 9, which all introduce a gap.

The gap elimination compiler takes as input (1) a ramp TT-KEM that is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders for reconstruction thresholds  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$  with security threshold t(r) < r, and (2) any standard (non-traceable) threshold KEM TE without ramp. It constructs a scheme that offers the same traceability guarantees as  $\Pi$ , and has security threshold t'(r) = r. The compiler works by setting up both schemes for the desired reconstruction threshold r, but sets up TE with t = r. To encrypt, one performs encryption in both schemes  $(k^{\Pi}, c^{\Pi}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\Pi})$  and  $(k^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}})$ , and outputs  $(k \coloneqq k^{\Pi} \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}, c \coloneqq (c^{\Pi}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}))$ .

<span id="page-60-1"></span>Construction 9 (Gap Elimination Compiler). Let  $\Pi$  be a TT-KEM that supports reconstruction thresholds in  $\mathcal{S}_n \subseteq [n]$  and security thresholds t(r). Let TE be a *perfect* threshold KEM (Definition 3.11). Let the keyspaces  $\Pi.\mathcal{K} = \mathsf{TE}.\mathcal{K}$  of both schemes be equal. Then, the TT-KEM  $\Pi'$  for  $\mathcal{S}_n$  and security threshold t'(r) = r is constructed as follows:

 $\Pi'$ .KGen $(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t', \varepsilon)$ : Generate keys in both schemes as follows

$$\left(\mathsf{pk}^\Pi, \{\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{tk}\right) \xleftarrow{\$} \Pi.\mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, r, t(r), \varepsilon)$$

$$\left(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}}\}_{i \in [n]}\right) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t').$$

Set 
$$\mathsf{pk} \leftarrow \left(\mathsf{pk}^\Pi, \mathsf{pk}^\mathsf{TE}\right)$$
 and  $\mathsf{sk}_i \leftarrow (\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi, \mathsf{sk}_i^\mathsf{TE})$  for all  $i \in [n]$ . Output  $\left(\mathsf{pk}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{tk}\right)$ .

 $\Pi'$ . Enc(pk := (pk $^{\Pi}$ , pk $^{TE}$ ): Generate two ciphertexts

$$(k^{\Pi}, c^{\Pi}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\Pi}) \text{ and } (k^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}}).$$

Set 
$$k \leftarrow k^{\Pi} \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}$$
 and  $c \leftarrow (c^{\Pi}, c^{\mathsf{TE}})$ . Output  $(k, c)$ .

$$\Pi'.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i \coloneqq (\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi, \mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}}), c \coloneqq (c^\Pi, c^{\mathsf{TE}}))$$
: Compute

$$d_i^{\Pi} \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{\Pi}, c^{\Pi}) \text{ and } d_i^{\mathsf{TE}} \leftarrow \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}).$$

Output  $d_i \leftarrow (d_i^{\Pi}, d_i^{\mathsf{TE}})$ .

 $\Pi'$ .Combine $(S, d_i := \{(d_i^{\Pi}, d_i^{\mathsf{TE}})\}_{i \in S}, c := (c^{\Pi}, c^{\mathsf{TE}})$ : If |S| < r, abort. Else, compute

$$k^{\Pi} \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{Combine}(S, \{d_i^{\Pi}\}_{i \in S}, c^{\Pi}) \text{ and } k^{\mathsf{TE}} \leftarrow \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Combine}(S, \{d_i^{\mathsf{TE}}\}_{i \in S}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}).$$

Output  $k \leftarrow k^{\Pi} \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}$ .

 $\Pi'.\mathsf{Trace}^{D'(\cdot)}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{tk},\varepsilon,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}},\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}\cup\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}})\text{: First, compute a decoder }D\text{ making use of }D'\text{ as follows.}$  On input  $D\left(k^\Pi,c^\Pi,\{\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}},\{d_i^\Pi\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\right)$ 

- Generate  $(k^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}})$  and compute  $d_i^{\mathsf{TE}} \leftarrow \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}})$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}$ .
- $\bullet \quad \text{Output } D'\Big(k^\Pi \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}, (c^\Pi, c^{\mathsf{TE}}), \{(\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi, \mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}})\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}}, \{(d_i^\Pi, d_i^{\mathsf{TE}})\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\Big).$

Then, output  $\mathcal{T}' \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{Trace}^D(\mathsf{pk}^\Pi,\mathsf{tk},\varepsilon,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}},\{\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}\cup\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}).$ 

Note that the public key, secret key, partial decryption, and ciphertext size are the sum of those of  $\Pi$  and  $\mathsf{TE}$ , respectively.

<span id="page-60-0"></span>**Theorem 11.1** Let  $\Pi$  be a secure TT-KEM that is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders that is  $\mathsf{c}$ -robust and supports reconstruction thresholds  $\mathcal{S}_n \subseteq [n]$  with security threshold  $t(r) \leq r$ . Let  $\mathsf{TE}$  be a perfect T-KEM (Definition 3.11). Then, the TT-KEM  $\Pi'$  (Construction 9) is also semantically secure,  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders, and  $\mathsf{c}$ -robust. It supports all reconstruction thresholds  $\mathcal{S}_n$  with security threshold t'(r) = r.

{61}------------------------------------------------

Observe that a traceable broadcast encryption scheme can essentially be seen as a TT-KEM with  $S_n = [n]$ , t = 1, and 1-traceability against (0,0)-insiders. Hence, Theorem 11.1 also formalizes the result of the generic compiler introduced in Section 2.1 from traceable broadcast encryption to traceable threshold encryption, and we get the following Corollary.

<span id="page-61-1"></span>Corollary 11.2 Let  $\Pi$  be a perfect traceable broadcast encryption scheme and TE be a perfect T-KEM. Then  $\Pi'$  (Construction 9) is 1-traceable against (0,0)-insiders, perfectly correct, perfectly robust, and semantically secure with  $S_n = [n]$  and t'(r) = r.

### <span id="page-61-0"></span>11.1 A Proof of Theorem 11.1

<span id="page-61-2"></span>**Lemma 11.3** If TE is semantically secure for t(r) = r, then  $\Pi'$  is also semantically secure for t(r) = r

Proof of Lemma 11.3. Lemma 11.3 follows a straightforward reduction to the semantic security of  $\Pi$ . Fix any  $n \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$ ,  $0 < t \le r$ ,  $\varepsilon \in (0,1/2]$ , and let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary against the semantic security of  $\Pi'$ . We construct a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  against the semantic security of TE. When run on input  $(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t)$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  first runs  $\mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$  to receive a set of corrupted parties Cor. W.l.o.g., assume that  $\operatorname{Cor} \subseteq [n]$  and  $|\operatorname{Cor}| < t$ . Then,  $\mathcal{B}$  runs  $\Pi$ .KGen $(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$  to obtain  $(\operatorname{pk}^{\Pi}, \operatorname{sk}^{\Pi}, \operatorname{tk})$ . It forwards Cor to its challenger to obtain  $(\operatorname{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}}, \{\operatorname{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}}\}_{i \in \mathsf{Cor}})$  as well as the challenge pair  $(k^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}})$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  runs  $(k^{\Pi}, c^{\Pi}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi$ .Enc $(\operatorname{pk}^{\Pi})$  and assembles  $k^* \leftarrow k^{\Pi} \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}$  and  $c^* \leftarrow (c^{\Pi}, c^{\mathsf{TE}})$  and obtains

$$b' \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{A}\left((\mathsf{pk}^\Pi,\mathsf{pk}^\mathsf{TE}),\mathsf{tk},\{(\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi,\mathsf{sk}_i^\mathsf{TE})\}_{i\in\mathsf{Cor}},k^*,c^*\right).$$

Finally,  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs the same bit b' to its challenger.

**Analysis.** Denote with  $\beta$  the internal bit of  $\mathcal{B}$ 's challenger. Now observe that, if  $\beta=0$ , then  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates Game-IND-CPA<sup>II'</sup> $(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$  to  $\mathcal{A}$  for b=0. If, however,  $\beta=1$ , then  $k^{\mathsf{TE}}$  is uniformly random, and thus  $k=k^{\mathsf{II}}\oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}$  is also uniformly random, hence  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates Game-IND-CPA<sup>II'</sup> $(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$  to  $\mathcal{A}$  for b=1. Thus,  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates Game-IND-CPA perfectly to  $\mathcal{A}$  and since it returns the same bit as  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  wins whenever  $\mathcal{A}$  wins. We get that

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Game\text{-}IND\text{-}CPA}^{\Pi'}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda},n,f,t,\varepsilon) = 1\right] = \Pr\left[\mathsf{Game\text{-}IND\text{-}CPA}^{\mathsf{TE}}_{\mathcal{B}}(1^{\lambda},n,r,t) = 1\right].$$

<span id="page-61-3"></span>**Lemma 11.4** If  $\Pi$  is c-robust and TE is perfectly robust, then  $\Pi'$  is also c-robust.

*Proof of Lemma 11.4.* A straightforward reduction shows that for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{c-rob.}}_{\mathcal{A},\Pi'}(1^{\lambda},n,r) \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{c-rob.}}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi}(1^{\lambda},n,r) + \mathsf{Adv}^{(n-r)\text{-}\mathrm{rob.}}_{\mathcal{B},\mathsf{TE}}(1^{\lambda},n,r).$$

<span id="page-61-4"></span>**Lemma 11.5** If  $\Pi$  is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders, then so is  $\Pi'$ . If  $\Pi$  is limitless, then so is  $\Pi'$ .

*Proof of Lemma 11.5.* Let  $\Pi$  be  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders. Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary against Game-Tr of  $\Pi'$  and  $\varepsilon$  be an inverse polynomial. Fix appropriate n, r, and t'.

We first show that, given a decoder D',  $\Pi$ . Trace produces a decoder D with the same decryption advantage.

<span id="page-61-5"></span> $\begin{array}{l} \textbf{Claim 11.6 For all } \left(\mathsf{pk}^\Pi, \{\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{tk}\right) \in \Pi.\mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, r, t(r), \varepsilon) \text{ as well as } \left(\mathsf{pk}^\mathsf{TE}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i^\mathsf{TE}\}_{i \in [n]}\right) \in \\ \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, r, t'), \ \mathsf{pk} \coloneqq (\mathsf{pk}^\Pi, \mathsf{pk}^\mathsf{TE}), \ \mathsf{and} \ \mathsf{sk}_i \coloneqq (\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi, \mathsf{sk}_i^\mathsf{TE}) \ \mathsf{for all} \ i \in [n], \ \mathsf{all} \ \mathcal{I}_\mathsf{sk} \subseteq [n] \ \mathsf{and} \ \mathcal{I}_\mathsf{pd} \subseteq [n] \\ \mathsf{with} \ |\mathcal{I}_\mathsf{sk}| \leq \sigma, \ |\mathcal{I}_\mathsf{pd}| \leq \rho, \ \mathsf{and} \ \mathcal{I}_\mathsf{sk} \cap \mathcal{I}_\mathsf{pd} = \emptyset, \ \mathsf{and} \ \mathsf{all} \ \mathsf{decoders} \ D', \ \mathsf{let} \ D \ \mathsf{be} \ \mathsf{the} \ \mathsf{decoder} \ \mathsf{produced} \ \mathsf{and} \\ \mathsf{subsequently traced by } \Pi'.\mathsf{Trace}^D(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{tk}, \mathcal{I}_\mathsf{sk}, \mathcal{I}_\mathsf{pd}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_\mathsf{sk} \cup \mathcal{I}_\mathsf{pd}}). \ \mathsf{Then, it holds that} \\ \end{array}$ 

$$P_{\Pi'}(D') = P_{\Pi}(D).$$

{62}------------------------------------------------

Proof of Claim 11.6. Fix all keys and parameters as indicated in the claim. It holds that

$$P_{\Pi}(D) = \Pr \left[ D \Big( c^{\Pi}, k_b^{\Pi}, \{ \mathsf{sk}_i^{\Pi} \}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}}, \{ d_i^{\Pi} \}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}} \Big) = b \colon \begin{array}{c} (c^{\Pi}, k_0^{\Pi}) \not \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\Pi}) \\ k_1^{\Pi} \not \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}, b \not \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\} \\ \{ d_i^{\Pi} \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{PDec}(sk_i^{\Pi}, c^{\Pi}) \}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}} \end{array} \right]$$

$$=\Pr\left[D'\Big((c^\Pi,c^{\mathsf{TE}}),k_b^\Pi\oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}},\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}},\{d_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\Big)=b\colon \begin{array}{c} (c^\Pi,k_0^\Pi) \xleftarrow{\$} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^\Pi) \\ (c^{\mathsf{TE}},k^{\mathsf{TE}}) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}}) \\ k_1^\Pi \xleftarrow{\$} \mathcal{K},b \xleftarrow{\$} \{0,1\} \\ \{d_i \leftarrow \Pi'.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i,(c^\Pi,c^{\mathsf{TE}})\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}} \end{array}\right]$$

$$=\Pr\begin{bmatrix} (c^{\Pi},k_{0}^{\Pi}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\Pi}) \\ (c^{\mathsf{TE}},k^{\mathsf{TE}}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}}) \\ (c^{\mathsf{TE}},k^{\mathsf{TE}}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}}) \\ k_{0} \leftarrow k_{0}^{\Pi} \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}} \\ k_{1} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}, b \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\} \\ \{d_{i} \leftarrow \Pi'.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_{i},(c^{\Pi},c^{\mathsf{TE}})\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}} \end{bmatrix}$$

$$=\Pr\left[D'\Big(c,k_b,\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}},\{d_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}\Big)=b\colon \begin{array}{c} (c,k_0) \ \stackrel{\$}{\Leftarrow} \Pi'.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}) \\ k_1 \ \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K},b \ \stackrel{\$}{\Leftarrow} \{0,1\} \\ \forall i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}\colon d_i \leftarrow \Pi'.\mathsf{PDec}(sk_i,c) \end{array}\right]=P_{\Pi'}(D').$$

In the first step, we replaced D by its call to D'. In the second step, we use the perfect secrecy of the one-time pad, i.e., if  $k_1^{\Pi}$  is uniformly random, then so is  $k_1^{\Pi} \oplus k^{\mathsf{TE}}$ . The result is then identical to the experiment for  $P'_{\Pi}(D')$ .

Equipped with Claim 11.6, we first show that  $\Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Consider a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  running in  $\mathsf{Game-Tr}^{\Pi'}_{\mathcal{B},\varepsilon,\sigma,\rho}(1^{\lambda},n,r,t(r))$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates  $\mathsf{Game-Tr}^{\Pi}_{\mathcal{A},\varepsilon,\sigma,\rho}(1^{\lambda},n,r,t')$  to  $\mathcal{A}$  as follows. Denote with  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$  and  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$  the set of real traitors corrupted by  $\mathcal{A}$  and the reduction  $\mathcal{B}$ , respectively. Denote with  $\mathcal{T}'_{\mathcal{A}}$  and  $\mathcal{T}'_{\mathcal{B}}$ , the set of traitors found by the trace algorithm in the  $\mathsf{Game-Tr}$  experiments when running  $\mathcal{A}$  against  $\Pi'$  and  $\mathcal{B}$  against  $\Pi$ , respectively.

When  $\mathcal{B}$  receives the security parameter  $\lambda$  and the challenge public key  $\mathsf{pk}^\Pi$ , it runs  $\mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{KGen}(1^\lambda, n, r, t')$  to generate the instance of  $\mathsf{TE}$ . It embeds the challenge public key by setting  $\mathsf{pk} \coloneqq (\mathsf{pk}^\Pi, \mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}})$  and runs  $\mathcal{A}(1^\lambda, \mathsf{pk})$ , simulating the  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}$  and  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}$  oracles, by forwarding queries to its challenger and embedding the  $\mathsf{TE}$  parts.

Simulating  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}(i)$ . On input i,  $\mathcal{B}$  queries  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}(i)$  from its challenger to obtain  $\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi$  and forwards  $(\mathsf{sk}_i^\Pi, \mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}})$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ . As a result,  $\mathcal{B}$  corrupts the same parties as  $\mathcal{A}$ , i.e.,  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}} = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$ .

Simulating  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}(i, r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  parses  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  as  $(r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{\Pi}, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{\mathsf{TE}})$  and obtains  $d_i^{\Pi}$  by querying  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}(i, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{\Pi})$  from its own challenger. It computes  $(k^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}}) \leftarrow \mathsf{TE.Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{\mathsf{TE}}; r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{\mathsf{TE}})$  and  $d_i^{\mathsf{TE}} \leftarrow \mathsf{TE.PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}}, c^{\mathsf{TE}})$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  returns  $d_i = (d_i^{\Pi}, d_i^{\mathsf{TE}})$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

When  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs  $(D', \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}})$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  constructs D as indicated by  $\Pi'$ . Trace, which is possible, because  $\mathcal{B}$  knows  $\mathsf{sk}_i^{\mathsf{TE}}$  for all  $i \in [n]$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs  $(D, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}})$  to its challenger.

**Analysis.** We start with BadTr: By construction it holds that  $\mathcal{T}'_{\mathcal{A}} = \mathcal{T}'_{\mathcal{B}}$ , and since  $\mathcal{B}$  corrupts the same parties as  $\mathcal{A}$  through its simulation of  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}$ , it also holds that  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}} = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$ . Hence, we get that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] = \Pr[\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}' \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}] = \Pr[\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}' \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}] = \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}],$$

which is assumed to be negligible.

To bound  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}]$ , consider Claim 11.6, which implies that  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] = \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}]$ . Hence, we get that

$$\begin{split} \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] &= \Pr[\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}' \subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}} \wedge |\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}'| \geq \tau] \\ &= \Pr[\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}' \subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}} \wedge |\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}'| \geq \tau] \\ &= \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}] \\ &\geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) = \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda), \end{split}$$

for a negligible function  $negl(\lambda)$  by assumption.

- <span id="page-66-1"></span>[44] Kelkar, M., Ganesh, A., Partap, A., Bonneau, J., Weinberg, S.M.: Breaking Omertà: On threshold cryptography, smart collusion, and whistleblowing. ## <span id="page-67-2"></span>**Appendices**

## <span id="page-67-0"></span>A Proofs of Partition Lemmas

### A.1 Some Probabilistic Facts

We first present some probabilistic facts that will be essential to prove our probabilistic lemmas on partitions of parties.

**Definition A.1** (Negative association [43]). Let  $X := (X_1, \ldots, X_\ell)$  be a vector of random variables. The variables X are negatively associated if, for every two disjoint index sets  $I, J \subseteq [\ell]$ ,

$$\mathbb{E}\Big[f\big(X_i, i \in I\big) \cdot g\big(X_j, j \in J\big)\Big] \leq \mathbb{E}\Big[f\big(X_i, i \in I\big)\Big] \cdot \mathbb{E}\Big[g\big(X_j, j \in J\big)\Big],$$

for all functions  $f: \mathbb{R}^{|I|} \to \mathbb{R}$  and  $g: \mathbb{R}^{|J|} \to \mathbb{R}$  that are both non-decreasing or both non-increasing.

We are interested in this definition because the Chernoff-Hoeffding bounds are applicable to sums of variables that satisfy the negative association condition [46].

<span id="page-67-3"></span>**Fact A.2** ([31]) Let  $X := (X_1, ..., X_n)$  be random variables taking values in  $\{0, 1\}$  and distributed uniformly over all m-weight vectors, where  $m \le n$ . That is, for every  $x = (x_1, ..., x_n) \in \{0, 1\}^n$  of Hamming weight m it holds that

$$\Pr[X = x] = \binom{n}{m}^{-1},$$

and  $\Pr[X = x] = 0$  for every x of Hamming weight  $\operatorname{wt}(x) \neq m$ . Then the random variables  $(X_1, \ldots, X_n)$  are negatively associated.

<span id="page-67-4"></span>Claim A.3 Given a fixed n-bit string x and a uniformly chosen partition  $\Pi$  of [n] into k bins of size n/k each, denote by  $Y_{i,j}$  the random variable that takes value 1 when the j-th bit in the i-th bin is 1. Then, for every index  $i \in [k]$ , the n/k variables  $\{Y_{i,j}\}_{1 \le j \le n/k}$  are negatively associated.

*Proof.* The  $n/k \cdot k$  random variables  $Y_{i,j}$  for  $1 \le i \le k$  and  $1 \le j \le n/k$  satisfy the conditions for Fact A.2 for  $\ell = n$  and m = wt(x), and they are therefore negatively associated. By [43], any subset of negatively associated variables are also negatively associated. Thus, the claim follows.

<span id="page-67-5"></span>Claim A.4 Given a fixed n-bit string x and a uniformly chosen partition  $\Pi$  of [n] into 2 bins  $B_1, B_2$  such that  $|B_1| = n^a, |B_2| = n - n^a$  for  $a \in \{0, 1\}$ , denote by  $Y_{i,j}$ , the random variable that takes value 1 when the j-th bit in the i-th bin is 1. Then, for every index  $i \in \{1, 2\}$ , the variables  $\{Y_{i,j}\}_{1 \le j \le |B_i|}$  are negatively associated.

*Proof.* The  $n^a + n - n^a$  random variables  $\{Y_{1,j}\}_{1 \leq j \leq n^a}, \{Y_{2,j}\}_{1 \leq j \leq n - n^a}$  satisfy the conditions for Fact A.2 for  $\ell = n$  and m = wt(x), and they are therefore negatively associated. By [43], any subset of negatively associated variables are also negatively associated. Thus, the claim follows.

### <span id="page-67-1"></span>A.2 Proof of Lemma 6.2

**Lemma 6.2 (Restated).** Let  $n = \operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$  and K = o(n). Further, let  $P_j = \{B_i^{(j)}\}_{i \in [K]}$  for  $j \in [\ell]$  be a collection of  $\ell$  independently and uniformly sampled (K, n)-balanced partitions, where  $|B_i^{(j)}| = n/K$  for every  $i \in [K]$  and every  $j \in [\ell]$ . Let  $t_{\mathsf{bin}} = \frac{n}{2K} - \sqrt{\frac{\lambda n}{K}}$ . Then,

1. For any  $\ell \geq 1$ , for uniformly distributed  $S \subseteq [n]$  where  $|S| \geq n/2$ , there exists some  $j \in [\ell]$  such that for all  $i \in [K]$ ,

$$|S \cap B_i^{(j)}| \ge t_{\mathsf{bin}},$$

except with negligible probability in  $\lambda$ .

2. Let  $r = n/K - t_{\mathsf{bin}}$ ,  $\mathbf{r} = r \cdot (1, \dots, 1) \in \mathbb{N}^K$ , and  $c \in (0, 1/2)$ . Then, for  $\ell \geq 2(1 - c)K/c^2$ , the probability that there is a subset  $S \subseteq [n]$ , where  $|S| \leq n/2 - cn$ , which  $(\ell, \mathbf{r})$ -blocks  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  is negligible in  $\lambda$ .

{68}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* Let S be a set and P a partition. For each bin  $B_i$  in P, define  $Y_{i,j}$  as an indicator random variable that equals 1 if and only if the j-th party in the i-th bin belongs to S. Equivalently, if we represent the i-th bin as a binary string of length  $|B_i|$ , the j-th bit of the string is 1 if the corresponding party is in S, and 0 otherwise. For each  $i \in [K]$ , the variables  $\{Y_{i,j}\}_{j \in [|B_i|]}$  are negatively associated (see Claim A.3). Let us denote by  $Y_i = \sum_j Y_{i,j}$  the random variable representing the number of parties of S that are in the i-th bin.

The first part of the lemma is proven using Chernoff's bound. First note that for  $|S| \ge n/2$ , we get that the expectation is  $\mu = \mathbb{E}[Y_i] \ge n/(2K)$ . Thus, by Chernoff bound, we get  $\Pr[Y_i < t_{\mathsf{bin}}] \le \exp(-\delta^2 \mu/2) = \exp(-\lambda)$ , where  $\delta = 2\sqrt{\lambda K/n}$ . By union bound over all bins, the probability that this holds for one of the bins is at most  $K \exp(-\lambda)$ , which is negligible in  $\lambda$ .<sup>14</sup>

We now turn to the second part. Fix a set  $S \subseteq [n]$  with |S| = n/2 - cn, for some constant  $c \in (0, 1/2)$ . For a given i, it now holds that  $\mu = \mathbb{E}(Y_i) \le n/(2K) - cn/K$ . We next bound the probability that S  $r_i$ -blocks the i-th bin for a partition P, i.e.,  $|S \cap B_i| \ge |B_i| - t_{\mathsf{bin}} = \frac{n}{2K} + \sqrt{\lambda n/K}$ . We use the Chernoff upper-tail bound:

$$\Pr\left[Y_i \ge \frac{n}{(2K)} + \sqrt{\lambda n/K}\right] = \Pr[Y_i \ge (1+\delta)\mu] \le \exp\left(\frac{-\delta^2 \mu}{(2+\delta)}\right).$$

We seek  $\delta$  such that  $(1+\delta)\mu = \frac{n}{2K} + \sqrt{\lambda n/K}$ , where  $\mu = \frac{n}{2K} - cn/K$ . That is,  $\delta = \frac{\sqrt{\lambda n/K} + cn/K}{n/(2K) - cn/K}$ . Since the Chernoff upper-tail bound is decreasing in  $\delta$ , we may lower bound  $\delta \geq \frac{cn/K}{n/(2K) - cn/K} = \frac{2c}{1-2c}$ . Hence, using  $\delta \geq \frac{2c}{1-2c}$  and  $\mu = \frac{(1-2c)n}{2K}$ , we get:  $\frac{\delta^2 \mu}{2+\delta} \geq \frac{c^2}{1-c} \cdot \frac{n}{K}$ . Thus,

$$\Pr[Y_i \ge n/(2K) + \sqrt{\lambda n/K}] \le \exp\left(\frac{-c^2 n}{(1-c)K}\right).$$

Taking a union bound over all K bins, the probability that the set S r-blocks partition P is

$$p_s = K \exp\left(\frac{-c^2 n}{(1-c)K}\right) \le \exp\left(\frac{-c^2 n}{(1-c)K} + \ln K\right).$$

Since the  $\ell$  partitions are sampled independently, the probability that S is  $(\ell, \mathbf{r})$ -blocks the collection of  $\ell$  partitions is at most  $\exp\left(\frac{-\ell c^2 n}{(1-c)K} + \ell \ln K\right)$ . Finally, taking a loose union bound over all subsets  $S \subseteq [n]$   $(|\mathcal{P}([n])| \leq \exp(n))$ , the probability that there exists such an S is at most  $\exp\left(n + \frac{-\ell c^2 n}{(1-c)K} + \ell \ln K\right)$ . Choosing  $\ell = 2K(1-c)/c^2$ , this expression can be bounded by

$$\exp\!\left(-n + \frac{K \ln K}{c^2}\right).$$

Since  $n \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , K = o(n), and c is a constant, this probability is negligible in  $\lambda$  for sufficiently large n, which concludes the proof of the second part.

#### <span id="page-68-0"></span>A.3 Proof of Lemma 9.2

**Lemma 9.2 (Restated).** Let  $P_j = \{B_1^j, B_2^j\}$  for  $j \in [\ell]$  be a collection of  $\ell$  independently and uniformly sampled (2, n)-unbalanced partitions, where for  $a \in (0, 1)$ ,  $|B_1^j| = n^a$  and  $|B_2^j| = n - n^a$  for every  $j \in [\ell]$ . Let  $\delta_1 = 6n^{-a/2}$ ,  $\delta_2 = 6\frac{\sqrt{n}}{n-n^a}$ ,  $t_1 = (1-\delta_1)\frac{n^a}{2}$ ,  $t_2 = (1-\delta_2)\frac{(n-n^a)}{2}$ . Then,
