- 1. If  $\ell \geq 6\lambda$ , for uniformly distributed  $S \subseteq [n]$  where  $|S| \geq n/2$ , it holds that  $S(0.8\ell, (t_1, t_2))$ -dominates the partitions  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  with overwhelming probability.
- 2. Let  $c \in (0, 0.5)$ ,  $\ell \ge 10n^{1-a}(1-c)/(c^2)$ ,  $\mathbf{r}_1 = (n^a t_1, 0)$  and  $\beta = 0.2\ell$ . Then, the probability that there exists a subset  $S \subseteq [n]$ , such that  $|S| \le n/2 cn$  and  $(\beta, \mathbf{r}_1)$ -blocks  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  is negligible in  $\lambda$ .

<span id="page-68-1"></span>The could decrease the gap by increasing  $\ell$ . However, as we aim to get  $\ell$ , which does not depend on  $\lambda$ , we keep the gap as is.

{69}------------------------------------------------

- <span id="page-69-0"></span>3. Let  $c \in (0, 0.5)$ ,  $\ell \ge 20n^{1-a}(2-3c)/c^2$ ,  $\mathbf{r}_2 = (t_1, 0)$ , and  $\beta = 0.2\ell$ . Then, the probability that there exists a subset  $S \subseteq [n]$ , such that  $|S| \le n/2 - cn$  and  $(0.2\ell, \mathbf{r}_2)$ -dominates  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  is negligible in  $\lambda$ .
- 4. If  $\ell \geq 3n^{1-a}\lambda$ , then the probability that there exists a  $u \in [n]$  such that  $u \in B_1^j$  for more than  $2\ell/n^{1-a}$  indices j is negligible.

*Proof.* Let S be a set and P a partition. For each bin  $B_i$  in P, define  $Y_{i,j}$  as an indicator random variable that equals 1 if and only if the j-th party in the i-th bin belongs to S. Equivalently, if we represent the bin as a binary string of length  $|B_i|$ , the j-th bit of the i-th bin is 1 if the corresponding party is in S. For each  $i \in \{1,2\}$ , the variables  $\{Y_{i,j}\}_{j \in [|B_i|]}$  are negatively associated (see Claim A.4). Let us denote by  $Y_i = \sum_j Y_{i,j}$  the random variable representing the number of parties of S that are in the i-th bin.

The first part of the lemma is proven using the Chernoff bound as follows. By the linearity of the expectation, for  $|S| \geq n/2$ ,  $\mu_1 = \mathbb{E}[Y_1] \geq n^a/2$  and  $\mu_2 = \mathbb{E}[Y_2] \geq (n-n^a)/2$ . We get  $\Pr[Y_1 < t_1] \leq e^{-\delta_1^2 \mu_1/2} = e^{-9}$  and  $\Pr[Y_2 < t_2] \leq e^{-\delta_2^2 \mu_2/2} = e^{-9 \cdot \frac{n}{n-n^a}} < e^{-9}$ . Thus, by union bound over the two bins, the probability that S does not  $(t_1, t_2)$ -dominate a partition is at most  $2e^{-9}$ . Finally, if we independently sample  $\ell$  partitions, we expect  $\mu \leq 2 \exp(-9)\ell$  undominated partitions. By Chernoff bound, taking  $\delta = \frac{\exp(9)}{10} - 1$ , and counting in a random variable X the partitions that are not dominated by S, we get that

$$\Pr\Big[S \ (0.8\ell, (t_1, t_2)) \text{-dominates} \ \{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}\Big] \ge 1 - \Pr[X \ge 0.2\ell] \ge 1 - \exp\left(-\frac{\delta^2 \mu}{2 + \delta}\right) \ge 1 - \exp\left(-\frac{\ell}{6}\right),$$

which is overwhelming for  $\ell \geq 6\lambda$ .

To prove the second part of the lemma, consider a fixed set  $S \subseteq [n]$  with |S| = n/2 - cn, for some constant 0 < c < 1/2. In order for S to be blocking for a partition  $P = \{B_1, B_2\}$ , it must hold that  $|S \cap B_1| \ge |B_1| - t_1 = \frac{n^a}{2} + 6n^{a/2}$  or  $|S \cap B_2| \ge |B_2| - t_2 = \frac{n-n^a}{2} + 6\sqrt{n}$ . As we assume that  $|S| \le n/2 - cn$ , we can ignore the second bin for sufficiently large n, since  $n/2 - cn < |B_2| - t_2 = n/2 - o(n)$ . We next find the probability that S is blocking for the bin  $B_1$  of P. Let  $Y_1 = |S \cap B_1|$  and  $\mu = \mathbb{E}[Y_1] = \frac{n^a}{2} - cn^a$ . By the Chernoff bound, the probability that S is blocking for the first bin satisfies

$$p_1 = \Pr\left[Y_1 \ge \frac{n^a}{2} + 6n^{a/2}\right] \le \exp\left(-\frac{\alpha^2 \mu}{2 + \alpha}\right),$$

where  $\alpha$  satisfies  $(1+\alpha)\mu = \frac{n^a}{2} + 6n^{a/2}$ . That is,

$$\alpha = \frac{6n^{a/2} + cn^a}{n^a/2 - cn^a} \ge \frac{cn^a}{n^a/2 - cn^a} \ge \frac{2c}{1 - 2c}.$$

Since the probability decreases as  $\alpha$  increases, we get the following:

$$p_1 \le \exp\left(-\frac{(2c/(1-2c))^2\mu}{2+(2c/(1-2c))}\right) \le \exp\left(-\frac{c^2}{1-c}n^a\right).$$

Since the  $\ell$  partitions are sampled independently, the probability that  $S(\beta, \mathbf{r}_1)$ -blocks  $\{P_j\}_{j \in [\ell]}$ , i.e., it is blocking for at least  $\beta$  partitions, is

$$\Pr[\operatorname{Binom}(\ell, p_1) \ge \tau] \le \sum_{j=\tau}^{\ell} {\ell \choose j} p_1^j.$$

Using the standard combinatorial bound  $\binom{\ell}{j} \leq (e\ell/j)^j \leq (e\ell/\tau)^j$ , which follows from Stirling's approximation [48], we can bound

$$\Pr[\text{Binom}(\ell, p_1) \ge \beta] \le \sum_{j=\beta}^{\infty} \left(\frac{e\ell p_1}{\beta}\right)^j$$

$$\stackrel{\text{(geometric series)}}{=} \frac{\left(\frac{e\ell p_1}{\beta}\right)^{\beta}}{1 - \frac{e\ell p_1}{\beta}} \stackrel{e\ell p_1/\beta < 1/2}{\le} 2\left(\frac{e\ell p_1}{\beta}\right)^{\beta}.$$

{70}------------------------------------------------

We can bound this by  $\exp(\beta(\ln p_1 + 1 + \ln(\ell/\beta)))$ . Hence, as  $\beta = 0.2\ell$ , we get that the probability is at most  $\exp(0.2\ell(-c^2n^a/(1-c)+3))$ .

Finally, taking a union bound over all possible sets  $S \subseteq [n]$  of size n/2 - cn, the probability that a  $(\beta, \mathbf{r}_1)$ -blocking set exists is at most

$$2^{n} \cdot \exp\left(0.2\ell(-c^{2}n^{a}/(1-c)+3)\right) = \exp\left(n + 0.2\ell(-c^{2}n^{a}/(1-c)+3)\right).$$

Taking

$$\ell = \frac{10n^{1-a}(1-c)}{c^2},$$

yields a probability of  $\exp(-n + 30n^{1-a}(1-c)/(c^2)) < \exp(-\lambda)$  for sufficiently large n.

In order to prove the third part of the lemma, we first calculate the probability that a fixed set S  $\mathbf{r}_2$ -dominates a 2-unbalanced uniformly random partition  $P = \{B_1, B_2\}$  as defined in the lemma. That is, fix a constant 0 < c < 1/2, and let |S| = n/2 - cn. We calculate the probability that  $|S \cap B_1| \ge t_1$ . Let  $Y_1 = |S \cap B_1|$  and  $\mu = \mathbb{E}(Y_1) = \frac{n^a}{2} - cn^a$ . By the Chernoff bound, the probability that  $Y_1 \ge t_1$  is

$$p_2 = \Pr[Y_1 \ge \frac{n^a}{2} - 3n^{a/2}] \le \exp\left(-\frac{\alpha^2 \mu}{2 + \alpha}\right),$$

where  $\alpha$  satisfies  $(1+\alpha)\mu = \frac{n^a}{2} - 3n^{a/2}$ . That is,

$$\alpha = \frac{cn^a - 3n^{a/2}}{n^a/2 - cn^a} \ge \frac{cn^a - cn^a/2}{n^a/2 - cn^a} = \frac{c}{1 - 2c},$$

where the inequality follows since  $8n^{a/2} = o(cn^a)$ , thus for sufficiently large n,  $3n^{a/2} \le cn^a/2$ . Hence, since the probability decreases as  $\alpha$  increases, we get the following.

$$p_2 = \Pr[Y_1 \ge t_1] \le \exp\left(-\frac{\alpha^2 \mu}{2+\alpha}\right) = \exp\left(-\frac{c^2}{(1-2c)^2} \cdot \frac{n^a/2 - cn^a}{2+c/(1-2c)}\right) = \exp\left(-\frac{c^2}{4-6c}n^a\right).$$

We proceed as in the second part of the lemma, replacing  $p_1$  by  $p_2$ . The probability that there exists an S that  $\mathbf{r}_2$ -dominates at least  $\beta = 0.2\ell$  partitions is

$$\exp\left(n+0.2\ell\left(-\frac{c^2}{4-6c}n^a+3\right)\right),\,$$

which is negligible in  $\lambda$  when

$$\ell = \frac{10n^{1-a}(4-6c)}{c^2}.$$

Finally, to prove the fourth part, notice that the probability that a given element  $u \in [n]$  appears in the first bin of a partition  $P_j$ , i.e.,  $u \in B_1^j$ , is  $1/n^{1-a}$ . Let X denote the number of partitions in which u appears in the first bin. Then

$$\mu = \mathbb{E}[X] = \frac{\ell}{n^{1-a}},$$

and setting  $\delta = 1$ , we have for  $a \in (0, 1)$ :

$$\Pr\left[X \ge \frac{2\ell}{n^{1-a}}\right] = \Pr[X \ge (1+\delta)\mu] \le \exp\left(-\mu/3\right) = \exp\left(-\frac{\ell}{3n^{1-a}}\right).$$

Taking a union bound over all  $u \in [n]$ , the probability that there is a party that appears in the first bin of more than  $2\ell/n^a$  partitions is at most

$$n \cdot \exp\left(-\frac{\ell}{3n^{1-a}}\right)$$
,

which is negligible in  $\lambda$  when

$$\ell = \Theta(\lambda n^{1-a}).$$

{71}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-71-2"></span><span id="page-71-0"></span>B Limit Elimination Compiler

Next, we extend Zhandry's limit elimination compiler [51, 28] to the multi-traitor and insider setting, resulting in the following theorem. In particular, the only difference between our construction and [51, 28] is that we need to take care of full and partial insiders. We explain how we can apply these modifications without affecting the traceability of the construction.

In order to simulate partial decryptions in the Trace algorithm, we require the underlying limited construction to support the following method.

**Definition B.1** (The method SimPD). We say that a TT-KEM  $\Pi$  supports the simulation method SimPD with syntax

$$d_i \leftarrow \mathsf{SimPD}(\mathsf{pk}, i, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}),$$

if for all  $\lambda$ ,  $n \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ ,  $r \in \mathcal{S}_n$ ,  $0 \le t \le t(r)$ , all  $\varepsilon \in (0, 1/2)$ , all  $\mathsf{pp} \in \Pi.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$ ,  $(\mathsf{pk}, \{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{tk}) \in \Pi.\mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda}, n, r, t, \varepsilon)$ , all  $i \in [n]$ , and all  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}} \in \mathcal{R}_{\lambda}$ , it holds that

$$SimPD(pk, i, r_{Enc}) = PDec(sk_i, c),$$

where  $(k, c) \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}; r_{\mathsf{Enc}}).$ 

Then, the following theerem is proven by Construction 10.

<span id="page-71-1"></span>Theorem B.2 (Limit Elimination Compiler). If there exists a limited secure threshold KEM,  $\Pi_{\text{Limited}}$ , that is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders, then there also exists a limitless secure threshold KEM that is  $\tau$ -traceable against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ -insiders. The resulting scheme has the same public and secret key size, while the ciphertext size only grows by a factor polynomial in the security parameter  $\lambda$ , independent of the number of parties n. If  $\rho > 0$ , we require that  $\Pi_{\text{Limited}}$  supports the the simulation method SimPD.

In the construction, we need the following theorem.

**Theorem B.3 (Goldreich-Levin Decoding [37]).** There exists a constant  $\Gamma$  and an oracle algorithm  $\mathsf{GL}^D(\ell,\varepsilon')$  running in time  $\mathsf{poly}(\ell,\log(1/\varepsilon'))$  and making  $\mathsf{poly}(\ell,\log(1/\varepsilon'))$  queries to D, such that the following holds. If there exists an  $x \in \{0,1\}^\ell$  such that  $\Pr[D(r) = x \cdot r \mod 2 \colon r \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^\ell] \geq \frac{1}{2} + \varepsilon'$ , then  $\Pr[\mathsf{GL}^{D(\ell,\varepsilon')} = x] \geq \Gamma \cdot (\varepsilon')^2$ .

<span id="page-71-3"></span>Construction 10. Let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}$  be a  $\tau$ -traceable threshold KEM against  $(\sigma, \rho)$  insiders with limit  $\varepsilon$ . We construct  $\Pi'$  that is  $\tau$ -traceable threshold KEM against  $(\sigma, \rho)$  without a limit, i.e., can trace any decoder with noticeable advantage.

- $\Pi'.\mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda},n,r,t)\text{: }\mathsf{Sample}\ (\mathsf{pk},\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i\in[n]},\mathsf{tk}) \xleftarrow{\$} \Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}.\mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda},n,r,t). \ \mathrm{Output}\ (\mathsf{pk},\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i\in[n]},\mathsf{tk}).$
- Π'.Enc(pk): Let  $y = \omega(\log \lambda)$  and  $z = z(\lambda)$  be a polynomial. Compute  $(k_{u,v}, c_{u,v}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk})$  for all  $u \in [y], v \in [z]$ . For all  $v \in [z]$  let  $k_v = k_{1,v} \oplus k_{2,v} \oplus \cdots \oplus k_{y,v}$ . Sample  $s \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}$  and let  $b_v = \langle s, k_v \rangle \mod 2$  (bit-wise inner product) for all  $v \in [z]$ . Let  $k = (b_1, \ldots, b_z) \in \{0, 1\}^z$  and let  $c = (s, (c_{u,v})_{u \in [y], v \in [z]})$ . Output (c, k).
- $\Pi'.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i,c)$ : Parse c as  $(s,(c_{u,v})_{u\in[y],v\in[z]})$ . For all  $u\in[y],v\in[z]$  compute partial decryptions  $d_{i,u,v}\leftarrow\Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i,c_{u,v})$ . Output  $(d_{i,u,v})_{u\in[y],v\in[z]}$ .
- Π'.Combine(pk, S,  $(d_i)_{i \in S}$ , c). Parse c as  $(s, (c_{u,v})_{u \in [y],v \in [z]})$  and  $d_i$  as  $(d_{i,u,v})_{u \in [y],v \in [z]}$  for all  $i \in S$ . Compute  $\hat{k}_{u,v} = \Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}$ .Combine(pk, S,  $\{d_{i,u,v}\}_{i \in S}, c_{u,v}$ ). For all  $v \in [z]$  compute  $\hat{k}_v = \hat{k}_{1,v} \oplus \cdots \oplus \hat{k}_{y,v}$  and  $\hat{b}_v = \langle s, \hat{k}_v \rangle \mod 2$ . Output  $\hat{k} = (\hat{b}_1, \ldots, \hat{b}_z)$ .
- $\Pi'.\mathsf{Trace}^{D(\cdot)}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{tk},\varepsilon,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}},\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}\cup\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}})\text{:} \ \mathrm{Output} \ \mathrm{as}\ \Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}.\mathsf{Trace}^{D_3(\cdot)}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{tk},\varepsilon,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}},\{\mathsf{sk}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}\cup\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}), \\ \text{where} \ D_3 \ \mathrm{is} \ \mathrm{constructed} \ \mathrm{as} \ \mathrm{follows}.$
- $D_1(s,(c_u)_{u\in[y]},\{\overline{\mathsf{sk}}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}},\{d_{i,u}\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}},u\in[y]})$ : The algorithm  $D_1$  is constructed as follows:
  - Sample a random  $v \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} [z]$ . For all  $u \in [y]$ , set  $c_{u,v} = c_u$  and  $d_{i,u,v} = d_{i,u}$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}, u \in [y]$ .
  - For all  $v' \in [z] \setminus \{v\}$  and all  $u \in [y]$ , compute  $(k_{u,v'}, c_{u,v'}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}; r_{u,v'})$ . Let  $c = (s, (c_{u,v})_{u \in [y], v \in [z]}).$

{72}------------------------------------------------

- <span id="page-72-2"></span>• Compute  $d_{i,u,v'} \leftarrow \Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}.\mathsf{SimPD}(\mathsf{pk},i,r_{u,v'}) \text{ for all } i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}, u \in [y], v' \in [z] \setminus \{v\}.$
- For all  $v' \in [z] \setminus \{v\}$  compute  $k_{v'} = k_{1,v'} \oplus \cdots \oplus k_{y,v'}$ .
- For all  $v' \leq v$  sample  $b_v \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}$  and for v' > v let  $b'_v = \langle s, k_{v'} \rangle \mod 2$ . Set  $k = (b_1, \ldots, b_z)$ .
- Output  $b_v \oplus D(k, c, \{\overline{\mathsf{sk}}_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}}, \{d_{i,u,v}\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}, u \in [y], v \in [z]}).$

$$D_2((c_u)_{u\in[y]},\{\overline{\operatorname{sk}}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{sk}}},\{d_{i,u}\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{pd}},u\in[y]})\text{: }\operatorname{GL}^{D_1(\cdot,(c_u)_{u\in[y]},\{\overline{\operatorname{sk}}_i\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{sk}}},\{d_{i,u}\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{pd}},u\in[y]})}(\ell,\varepsilon'=\varepsilon/z).$$

 $D_3(k, c, \{\overline{\mathsf{sk}}_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}}, \{d_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}})$ : Let (k, c) be the output of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}$ . Enc and  $d_i$  be the (simulated) partial decryption of party i for the ciphertext c. The algorithm  $D_3$  is constructed as follows.

- For  $w \in [1, \zeta]$  where  $\zeta = \left(\frac{2yz^3}{\Gamma \varepsilon^3}\right) \times \omega(\log \lambda)$ :
  - Choose a random  $u \in [y]$  and set  $c_u = c$  and  $d_{i,u} = d_i$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}$ .
  - For each  $u' \in [y] \setminus [u]$ , run  $(k_{u'}, c_{u'}) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}; r_{u'})$ .
  - Compute  $d_{i,u'} \leftarrow \Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}.\mathsf{SimPD}(\mathsf{pk},i,r_{u'}) \text{ for all } i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}, u' \in [y] \setminus \{u\}.$
  - $\operatorname{Run} k' \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} D_2((c_u)_{u \in [y]}, \{\overline{\mathsf{sk}}_i\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{sk}}}, \{d_{i,u'}\}_{u' \in [y], i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}}) \text{ and set } \hat{k} = k' \oplus k_1 \oplus k_{u-1} \oplus k_{u+1} \oplus k_y.$
- If  $k = \hat{k}$  for any  $w \in [\zeta]$ , output 0. Otherwise, output 1.

Changes Compared to [51, 28]. Our construction differs from [51, 28] in two respects. First, in the construction of the decoders  $D_1$  and  $D_3$ , the tracer must simulate partial decryptions of parties in  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ . These partial decryptions are always for ciphertexts generated by the tracer itself, and therefore can be simulated using the procedure SimPD from the underlying  $\Pi_{\text{Limited}}$ . Second, the decoders are forwarded the secret keys of parties in  $\mathcal{I}_{sk}$  as input by  $\Pi_{\text{Limited}}$ . Trace.

Correctness and Security. As discussed above, the construction is identical to [28] except for the trace algorithm. Thus, correctness and security follow directly from the construction of [28].

**Lemma B.4** Let  $y = \omega(\log \lambda)$ , let  $\varepsilon' = \varepsilon/z$ , and let  $\zeta = \frac{2yz^3}{\Gamma \varepsilon^3} \cdot \omega(\log \lambda)$ . Suppose  $\ell = \omega(\lambda)$ . If  $\Pi_{\text{Limited}}$  is a  $\tau$ -threshold traitor tracing scheme against  $(\sigma, \rho)$  insiders with limit  $\varepsilon$ , then  $\Pi'$  is a limitless  $\tau$ -threshold traitor tracing scheme against  $(\sigma, \rho)$ . If  $\rho > 0$ , we require that  $\Pi_{\text{Limited}}$  supports the the simulation method SimPD.

The above changes do not affect the decryption capability of the decoder  $D_3$ . Indeed, this follows from the fact that D is a good decoder with respect to  $\mathcal{I}_{sk}$  and  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$ , and that in the constructions of  $D_1$ ,  $D_2$ , and  $D_3$  we generate valid partial decryptions and provide valid secret keys for the parties in  $\mathcal{I}_{sk}$ . Therefore, the traceability of the construction follows from the arguments of [51, 28]. The only additional consideration is that the reduction must simulate partial decryptions, which we sketch below.

Fix an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  for  $\Pi'$  and an inverse-polynomial  $\varepsilon$ . Let  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}$ ,  $\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}$ , and  $\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}$  be the events defined in Definition 4.1 for the scheme  $\Pi'$ . Similarly, let  $\mathcal{B}$  be an adversary against  $\Pi_{\mathsf{Limited}}$ , and let  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$ ,  $\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$ , and  $\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}$  be the corresponding events.

The reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates the tracing experiment for  $\mathcal{A}$  in a straightforward manner. All public parameters and corruption queries are forwarded honestly. Eventually,  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs a decoder  $D^*$ . The reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  then constructs the decoder  $D_3^*$  exactly as in  $\Pi'$ . Trace and submits it to its challenger. This is possible since the reduction can simulate the partial decryptions using SimPD of the underlying  $\Pi_{\text{Limited}}$ .

Since the set of corrupted parties is identical in both experiments, if the event  $\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}$  occurs, then  $\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  occurs as well. Therefore,  $\mathsf{Pr}[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \mathsf{Pr}[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}]$ .

The proof that  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] \ge \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  follows identically to [28, 51], with the only modification being the simulation of partial decryptions as described above.

# <span id="page-72-0"></span>C Deferred Proofs for our Compilers

#### <span id="page-72-1"></span>C.1 A Proof of Theorem 7.1 (Muilti-Instance Expansion Retains Traceability)

*Proof of Theorem 7.1.* Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary against  $\tau$ -traceability of  $\Pi'$  against  $\rho$  partial insiders, and let  $\varepsilon$  be any inverse polynomial. We will bound the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in breaking traceability by the

{73}------------------------------------------------

advantage of a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  against traceability of the underlying scheme  $\Pi$ . We start by showing that, whenever  $\mathcal{A}$  frames an honest party (i.e., causes  $\mathsf{BadTr}=1$ ), then  $\mathcal{B}$  does so with probability  $1/\ell$ , and thus

$$\Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A},\Pi'}] \leq \ell \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B},\Pi}] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

for a negligible function negl. To see this, consider a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  that initially samples a random index  $j^* \in [\ell]$ . Upon receiving a challenge public key  $\mathsf{pk}^*$  from its  $\Pi$ -challenger,  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates key generation by embedding its challenge public key at index  $j^*$ :  $\mathsf{pk}^{(j^*)} \leftarrow \mathsf{pk}^*$ . For all other instance,  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates key generation according to protocol.

Simulating  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec}\text{-Hon}}$ : On input  $(i, r_{\mathsf{Enc}})$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  parses  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  as  $(k, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(1)}, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(2)}, \dots, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(\ell)})$ , where  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j)}$  is the randomness used to encrypt in instance  $j \in [\ell]$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  then computes  $d_i = \{d_i^{(j)}\}_{j \in [\ell]}$  as follows:

- For  $j \neq j^*$ , compute  $(k_j, c_j) \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{(j)}; r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j)})$  and  $d_i^{(j)} \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)})$ .
- For  $j = j^*$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  queries  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec\text{-}Hon}}(i, r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j^*)})$  to obtain  $d_i^{(j^*)}$  from its challenger.

To simulate the corruption oracle queries  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{corrupt}}(i)$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  queries the corruption oracle of its own challenger to obtain  $\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j^*)}$ . Hence, it holds that  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}} = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$ . All other shares of party i are known to the reduction. When  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs a decoder D, the reduction constructs  $D_{j^*}$  as indicated by the  $\Pi'$ . Trace and outputs it to its challenger.

Observe that  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates  $\Pi'$  perfectly to  $\mathcal{A}$ , and in particular,  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view is independent of  $\mathcal{B}$ 's choice of  $j^*$ . Suppose that  $\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A},\Pi'}$  happens, i.e.,  $\mathcal{T}'_{\mathcal{A}} \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$ . Then, there must exists at least one index  $j \in [\ell]$  such that  $\mathcal{T}'_{j,\mathcal{A}} \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  will choose this index with probability  $1/\ell$ , and thus

$$\Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] = \Pr[\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}' \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \ell \cdot \Pr[\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}' \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}] = \ell \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}].$$

Next, we will show that if D is a good decoder, then at least one decoder  $D_j$  produced by  $\Pi'$ . Trace is good with overwhelming probability, and will be traced.

Suppose D is a good decoder, i.e., GoodDec happens. By definition, this means that

$$\Pr\left[D\left(\{(c_j,u_j)\}_{j\in[\ell]},k^b,\{d_i^{(j)}\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}},j\in[\ell]}\right): \begin{array}{c} b \xleftarrow{\$} \{0,1\},(k^0,k^1) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathcal{K}^2 \\ \text{For all } j\in[\ell]: \\ (c_j,k_j) \xleftarrow{\$} \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{(j)}) \\ u_j \leftarrow k_j \oplus k^0 \\ \forall i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}: d_i^{(j)} \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)},c_j) \end{array}\right] \geq \frac{1}{2} + \varepsilon.$$

We define a series of distributions  $X_j(b)$  for  $j \in [0,\ell]$ : Let  $X_0(b)$  be the above distribution on the tuple  $\left(\{(c_q,u_q)\}_{q\in[\ell]},k^b,\{d_i^{(q)}\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{pd},q\in[\ell]}\right)$ , where b is fixed. For all  $j\in[\ell]$ , let  $X_j$  be the same distribution, except that for all  $q\leq j,\ u_q$  is replaced with a uniformly random element from  $\mathcal{K}$ , respectively. We set  $p_j:=\Pr[D(X_j(b))=b\colon b\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}\{0,1\}]$ . Then, by definition, it holds that  $p_0\geq 1/2+\varepsilon$ . Also, observe that in  $X_\ell(b)$ , all  $u_j$  are just uniform random elements from  $\mathcal{K}$  and thus  $X_\ell(b)$  does not depend on b. Hence,  $X_\ell(0)$  and  $X_\ell(1)$  are identically distributed and  $p_\ell=1/2$ . As a consequence, there must be at least one  $j\in[\ell]$  such that  $p_j\leq p_{j-1}-\varepsilon/\ell$ . Let  $j^*$  be such a j and consider the decoder  $D_{j^*}$  on input  $(c_{j^*}k_{j^*},\{d_i^{(j^*)}\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}_{pd}})$ . Suppose  $k_{j^*}$  is the correct key, i.e.,  $(c_{j^*},k_{j^*})$  is sampled from  $\Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{(j^*)})$ . In this case,  $D_{j^*}$  samples a random bit b and outputs  $b\oplus D(X_{j^*-1}(b))$ . As a result,  $D_{j^*}$  outpus 0, whenever D correctly guesses b and 1 otherwise, which means if  $k_{j^*}$  is valid, then  $D_{j^*}$  outputs 0 with probability  $p_{j^*-1}$ , i.e.,  $D_{j^*}$  correctly guesses that  $k_{j^*}$  is valid with probability  $p_{j^*-1}$ . Also, observe that if  $k_{j^*}$  is invalid (i.e., if it does not fit  $c_{j^*}$ ), then  $D_{j^*}$  runs D on distribution  $X_{j^*}(b)$  for a random bit b and outputs 1, whenever D incorrectly guesses b. Hence  $D_{j^*}$  correctly guesses that  $k_{j^*}$  is invalid with probability b

$$\frac{1}{2}(p_{j^*-1}+1-p_{j^*}) = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{p_{j^*-1}-p_{j^*}}{2} \ge \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon}{2\ell}.$$

Above, we have shown that, if D is an  $\varepsilon$ -good decoder for  $\Pi'$ , there must exist some  $D_j^*$  that is  $\varepsilon/2\ell$ -good with respect to  $\Pi$ .

{74}------------------------------------------------

Suppose for now that we use exactly the same reduction as in the BadTr case. Since this reduction commits in the beginning on a random index  $j^*$  to attack, we would get that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \geq \frac{\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}]}{\ell} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

which is insufficient by a factor of  $1/\ell$ .

To fix this, we modify the reduction to test the decoders in a certain way such that the (modified) reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  can only ever trigger  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  if it guesses  $j^*$  correctly, which essentially concentrates the ability of  $\mathcal{B}$  to achieve  $\mathsf{GoodTr}$  to only happen for correct guesses, in which case it implies  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}$ . This will allow us to show that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}] \leq \underbrace{\frac{\ell-1}{\ell} \cdot 0}^{\mathrm{incorrect\ guess}} + \underbrace{\frac{1}{\ell} \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}]}_{\mathrm{correct\ guess}} = \frac{\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}]}{\ell},$$

which eliminates the loss of  $\ell$  encountered in the naive approach.

To achieve this, the reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  constructs all decoders, and tests whether each  $D_j$  is good. For  $j=j^*$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  constructs  $D_j^*$  according  $\Pi'$ . Trace. In particular, it can generate all partial decryptions on behalf of  $\mathcal{I}_{pd}$  on the generated ciphertexts  $c_j$ , where  $j\neq j^*$ , because it knows all  $\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)}$  for  $j\neq j^*$ .

For all decoders  $D_j$ , where  $j \neq j^*$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  can simulate the behavior of the decoder and generate  $d_i^{(j^*)}$  on behalf of  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}$  by querying its challenger's partial decryption oracle  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec\text{-}Hon}}$ . Note that simulation is possible, because when given on input  $(c_j, k_j, \{d_i^{(j)}\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}_{pd}})$ , the decoder  $D_j$  is supposed to generate  $c_{j^*}$  honestly, and thus  $\mathcal{B}$  knows  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}}^{(j^*)}$ . For all other ciphertexts  $c_q$  where  $q \neq j$  and  $q \neq j^*$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  again simulates partial decryptions using the known secret keys.

Then  $\mathcal{B}$  performs the following test on all simulated decoders  $D_j$   $(j \neq j^*)$  and the decoder  $D_{j^*}$  Let  $Q := 32\ell^2 \lambda/\varepsilon^2$ , ctr := 0 and repeat Q times:

- Sample  $r_{\mathsf{Enc}} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi.\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{Enc}}$  and generate  $(c,k^0) \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}^{(j)};r_{\mathsf{Enc}}).$  Sample  $k^1 \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K},$  and  $b \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}.$  If  $j \neq j^*,$  compute  $d_i \leftarrow \Pi.\mathsf{PDec}(\mathsf{sk}_i^{(j)},c)$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{pd}}.$  If  $j = j^*,$  obtain  $d_i^{(j)}$  by querying the challenger on  $\mathcal{O}^{\mathsf{PDec-Hon}}(i,r_{\mathsf{Enc}}).$
- Run  $b' \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} D_j(c, k^b)$ . If b' = b, increment ctr.
- If  $ctr \geq Q \cdot (1/2 + 3\varepsilon/8\ell)$ , set the flag  $\mathsf{test}_j \leftarrow 1$  (in this case, we say that  $D_j$  "tested good"). Otherwise, set  $\mathsf{test}_j \leftarrow 0$ .

Suppose  $\mathcal{B}$  has chosen index  $j^*$  to embed the instance of the challenger initially. We make the following modification to the decoder that is returned by  $\mathcal{B}$ : If  $\mathsf{test}_{j^*} = 1$  and  $\mathsf{test}_j = 0$  for all  $j < j^*$  (i.e., if  $D_{j^*}$  is the first decoder that tested good) then  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs  $D_{j^*}$  to the challenger as before. Otherwise, however,  $\mathcal{B}$  instead outputs a dummy decoder Dummy that always outputs 0.

Before we can close the argument above, we first need to show that (a) if D is a good decoder, then with overwhelming probability at least one  $D_j$  will test good, and (b) except with negligible probability, it can not happen that  $D_j$  tests good, but is not  $\varepsilon/4\ell$ -good for instance j of  $\Pi$ .

<span id="page-74-0"></span>Claim C.1 If GoodDec happens, then with overwhelming probability, at least one  $D_j$  will test good.

Proof of Claim C.1. Our PLBE-style analysis has already shown that, if D is  $\varepsilon$ -good, then for at least one j it must hold that  $D_j$  is  $\varepsilon/2\ell$ -good. Suppose w.l.o.g. that  $P(D_j) = 1/2 + \varepsilon/2\ell$ , then by Hoeffding's inequality the probability that  $ctr < Q \cdot (1/2 + 3\varepsilon/8\ell)$  is negligible in  $\lambda$  if  $Q \ge 32\ell^2\lambda/\varepsilon^2$ .

<span id="page-74-1"></span>Claim C.2 For all  $j \in [\ell]$ , the event that  $D_j$  tests good, but is not  $\varepsilon/4\ell$ -good with respect to instance j can happen only with negligible probability.

Proof of Claim C.2. Again, applying Hoeffding's inequality yields a negligible bound for  $Q \geq 32\ell^2 \lambda/\varepsilon^2$ .

Let  $s_j$  be the probability that  $\mathsf{test}_j$  happens and  $\mathsf{test}_q$  does not happen for any q < j. Following Claim C.1, we get that  $\sum_{j \in [\ell]} s_j \ge \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  and Claim C.2 says that  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}] \ge 1/\ell \sum_{j \in [\ell]} s_j - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , which when combined gives us

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}] \geq \frac{\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}]}{\ell} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

{75}------------------------------------------------

Next, we make use of the fact that, except with negligible probability,  $\mathcal{B}$  will only trace good if it guesses the correct  $j^*$  such that  $\mathsf{test}_{j^*} = 1$  and  $\mathsf{test}_{j} = 0$  for all  $j < j^*$ . Observe that the respective events that  $\mathsf{test}_{j}$  happens are independent of  $\mathcal{B}$ 's choice of  $j^*$ . Hence, given that  $\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1$  for at least one j,  $\mathcal{B}$  will play against that instance with probability  $1/\ell$ . If  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses incorrectly, then  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs a dummy decoder. In this case,  $\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}$  must not happen because  $P(\mathsf{Dummy}) = 1/2$  and  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  can happen only with negligible probability, since otherwise a trivial adversary that corrupts no parties and outputs the dummy decoder could frame honest parties with non-negligible probability in  $\Pi$ . If, however,  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses correctly, then  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  implies  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}$ , because  $\mathcal{B}$  corrupts the same parties as  $\mathcal{A}$  and

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}] \leq \frac{\ell-1}{\ell} \cdot \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) + \frac{\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}]}{\ell}.$$

Putting it all together (and simplifying negligibilities), we get that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] \ge \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

for a negligible function negl.

<span id="page-75-1"></span>**Lemma C.3** (Semantic Security of  $\Pi'$ ). If  $\Pi$  is a semantically secure TT-KEM, then multi-instance expansion of  $\Pi$  across  $\ell \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  instances retains semantic security.

Proof of Lemma C.3. Let  $\Pi$  be a semantically secure TT-KEM, and let  $\Pi'$  be a multi-instance expansion of  $\Pi$  across  $\ell \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  bins. Then, due to the nature of 1-out-of- $\ell$  sharing the KEM key across instances, a simple hybrid argument shows that for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a PPT reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{IND-CPA}}_{\mathcal{A}.\Pi'}(\lambda) \leq \ell \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{IND-CPA}}_{\mathcal{B}.\Pi}(\lambda),$$

which is negligible by assumption.

## <span id="page-75-0"></span>C.2 A Proof of Theorem 8.1

<span id="page-75-2"></span>**Lemma C.4** For all  $u \in K$  where  $\mathsf{test}_u = 1$  it holds that  $P_{u,\Pi'}(D) = P_\Pi(D_u)$ , where D is the decoder that is traced in  $\Pi'$  and  $D_u$  is the decoder for the u-th instance of  $\Pi$  that is constructed by  $\Pi'$ . Trace.

Proof of Lemma C.4. Fix an arbitrary  $u \in [K]$  and let  $(pk := \{pk^{(\ell)}\}_{\ell \in [K]}, sk, tk) \leftarrow \Pi'.KGen(*)$ . Let D be an arbitrary decoder and  $D_u$  be the decoder that is constructed by  $\Pi'.Trace$  for instance u before it is potentially replaced with the dummy decoder. When instantiating the definition of P with  $\Pi'$ , we get that

$$P_{u,\Pi'}(D)$$

$$= \Pr \left[ D(k^b, ((c_1, \dots, c_K), u)) = b \colon \begin{cases} (c_\ell, k_\ell) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE.Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_2^{(\ell)}) \}_{\ell \in [K] \setminus \{u\}} \\ (c_u, k_u) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_1^{(u)}) \\ k^0 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \bigoplus_{\ell=1}^K k_\ell \\ k^1 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}, b \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\} \end{cases} \right]$$

$$= \Pr \left[ D(k, ((c_1, \dots, c_K), u)) = b \colon \begin{cases} (c_\ell, k_\ell) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{TE.Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_2^{(\ell)}) \}_{\ell \in [K] \setminus \{u\}} \\ (c_u, k_u) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_1^{(u)}) \\ k_u^0 \leftarrow k_u, k_u^1 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}, b \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0, 1\} \\ k \leftarrow k_u^b \oplus \bigoplus_{\ell \in [K] \setminus \{u\}} k_\ell \end{cases} \right].$$

The above equality holds. To see this, observe that for b = 0, both experiments are identical. When b = 1, we have  $k_u^b = k_1^1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}$ , and thus the two distributions are are identical:

$$\left(k \leftarrow k_u^b \oplus \bigoplus_{\ell \in [K] \setminus \{u\}} k_\ell\right) \equiv (k \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{K}).$$

{76}------------------------------------------------

Next, let us instantiate  $P_{\Pi}(D_u)$ . We get that

$$\begin{split} &P_{\Pi}(D_{u})\\ &=\Pr\left[D_{u}\left(k_{u}^{b},c_{u}\right)=b\colon \begin{array}{c} (c_{u},k_{u}^{0})\overset{\$}{\leftarrow}\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_{1}^{(u)})\\ k_{j}^{1}\overset{\$}{\leftarrow}\mathcal{K},b\overset{\$}{\leftarrow}\{0,1\} \end{array}\right]\\ &=\Pr\left[D(k,(c_{1},\ldots,c_{u},\ldots,c_{K}))=b\colon \begin{array}{c} \{(c_{\ell},k_{\ell})\overset{\$}{\leftarrow}\mathsf{TE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_{2}^{(\ell)})\}_{\ell\in[K]\backslash\{u\}}\\ (c_{u},k_{u}^{0})\overset{\$}{\leftarrow}\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}_{1}^{(u)})\\ k_{u}^{1}\overset{\$}{\leftarrow}\mathcal{K},b\overset{\$}{\leftarrow}\{0,1\}\\ k\leftarrow k_{u}^{b}\oplus\bigoplus_{\ell\in[K]\backslash\{u\}}k_{\ell} \end{array}\right]\\ &=P_{u,\Pi'}(D), \end{split}$$

where in the second step, we inlined the construction of  $D_u$  with respect to D as defined by  $\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}$ . Trace.

<span id="page-76-0"></span>Claim C.5 If  $D_j$  is  $\varepsilon'$ -good, then  $\mathsf{test}_j = 0$  can happen with at most negligible probability.

Proof of Claim C.5. Suppose  $D_j$  is  $\varepsilon'$ -good. Let the random variable X count the number of successful tests on  $D_j$  and let N denote the number of trials. Further, we can bound the mean  $\mu = N \cdot P_{\Pi}(D_j) \ge N \cdot (1/2 + \varepsilon')$  We are interested in

$$\Pr[\mathsf{test} = 0 | D_i \text{ is } \varepsilon' \text{-good}] = \Pr[X < N \cdot (1/2 + \varepsilon'/2)],$$

and applying the Hoeffding inequality with  $t = N\varepsilon'/2$ , we get that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{test} = 0 | D_j \text{ is } \varepsilon' \text{-good}] \le \Pr[X \le \mu - t] \le \exp\left(-\frac{N\varepsilon'^2}{2}\right),$$

which is negligible for  $N \ge 2\lambda/\varepsilon'^2$ . Since we set  $Q = 8\lambda/\varepsilon'^2 \ge 2\lambda/\varepsilon'^2$  in the construction, the probability is negligible.

Proof of Theorem 8.1. Intuitively, our proof works as follows: We will show that in all bins  $B_j$  such that  $D_j$  is at least  $\varepsilon'/4$ -good, tracing will succeed, and we will find at least  $t_{\text{bin}}$  traitors from each one of these bins due to  $t_{\text{bin}}$ -traceability of  $\Pi_{\text{t-tr}}$ . Then, as this is true for at least  $(1 - \gamma)K$  bins (Lemma 8.2), we get at least  $(1 - \gamma)K \cdot t_{\text{bin}}$  traitors.

To formalize this intuition, we follow proof of Theorem 6.4, with minor modifications to reflect the new construction. We include the details for completeness.

First, we define the following two events for all  $j \in [K]$ :

- GoodTrBin<sub>j</sub>: This event occurs whenever both  $|\mathcal{T}'_j| \ge t_{\text{bin}}$  and  $\mathcal{T}'_j \subseteq \mathcal{T}_j$ .
- BadTrBin<sub>j</sub>: This event occurs whenever  $\mathcal{T}'_i \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_j$ .

Let  $\mathcal{H}_0$  be the  $\tau$ -traceability game (Definition 4.1) instantiated with  $\Pi'$  for  $\tau = (1 - \gamma)Kt_{\text{bin}}$ . For  $u \in [K]$ , let  $\mathcal{H}_u$  be the same game instantiated except with a modified winning condition. In  $\mathcal{H}_u$ , the additional condition triggers if the adversary manages to avoid being traced or to frame an honest party in  $B_j$  for some  $j \leq u$ . More formally, in  $\mathcal{H}_u$ , we set BadTr to 0, whenever BadTrBin<sub>j</sub> = 1 for some  $j \leq u$ , and we set GoodTr to 1, whenever there exists some  $j \leq u$  for which GoodTrBin<sub>j</sub> = 0 and test<sub>j</sub> = 1, where test<sub>j</sub> is the event that the decoder  $D_j$  passes the test described in the tracing procedure.

Now, observe that  $\Pi'$  is naively  $\tau$ -traceable with respect to  $\mathcal{H}_K$ . To show this, we prove that for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  it holds that  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , and  $\Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)}] = 0$ . In particular, let  $\mathcal{A}$  be any PPT adversary. If  $\mathcal{A}$  hits any event  $\mathsf{BadTrBin}_j$  for  $j \in [K]$ , then  $\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)}$  is set to 0 by definition. If, however,  $\mathcal{A}$  avoids triggering this condition, it must cause  $\mathcal{T}_j' \subseteq \mathcal{T}_j$  for all  $j \in [K]$ . As a consequence, the event  $\mathsf{BadTr}$  cannot occur, because

$$\mathcal{T}' = \bigcup_{j=1}^K \mathcal{T}'_j \subseteq \bigcup_{j=1}^K \mathcal{T}_j = \mathcal{T}.$$

{77}------------------------------------------------

A similar argument holds for  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)}$ . Suppose  $\mathcal{A}$  avoids hitting the condition, i.e. for all  $j \in [K]$  with  $\mathsf{test}_j = 1$  it holds that  $\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_j = 1$ . Then,  $\mathcal{T}'_j \subseteq \mathcal{T}_j$  for all  $j \in [K]$ , and because  $|\mathcal{T}'_j| \ge t_{\mathsf{bin}}$  for all  $j \in [K]$  such that  $\mathsf{test}_j = 1$ , it holds that

$$|\mathcal{T}'| = \sum_{j=1}^{K} |\mathcal{T}'_j| \ge \sum_{j \in [K] \text{ s.t. } \mathsf{test}_j = 1} t_{\mathsf{bin}} \ge (1 - \gamma)K \cdot t_{\mathsf{bin}} = \tau,$$

which causes  $\mathsf{GoodTr}^{(K)}$  to be set to 1. The last inequality follows form Lemma 8.2 and Claim C.5. If, however,  $\mathcal{A}$  does trigger the additional condition, then  $\mathsf{GoodTr}^{(K)}$  is set to 1.

Hence, in order to show  $\tau$ -traceability, it remains to show that for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , it holds that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \text{ and } \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] \leq \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)}] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

for a negligible function negl.

Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be any PPT adversary. We use  $\mathcal{A}$  to construct a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  against  $t_{\mathsf{bin}}$ -traceability of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}$  as follows: First,  $\mathcal{B}$  picks an index  $u \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} [K]$  uniformly at random. For all indices  $j \neq u$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  runs the key generation algorithm of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}$  and TE to generate  $\mathsf{pk}_1^{(j)}, \mathsf{pk}_2^{(j)}, \{\mathsf{sk}_{1,i}, \mathsf{sk}_{2,i}\}_{i \in B_j}, \text{ and } \mathsf{tk}_j \text{ according to protocol. } \mathcal{B}$  embeds the challenge public key  $\mathsf{pk}^*$ , as received by  $\mathsf{Game-Tr}$  of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}$ , for instance u, i.e.,  $\mathsf{pk}_1^{(u)} \leftarrow \mathsf{pk}^*$ . The reduction also generates  $\mathsf{pk}_2^{(u)}, \{\mathsf{sk}_{2,i}\}_{i \in B_u}$ . Then,  $\mathcal{B}$  runs  $\mathcal{A}$ , simulating corruption queries for parties in  $B_u$  by making use of its own corruption oracle for  $\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}$ . Secret for other bins, or for TE, can be simulated without oracle queries, as the key pairs were generated by the reduction.

As a result,  $\mathcal{B}$  receives a decoder D. Next,  $\mathcal{B}$  constructs the decoder  $D_u$  as prescribed by the Trace algorithm of  $\Pi'$ . Then,  $\mathcal{B}$  submits  $D_u$  to its challenger. As part of this,  $\mathcal{B}$  runs the test on decoder  $D_u$ . If  $\mathsf{test}_u = 0$ , then  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs a Dummy decoder which always returns 0. Denote with  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$ ,  $\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$ , and  $\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}$  the events  $\mathsf{GoodTr}$ ,  $\mathsf{BadTr}$ , and  $\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}$  when running in the  $t_{\mathsf{bin}}$ -traceability game of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}$ .

Before we bound  $\mathcal{A}$ 's probability to distinguish adjacent hybrids with  $\mathcal{B}$ 's success probability, we first prove the following intuitive claim.

<span id="page-77-0"></span>Claim C.6 For all adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function  $negl(\lambda)$  such that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{j,\mathcal{A}} = 1] \leq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

*Proof.* Suppose that  $\mathsf{test}_j$  happens and u = j. We will show that, in this case,  $\mathcal{B}$  produces a good decoder with overwhelming probability or in other words  $\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{j,\mathcal{A}} \land D_u \text{ is not } \varepsilon'/4\text{-good}] \leq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{j,\mathcal{A}} = 1 \mid D_u \text{ is not } \varepsilon'/4\text{-good}] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Denote with X the random variable counting the successful responses of D in the test. We can apply Lemma C.4 and bound

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1 | P(D_{u}) < \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon'}{4}\right] = \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1 | P_{u,\Pi'}(D) < \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon'}{4}\right]$$

$$\leq \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1 | P_{u,\Pi'}(D) = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon'}{4}\right]$$

$$= \Pr\left[X \geq N \cdot \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon'}{2} | P_{u,\Pi'}(D) = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon'}{4}\right],$$

where N denotes the number of repetitions in the test. Observe that, if  $P(D_u) < 1/2 + \varepsilon'/4$ , then  $\mathbb{E}[X] \leq \mu := N \cdot (1/2 + \varepsilon'/4)$ . Let  $t = N\varepsilon'/4$ . We can apply Hoeffding's inequality and bound

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_j = 1 | P(D_u) < \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\varepsilon'}{4}\right] \le \Pr[X \ge \mu + t] \le \exp\left(-\frac{N\varepsilon'^2}{8}\right),$$

which is negligible for  $N \geq 8\lambda/\varepsilon'^2$ . Since we set  $Q := 8\lambda/\varepsilon'^2$  in the construction, the claim holds.

Equipped with Claim C.6, we next bound  $\mathcal{A}$ 's advantage in distinguishing subsequent hybrids with respect to GoodTr and BadTr.

{78}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-78-0"></span>Claim C.7 For all  $j \in [K]$  there exists a negligible function negl such that

$$\Pr\Big[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j)}\Big] - \Pr\Big[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j-1)}\Big] \leq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

as well as

$$\Pr\!\left[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j-1)}\right] - \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j)}\right] = \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j\right]\!.$$

Proof of Claim C.7. We start with the latter part of the claim. Observe that the only way for  $\mathcal{A}$  to set  $\mathsf{BadTr} = 1$  in  $\mathcal{H}_{j-1}$  but not in  $\mathcal{H}_j$  is to cause  $\mathsf{BadTrBin}_j = 1$ , i.e., causing the tracing procedure to output some  $\mathcal{T}'$  such that  $\mathcal{T}'_j \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_j$ . Denote with  $\mathcal{T}'_{j,\mathcal{A}}$  and  $\mathcal{T}_{j,\mathcal{A}}$  the random variable describing the set of identified traitors in  $B_j$  and the set of real traitors from  $B_j$ , respectively, when  $\mathcal{A}$  runs in the tracing experiment of  $\Pi'$ . Similarly, denote with  $\mathcal{T}'_{j,\mathcal{B}}$  and  $\mathcal{T}_{j,\mathcal{B}}$  the same variables, when  $\mathcal{B}$  runs in the tracing experiment of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{t-tr}}$ . Applying the observation above, we can impose the following bound:

$$\Pr\!\left[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j-1)}\right] - \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j)}\right] = \Pr\!\left[\mathcal{T}_{j,\mathcal{A}}' \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{j,\mathcal{A}}\right].$$

By construction of  $\Pi'$ . Trace and  $\mathcal{B}$ , it must also hold that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j] = \Pr[\mathcal{T}'_{j,\mathcal{B}} \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{j,\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j] = \Pr[\mathcal{T}'_{j,\mathcal{A}} \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{j,\mathcal{A}}],$$

and thus

$$\Pr \Big[ \mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j-1)} \Big] - \Pr \Big[ \mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j)} \Big] = \Pr \big[ \mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j \big].$$

Next, we apply a similar argument to the first part of the claim. Again, the only way that  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j)} = 1$ , but  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j-1)} = 0$  is if  $\mathcal{A}$  has hit the modified condition in  $\mathcal{H}_j$ , i.e.,  $\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_j = 0$  and  $\mathsf{test}_j = 1$ .

$$\begin{split} &\Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j)}\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j-1)}\right] \\ &\leq \Pr\left[\neg\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}} \wedge \mathsf{test}_{j} = 1\right] \\ &= \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1 \wedge \mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}}\right] \\ &= \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1\right] - \left(\Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}}\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}} \wedge \mathsf{test}_{j} = 0\right]\right) \\ &= \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}}\right] + \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}} \wedge \mathsf{test}_{j} = 0\right] \\ &\leq \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j} = 1\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}}\right] + \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}} \mid \mathsf{test}_{j} = 0\right] \\ &\leq \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{j}\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}}\right] + 0 \end{split}$$

where pk and tk are treated as random variables. Hence, there exists a negligible function negl such that

$$\Pr \Big[ \Pi.\mathsf{Trace}^{\mathsf{Dummy}(\cdot)}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{tk},\varepsilon'/4) \neq \emptyset \Big] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Applying additionally Claim C.6 to substitute  $Pr[test_i]$ , and simplifying negligibilities yields the following:

$$\begin{split} &\Pr\!\left[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j)}\right] - \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(j-1)}\right] \\ &\leq \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}|\mathcal{B}.u = j\right] - \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{GoodTrBin}_{j,\mathcal{A}}\right] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \\ &= \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}|\mathcal{B}.u = j\right] - \Pr\!\left[\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}' \subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}} \wedge |\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}| \geq t_{\mathsf{bin}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j\right] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \\ &= \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}|\mathcal{B}.u = j\right] - \Pr\!\left[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{j,\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.u = j\right] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \end{split}$$

We next continue as in the proof of Theorem 6.4 and get:

$$\begin{split} \Pr\Big[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}\Big] &\geq \Pr\Big[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)}\Big] - K \cdot \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \\ &\geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - K \cdot \mathsf{negl}(\lambda), \end{split}$$

and

$$\Pr \Big[ \mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)} \Big] \leq \Pr \Big[ \mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(K)} \Big] + K \cdot \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) = K \cdot \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

{79}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-79-2"></span><span id="page-79-1"></span>C.3 Correctness and Semantic Security of Construction 6

In this section, we prove probabilistic correctness, and semantic security of Construction 6 as stated in Theorem 9.1.

We first recall the parameters of Theorem 9.1. Let  $a \in (0,1)$  and  $c_1, c_2 \in (0,1/2)$ , let  $\delta_1 = 6n^{-a/2}$  and  $\delta_2 = 6\sqrt{n}/(n-\sqrt{n})$ , and thus  $t_1 = (1-\delta_1)\frac{n^a}{2}$  and  $t_2 = (1-\delta_2)\frac{n-n^a}{2}$ . Let

$$\ell = \max\left\{3\lambda n^{1-a}, \ \frac{10n^{1-a}(1-c_1)}{c_1^2}, \ \frac{20n^{1-a}(2-3c_2)}{c_2^2}\right\} \text{ and } t_{\mathsf{parts}} = 0.8\ell.$$

Let  $\Pi$  be a perfect TT-KEM that is 1-traceable against  $t_1-1$  partial insiders and TE be a perfect T-KEM. Let  $\Pi'$  be instantiated as in Construction 6 with the parameters above. Then, the following lemmas apply.

<span id="page-79-3"></span>**Lemma C.8** (Correctness of Construction 6).  $\Pi'$  is probabilistically correct for  $S_n = \{n/2\}$ .

Proof of Lemma C.8. The lemma follows directly from the first part of Lemma 9.2.

<span id="page-79-4"></span>**Lemma C.9** (Semantic Security of Construction 6). Construction 6 is semantically secure for  $t = t_1 + t_2 = n/2 - o(n)$ .

Proof Sketch of Lemma C.9. Suppose an adversary corrupts less than  $t = t_1 + t_2$  parties. As a result, to decrypt in any given partition, it must either decrypt break  $\Pi$  in the first bin while corrupting less than  $t_1$  parties, or TE in the second bin, while corrupting less than  $t_2$  parties. Then, even disregarding the fact that the adversary must achieve the above in at least  $t_{parts}$  out of the  $\ell$  partitions, we can loosely bound any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ 's advantage against semantic security of  $\Pi'$  by

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{IND\text{-}CPA}}_{\mathcal{A},\Pi'} \leq 2\ell \left(\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{IND\text{-}CPA}}_{\mathcal{B}_1,\Pi} + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{IND\text{-}CPA}}_{\mathcal{B}_2,\mathsf{TE}}\right)$$

for appropriate PPT reductions  $\mathcal{B}_1$  and  $\mathcal{B}_2$ .

# <span id="page-79-0"></span>D Naive t-Traceable Construction with Large Ciphertext

The construction was outlined in [21], but without a formal security proof. In this section, we provide such a proof.

A naive t-traceable threshold encryption scheme is as follows: each party samples its own key pair  $(\mathsf{sk}_i, \mathsf{pk}_i)$ , and encryption proceeds by t-out-of-n secret-sharing the message m, with the i-th share encrypted under  $\mathsf{pk}_i$ . Then, Traceability follows from PLBE.

As a starting point, we assume that the naive PLBE-scheme is 1-traceable, and make two straightforward observations about its tracing algorithm:

- We can carry out tracing following an arbitrary order of revocation, i.e., we assume that  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$ . Trace takes an additional input Ord, where  $\text{Ord} = (i_1, i_2, \dots, i_n)$  is an ordering of the set of parties [n].
- We obsere that, when instantiated with threshold t, for all decoders D, all public keys  $\mathsf{pk}$ , all  $\varepsilon \in (0,1/2)$ , and all orderings  $\mathsf{Ord}$ , the algorithm  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}.\mathsf{Trace}^D(\mathsf{pk},\varepsilon,\mathsf{Ord})$ , either returns an empty set  $\emptyset$ , or a singleton  $\{p\}$ , where  $p \in \mathsf{Ord}[\leq n-t+1]$ .

In summary, it holds that  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}.\mathsf{Trace}^D(\mathsf{pk},\varepsilon,\mathsf{Ord}) \in \mathsf{Ord}[\leq n-t+1].$ 

We exploit this property to find t traitors by tracing D a total of t times. We start with an arbitrary initial ordering  $\operatorname{Ord}_1$ , and in every step  $i \in [t]$ , we construct  $\operatorname{Ord}_{i+1}$  by swapping the traitor  $p_i$  that was found in step i into the trailing t-1 positions. More formally, we construct  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}^{\mathsf{t-trace}}$  as follows

Construction 11 (Naive t-traceable TT-KEM). Let  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}$  be the naive PLBE scheme. Then  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}^{\mathsf{t-trace}}$  is identical to  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}$  except for the tracing algorithm.

 $\Pi_{\text{naive}}^{\text{t-trace}}.\mathsf{Trace}^D(\mathsf{pk},\varepsilon)$ : First, test the decoder D as test  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  TestDec( $\mathsf{pk},\ldots,Q\coloneqq 8\lambda/\varepsilon^2,\varepsilon/2$ ). If test =0, output an empty set. Otherwise, let  $\mathsf{Ord}_1=(1,2,\ldots,n)$ . Initialize  $\mathcal{T}'\leftarrow\emptyset$ , and run  $i\in[t]$  steps:

• Compute  $\mathcal{T}'_i \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}.\mathsf{Trace}^D(\mathsf{pk},\varepsilon/4,\mathsf{Ord}_i).$  If  $\mathcal{T}'_i = \emptyset$ , return  $\mathcal{T}'$ .

{80}------------------------------------------------

- <span id="page-80-0"></span>• Else, it must hold that  $\mathcal{T}'_i = \{p_i\}$  for some  $p_i \in \mathsf{Ord}_i [\leq n - t + 1]$ . Let  $\mathsf{pos}_i$  be  $p_i$ 's position in  $\mathsf{Ord}_i$ , i.e.,  $p_i = \mathsf{Ord}_i[\mathsf{pos}_i]$ .
- Initialize  $\mathsf{Ord}_{i+1} \leftarrow \mathsf{Ord}_i$ , and then swap  $p_i$  with the party at position n-i+1

$$\operatorname{Ord}_{i+1}[\operatorname{pos}_i] \leftarrow \operatorname{Ord}_i[n-i+1] \text{ and } \operatorname{Ord}_{i+1}[n-i+1] \leftarrow p_i.$$

• Update  $\mathcal{T}' \leftarrow \mathcal{T}' \cup \mathcal{T}'_i$ .

Finally, after executing t steps, output  $\mathcal{T}'$ .

**Theorem D.1** There exists a t-traceable threshold encryption scheme with threshold t whose ciphertext and public key sizes are O(n), and whose secret share size is O(1).

*Proof.* The theorem follows by instantiating  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}^{\text{t-trace}}$  with  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$ , which, as discussed in [21], satisfies the requirement that the revocation order is not fixed in advance. We then show that the tracing algorithm described above identifies t traitors. This follows from the argument that each step  $i \in [t]$ , the tracing procedure outputs a distinct traitor. The proof is a simplified version of the argument given in Section 9; we include it here for completeness.

Let  $\mathsf{Cut} = n - t + 1$ , and recall the invariant that  $\mathcal{T}_i' \subseteq \mathsf{Ord}_i [\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$ . We show that in each step  $i \in [t]$ , a traitor from  $\mathsf{Ord}_i [\leq \mathsf{Cut}]$  is found, and that honest parties cannot be framed in any of the steps. To formalize this intuition, we first define what it means that tracing  $\mathit{succeeds}$  in a step i. Denote for steps  $i \in [t]$  with  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_i$  the following event:

$$\mathsf{GoodStep}_i = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } |\mathcal{T}_i'| > 0 \text{ and } \mathcal{T}_i' \subseteq \mathcal{T} \\ 0 & \text{otherwise.} \end{cases}$$

Denote with GoodSteps the event that  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_i = 1$  for all  $i \in [t]$ . Also, denote with  $\mathsf{BadStep}_i$  the event that any non-traitor is framed as traitor in step i, and with  $\mathsf{BadSteps}$  the event that there exists an  $i \in [t]$  such that  $\mathsf{BadStep}_i = 1$ .

Reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  to 1-traceability of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}$ . Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be an adversary against the t-traceability of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}^{\mathsf{t-trace}}$ . We construct a reduction  $\mathcal{B}$  that initially samples a random guess for a step  $i^* \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} [t]$ . The reduction runs against 1-traceability of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}$  and simulates t-traceability of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{naive}}^{\mathsf{t-trace}}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$  by embedding the challenge public key. When  $\mathcal{A}$  issues a corruption query for party p, then  $\mathcal{B}$  queries its own corruption oracle on p to learn  $\mathsf{sk}_p$  and forwards the result to  $\mathcal{A}$ . In particular, this means that  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}} = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$ .

When  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs a decoder D,  $\mathcal{B}$  runs the tracing procedure up to step  $i^*$ . This is possible since the tracing is public (i.e., no tracing key is required). Thus,  $\mathcal{B}$  simulates  $\operatorname{Ord}_{i^*}$ , and returns the tuple  $(D, \operatorname{Ord}_{i^*})^{15}$  to its challenger.

Observe that  $\mathcal{A}$ 's probability of triggering BadSteps is independent of  $\mathcal{B}$ 's choice of  $i^*$ , and thus whenever  $\mathcal{A}$  triggers BadSteps, there must exist one  $i \in [t]$  such that tracing in the i-th step outputs at least one non-traitor ( $\mathcal{T}_i' \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}$ ). Hence,  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses i correctly with probability at least 1/t. Denote by  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}'(i^*)$  the traitors found in step  $i^*$  from  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}$ . If  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses correctly, then  $\mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}' = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}}'(i^*) \not\subseteq \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{A}} = \mathcal{T}_{\mathcal{B}}$ , and thus  $\Pr[\mathsf{BadSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq t \cdot \Pr[\mathsf{BadTr}_{\mathcal{B}}]$ .

**Proving**  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Next, we show that for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  it holds that  $\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . We define a series of hybrids  $\mathcal{H}_i$  for  $i \in [0, t]$  such that in  $\mathcal{H}_0$ , all events are defined as is, i.e.,  $\mathcal{H}_0$  is identical to the real game. In  $\mathcal{H}_i$ , however, we modify the definition of events as follows: If for any  $u \leq i$  it holds that  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_u = 0$ , then we overwrite  $\mathsf{GoodSteps} \leftarrow \mathsf{test}$ , i.e., if the adversary manages that in any step  $u \leq i$  either no traitors is found or non-traitors are framed, then we just set  $\mathsf{GoodSteps}$  to 1, iff the decoder D tests good.

Denote with  $GoodSteps^{(i)}$  the random variable representing the event GoodSteps in hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_i$ . First, observe that in the last hybrid  $\mathcal{H}_t$  it trivially holds that for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$  there exists a negligible function negl such that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(t)}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

<span id="page-80-1"></span>Recall that we use the observation here, that in  $\Pi_{\text{naive}}$ , the revocation ordering can be adversarially chosen at the time of tracing

{81}------------------------------------------------

since if a decoder is good, it will only test bad with negligible probability.<sup>16</sup> It remains to show that for all PPT adversaries there exists a negligible function negl such that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] \ge \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(t_{\mathsf{parts}})}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \tag{5}$$

To prove this, we argue that for all subsequent hybrids, it holds that

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)}\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)}\right] \leq \Pr\left[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}\right] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

To see this, observe that the only way for  $\mathcal{A}$  to cause  $\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)} = 1$ , but  $\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)} = 0$  is to set  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_i = 0$ , and  $\mathsf{test} = 1$ , and thus we obtain the following bound.

$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)}] \\ &\leq \Pr[\neg \mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \wedge \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] = \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \wedge \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] \\ &\leq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}} \mid \mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}} = 0] \\ &\leq \Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}] + 0 \end{split}$$

Next, we need to show that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i..\mathcal{A}}] \le \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i]) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda), \tag{6}$$

This follows from the following two arguments. First, if  $\mathcal{B}$  picks  $i^* = i$ , then  $\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}$  implies  $\mathsf{GoodStep}_{i,\mathcal{A}}$ . Second, note that if  $\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}$  happens, then D must be at least  $\varepsilon/4$  good decoder except with negligible probability. Hence, since  $\mathcal{B}$  returns the same decoder D, then it must hold that  $\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}$  happens except with negligible probability, and thus

$$\Pr[\mathsf{test}_{\mathcal{A}}] \leq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Therefore,

$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(t)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] \\ &= \sum_{i=1}^{t} \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i)}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(i-1)}] \right) \\ &\leq \sum_{i=1}^{t} \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \mid \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] \right) + \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda) \\ &= \sum_{i=1}^{t} \frac{\Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \wedge \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \wedge \mathcal{B}.i^* = i]}{\Pr[\mathcal{B}.i^* = i]} + t \cdot \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda) \\ &= t \cdot \sum_{i=1}^{t} \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}} \wedge \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}} \wedge \mathcal{B}.i^* = i] \right) + t \cdot \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda) \\ &= t \cdot \left( \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{B}}] - \Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{B}}] \right) + t \cdot \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda) \\ &\leq t \cdot \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) + t \cdot \mathsf{negl'}(\lambda). \end{split}$$

The last inequality follows from the 1-traceability of  $\Pi_{\sf naive}$ . Hence,

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] = \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(0)}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}_{\mathcal{A}}^{(t)}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}'(\lambda).$$

Taking into account our invariant that  $\mathcal{T}'_i \subseteq \mathsf{Ord}_i$ , we can conclude that, if  $\mathsf{GoodSteps}$  happens, i.e., in every step, one unique traitor is found, then we must have found t distinct real traitors. Thus, we get that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{GoodTr}_{\mathcal{A}}] = \Pr[\mathsf{GoodSteps}] \geq \Pr[\mathsf{GoodDec}_{\mathcal{A}}] - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

<span id="page-81-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>16</sup>This follows a Hoeffding inequality identically to that of Claim 6.6.

{82}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-82-1"></span><span id="page-82-0"></span>E Deferred Proof of Assumption 1

We prove that Assumption 1 holds in the generic group model, making use of the master theorem.

<span id="page-82-3"></span>**Theorem E.1 (Master Theorem [6, 22]).** Let  $p \in \mathbb{N}$  be a prime, let  $n, v_1, v_2, v_t \in \mathbb{N}$ , let  $\mathbf{L}_1 \in \mathbb{F}_p[X_1, \dots, X_n]^{v_1}$ ,  $\mathbf{L}_2 \in \mathbb{F}_p[X_1, \dots, X_n]^{v_2}$ ,  $\mathbf{L}_T \in \mathbb{F}_p[X_1, \dots, X_n]^{v_t}$ , and  $f \in \mathbb{F}_p[X_1, \dots, X_n]$  be tuples of n-variate polynomials of maximum degree  $d_{\mathbf{L}_1}, d_{\mathbf{L}_2}, d_{\mathbf{L}_T}$ , and  $d_f$  respectively. Denote  $v = v_1 + v_2 + v_t$  and  $d = \max(d_{\mathbf{L}_1} + d_{\mathbf{L}_2}, d_{\mathbf{L}_T}, d_f)$ . If f is independent of  $(\mathbf{L}_1, \mathbf{L}_2, \mathbf{L}_T)$ , then for any generic adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  that makes at most q group oracle queries:

$$\left|\Pr\left[\mathcal{A}\binom{p,\mathsf{h}_1[\mathbf{L}_1(x)],\mathsf{h}_2[\mathbf{L}_2(x)],}{\mathsf{h}_T[\mathbf{L}_T(x),f(x)]}\right) = 1\right] - \Pr\left[\mathcal{A}\binom{p,\mathsf{h}_1[\mathbf{L}_1(x)],\mathsf{h}_2[\mathbf{L}_2(x)],}{\mathsf{h}_T[\mathbf{L}_T(x),r]}\right) = 1\right] \right| \leq \frac{(q+v+2)^2 \cdot d}{2p},$$

where  $h_1, h_2$ , and  $h_T$  denote the corresponding handles, and the probabilities are taken over the choice of  $x \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p^n$  and  $r \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}_p$ .

**Definition E.2** (Independence). Let the completion  $\mathbf{C}(\mathbf{L}) = {\mathbf{L}_1 \otimes \mathbf{L}_2} \cup \mathbf{L}_T$  of size D for lists  $(\mathbf{L}_1, \mathbf{L}_2, \mathbf{L}_T)$  be of the form

$$\mathbf{C}(\mathbf{L}) = \{g_1(X_1, \dots, X_n), \dots g_D(X_1, \dots, X_n)\}.$$

We say that an element f is dependent on lists  $(\mathbf{L}_1, \mathbf{L}_2, \mathbf{L}_T)$ , if there exist coefficients  $\kappa_i \in \mathbb{F}_p$  such that

$$f(X_1, \dots, X_n) = \sum_{i=1}^{D} \kappa_i \cdot g_i(X_1, \dots, X_n).$$

Otherwise, we say that f is independent of  $(\mathbf{L}_1, \mathbf{L}_2, \mathbf{L}_T)$ .

<span id="page-82-2"></span>**Lemma E.3** The interactive t-augmented bilinear computational Diffie-Hellman assumption holds in the generic group model.

Proof. We prove Lemma E.3 by showing an upper bound on the advantage against the decisional version of the t-ABCDH problem in the GGM using the master theorem (Theorem E.1), which trivially implies the same upper bound on the advantage against the computational version. Fix an arbitrary  $t \in \mathbb{N}$  and let  $\mathcal{A}$  be any PPT adversary playing the Int-ABCDH game against the challenger in the GGM. We denote with  $H_0, H_1, \{A_k\}_{k \in [0,t-1]}, Y, Z, R, V$  the formal variables corresponding to the field elements  $h_0, h_1, \{\alpha_k\}_{k \in [0,t-1]}, y, z, r, v$  that are sampled by the challenger and hidden from the adversary. Instead of receiving group elements directly, the adversary receives corresponding handles and can perform generic operations on the handles using an addition and a pairing oracle. W.l.o.g., assume that the adversary, after receiving handles to view<sub>1</sub> and view<sub>2</sub> as well as the group elements in E, outputs a set  $\mathcal{I} \subseteq \mathbb{N}$  with  $|\mathcal{I}| < t$  and  $0 \notin \mathcal{I}$ . As a response, the adversary receives a set of handles to elements in view<sub>T</sub>. Denote the set  $S = \{0, 1, \ldots, t-1\}$ . As a result, we can express the adversary's view as a list of polynomials in our formal variables as follows:

$$\mathbf{L}_{1} = \left(1, H_{0}, H_{1}, Y, Z, \{A_{k}\}_{k \in S}, \{H_{b}YA_{k}\}_{b \in \{0,1\}, k \in S}, \{H_{1}ZA_{k}\}_{k \in S}, A_{0}YZ, YR + H_{0}V\right)$$

$$\mathbf{L}_{2} = \left(1, V, \{YA_{k}\}_{k \in S}, \{ZA_{k}\}_{k \in S}\right)$$

$$\mathbf{L}_{T} = \left(\left\{YZR\sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_{k}^{(i)} A_{k}\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}}\right)$$

with the challenge

$$f = A_0 Y Z R$$
.

To apply the master theorem, we need to show that f is independent of the completion of the above list.

<span id="page-82-4"></span>Claim E.4 It holds that f is independent of the completion  $\mathbf{C} = \mathbf{C}(\mathbf{L}_1, \mathbf{L}_2, \mathbf{L}_T) = {\mathbf{L}_1 \otimes \mathbf{L}_2} \cup \mathbf{L}_T$ .

{83}------------------------------------------------

*Proof of Claim.* Let us first list the completion, omitting redundant terms.

$$\mathbf{C}(\mathbf{L}) = \left\{ 1, H_0, H_1, Y, Z, V, \{A_k\}_{k \in S}, \{H_b Y A_k\}_{b \in \{0,1\}, k \in S}, \{H_1 Z A_k\}_{k \in S}, A_0 Y Z, Y R + H_0 V, \{Y A_k\}_{k \in S}, \{Z A_k\}_{k \in S}, \left\{Y Z R \sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_k^{(i)} A_k\right\}_{i \in \mathcal{I}}, A_0 Y Z V, \{A_0 Y^2 Z A_k\}_{k \in S}, \{A_0 Y Z^2 A_k\}_{k \in S}, \{H_0 Z A_k\}_{k \in S}, H_0 V, H_1 V, \{Y A_k A_\ell\}_{k,\ell \in S}, \{Z A_k A_\ell\}_{k,\ell \in S}, \{A_k V\}_{k \in S}, \{H_0 Y^2 A_k A_\ell\}_{k,\ell \in S}, \{H_0 Y A_k V\}_{k \in S}, \{H_0 Y A_k V\}_{k \in S}, \{H_1 Y^2 A_k A_\ell\}_{k,\ell \in S}, \{H_1 Y A_k V\}_{k \in S}, \{H_1 Y A_k V\}_{k \in S}, \{H_1 Z A_k A_\ell\}_{k,\ell \in S}, \{H_1 Z A_k V\}_{k \in S}, \{Y Z A_k\}_{k \in S}, Y V, \{Z^2 A_k\}_{k \in S}, Z V, Y R V + H_0 V^2, \{Y^2 R A_k + H_0 Y V A_k\}_{k \in S}, \{A_k Y Z R + A_k H_0 Z V\}_{k \in S} \right\}$$

To prove independence, we have to show that f cannot be represented as a linear combination of elements in the completion. First, observe that the only elements in the completion that even contain  $A_0YZR$  as a summand are

$$A_0YZR + A_0H_0ZV$$
 as well as  $\left\{YZR\sum_{k=0}^{t-1}\lambda_k^{(i)}A_k\right\}_{i\in\mathcal{I}}$ .

We note that  $A_0YZR + A_0H_0ZV$  cannot be symbolically useful in a linear combination for f, because there is no way to cancel out the monomial  $A_0H_0ZV$ , which does not appear elsewhere in the completion. Further, as there are no other elements in the completion that contain terms of  $A_kYZR$  for any  $k \in S$ , none of the remaining elements can contribute to a linear combination of f in a non-trivial manner. As a result, we argue that the claim of independence is equivalent to whether there exists a linear combination  $\beta_1, \ldots, \beta_{t-1} \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  such that

$$A_0 Y Z R = \sum_{i=1}^{t-1} \beta_i Y Z R \cdot \left( \sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_k^{(p_i)} A_k \right),$$

where  $\{p_i|i\in[t-1]\}=\mathcal{I}$ . Observe that we can divide YZR on both sides such that

$$A_0 = \sum_{i=1}^{t-1} \beta_i \sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_k^{(p_i)} A_k.$$

We can factor out  $A_k$  and rearrange the equation to

$$A_0 = \left( A_0 \cdot \sum_{i=1}^{t-1} \beta_i \lambda_0^{(p_i)} \right) + \sum_{k=1}^{t-1} A_k \cdot \sum_{i=1}^{t-1} \beta_i \lambda_k^{(p_i)}.$$

Note that this is equivalent to the following equation system

$$\underbrace{\begin{pmatrix} \lambda_0^{(p_1)} & \lambda_0^{(p_2)} & \cdots & \lambda_0^{(p_{t-1})} \\ \lambda_1^{(p_1)} & \lambda_1^{(p_2)} & \cdots & \lambda_1^{(p_{t-1})} \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\ \lambda_{t-1}^{(p_1)} & \lambda_{t-1}^{(p_2)} & \cdots & \lambda_{t-1}^{(p_{t-1})} \end{pmatrix}}_{M} \cdot \underbrace{\begin{pmatrix} \beta_1 \\ \beta_2 \\ \vdots \\ \beta_{t-1} \end{pmatrix}}_{\beta} = \underbrace{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 0 \\ \vdots \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}}_{\mathbf{b}},$$

where  $M \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{t \times t - 1}$ , and  $\lambda_i^{(p)}$  is the lagrange coefficient for index i to interpolate among set S at position p. Assume towards contradiction that the above equation system  $M\beta = \mathbf{b}$  has a solution. We derive a contradiction by constructing a row vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^t$  such that

$$\mathbf{v} \cdot M = \mathbf{0} \text{ but } \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{b} \neq 0.$$

{84}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-84-1"></span>First, we define a polynomial

$$P(X) = \prod_{i=1}^{t-1} (X - p_i),$$

which is the unique polynomial of degree *t* − 1 that has roots at all positions *p<sup>i</sup>* ∈ I. Observe that, since 0 ̸∈ I, it holds that *P*(0) ̸= 0. We set

$$\mathbf{v} = (P(0), P(1), \dots, P(t-1)).$$

Then, for each column *j* of **v** · *M* we get that

$$\sum_{k=0}^{t-1} \lambda_k^{(p_j)} P(k) = P(p_j) = 0$$

and thus **v** · *M* = **0**. On the other hand, observe that **v** · **b** = *P*(0) ̸= 0, because 0 ̸∈ I and thus *f* must be independent of **C**.

Following Claim [E.4](#page-82-4) we can apply the master theorem (Theorem [E.1\)](#page-82-3) to bound A's advantage in winning the Int-ABCDH experiment in the generic group model by

$$\frac{(q+v+2)^2 \cdot d}{2p},$$

where *q* ∈ poly(*λ*) is the number of group operation oracle queries made by A, *v* = 8 + 7 · *t* is the number of handles in the adversary's view, *d* = 5 is the maximum degree of the elements in the completion **C**, and *p* is a *λ* bit prime. Since *t* ∈ poly(*λ*), we conclude that A's advantage to break the *t*-ABCDH assumption is negligible in the GGM.

## <span id="page-84-0"></span>**F Full-Insider Attacks in Existing Schemes**

In this section, we explain why existing constructions are inherently vulnerable to full-insider attacks. This observation supports our view that achieving full-insider resilience for existing schemes seems out of reach with current techniques without resorting to multi-traitor tracing.

**PLBE-based Constructions [\[21,](#page-64-3) [24,](#page-64-4) [28\]](#page-65-5).** These constructions rely on an algorithm TrEnc(*i*) that, on input an index *i*, produces a ciphertext that can be decrypted only using the secret keys of parties in [*i* + 1*, . . . , n*]; equivalently, the first *i* parties are revoked. Traceability is achieved via an *index-hiding* property, which requires that TrEnc(*i*) and TrEnc(*i* − 1) are computationally indistinguishable for any adversary that does not possess sk*<sup>i</sup>* .

This guarantee, however, fails in the presence of a full insider. When a decoder is sold to a full insider, the insider must attach its secret key sk*<sup>i</sup>* to each query issued to the decoder. During tracing, this allows the decoder to distinguish ciphertexts generated by TrEnc(*i* − 1) from those generated by TrEnc(*i*). Consequently, the decoder can be designed such that the tracing algorithm deterministically identifies the full insider.

A natural attempt to mitigate this issue is to avoid fixing a revocation order in the setup, ensuring that the full insider is never revoked during tracing. However, it is unclear how to realize such a tracing procedure while preserving *succinct* ciphertexts. Moreover, being able to trace in an arbitrary revocation order seems already sufficient to identify at least *t* traitors (see [\[21\]](#page-64-3)), suggesting that, barring any new insights, full-insider resilience may be equivalent to multi-traitor tracing, at least for PLBE-based constructions.

**Fingerprinting-based Constructions [\[12,](#page-63-4) [3,](#page-63-5) [23\]](#page-64-5).** In these constructions, each party *i* is assigned a private codeword *w<sup>i</sup>* drawn from an underlying fingerprinting code. By interacting with the decoder, the tracer can extract a codeword that is feasible with respect to the codewords of the colluding parties. The tracing algorithm of the fingerprinting code is then applied to the extracted codeword to identify one of the colluders responsible for constructing the decoder.

{85}------------------------------------------------

In the presence of a full insider, this approach breaks down. Since the full insider is required to input its secret key, and thus private codeword, the decoder can be designed such that the extracted codeword is always feasible for the insider. As a result, the tracing algorithm always outputs the full insider, irrespective of the actual colluders.
